<BOS>Key Points Adult acute lymphoblastic leukemia (ALL) is a type of cancer in which the bone marrow makes too many lymphocytes (a type of white blood cell).<EOS>
<BOS>Leukemia may affect red blood cells, white blood cells, and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>Previous chemotherapy and exposure to radiation may increase the risk of developing ALL.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of adult ALL include fever, feeling tired, and easy bruising or bleeding.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the blood and bone marrow are used to detect (find) and diagnose adult ALL.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>Adult acute lymphoblastic leukemia (ALL) is a type of cancer in which the bone marrow makes too many lymphocytes (a type of white blood cell).<EOS>
<BOS>Adult acute lymphoblastic leukemia (ALL; also called acute lymphocytic leukemia) is a cancer of the blood and bone marrow.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of cancer usually gets worse quickly if it is not treated.<EOS>
<BOS>Leukemia may affect red blood cells, white blood cells, and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>Normally, the bone marrow makes blood stem cells (immature cells) that become mature blood cells over time.<EOS>
<BOS>A blood stem cell may become a myeloid stem cell or a lymphoid stem cell.<EOS>
<BOS>A myeloid stem cell becomes one of three types of mature blood cells: Red blood cells that carry oxygen and other substances to all tissues of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Platelets that form blood clots to stop bleeding.<EOS>
<BOS>Granulocytes (white blood cells) that fight infection and disease.<EOS>
<BOS>A lymphoid stem cell becomes a lymphoblast cell and then one of three types of lymphocytes (white blood cells): B lymphocytes that make antibodies to help fight infection.<EOS>
<BOS>T lymphocytes that help B lymphocytes make the antibodies that help fight infection.<EOS>
<BOS>Natural killer cells that attack cancer cells and viruses.<EOS>
<BOS>In ALL, too many stem cells become lymphoblasts, B lymphocytes, or T lymphocytes.<EOS>
<BOS>These cells are also called leukemia cells.<EOS>
<BOS>These leukemia cells are not able to fight infection very well.<EOS>
<BOS>Also, as the number of leukemia cells increases in the blood and bone marrow, there is less room for healthy white blood cells, red blood cells, and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>This may cause infection, anemia, and easy bleeding.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer can also spread to the central nervous system (brain and spinal cord).<EOS>
<BOS>This summary is about adult acute lymphoblastic leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>See the following PDQ summaries for information about other types of leukemia: Childhood Acute Lymphoblastic Leukemia Treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Adult Acute Myeloid Leukemia Treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Childhood Acute Myeloid Leukemia/Other Myeloid Malignancies Treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Chronic Lymphocytic Leukemia Treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia Treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Hairy Cell Leukemia Treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the blood and bone marrow are used to detect (find) and diagnose adult ALL.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used: Physical exam and history : An exam of the body to check general signs of health, including checking for signs of disease, such as infection or anything else that seems unusual.<EOS>
<BOS>A history of the patient's health habits and past illnesses and treatments will also be taken.<EOS>
<BOS>Complete blood count (CBC) with differential : A procedure in which a sample of blood is drawn and checked for the following: The number of red blood cells and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>The number and type of white blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The amount of hemoglobin (the protein that carries oxygen) in the red blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The portion of the blood sample made up of red blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood chemistry studies : A procedure in which a blood sample is checked to measure the amounts of certain substances released into the blood by organs and tissues in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>An unusual (higher or lower than normal) amount of a substance can be a sign of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Peripheral blood smear : A procedure in which a sample of blood is checked for blast cells, the number and kinds of white blood cells, the number of platelets, and changes in the shape of blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Bone marrow aspiration and biopsy : The removal of bone marrow, blood, and a small piece of bone by inserting a hollow needle into the hipbone or breastbone.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the bone marrow, blood, and bone under a microscope to look for abnormal cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests may be done on the samples of blood or bone marrow tissue that are removed: Cytogenetic analysis: A laboratory test in which the cells in a sample of blood or bone marrow are looked at under a microscope to find out if there are certain changes in the chromosomes of lymphocytes.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, in Philadelphia chromosome positive ALL, part of one chromosome switches places with part of another chromosome.<EOS>
<BOS>This is called the Philadelphia chromosome.<EOS>
<BOS>Immunophenotyping : A process used to identify cells, based on the types of antigens or markers on the surface of the cell.<EOS>
<BOS>This process is used to diagnose the subtype of ALL by comparing the cancer cells to normal cells of the immune system.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, a cytochemistry study may test the cells in a sample of tissue using chemicals (dyes) to look for certain changes in the sample.<EOS>
<BOS>A chemical may cause a color change in one type of leukemia cell but not in another type of leukemia cell.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Adult acute myeloid leukemia (AML) is a type of cancer in which the bone marrow makes abnormal myeloblasts (a type of white blood cell), red blood cells, or platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>Leukemia may affect red blood cells, white blood cells, and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different subtypes of AML.<EOS>
<BOS>Smoking, previous chemotherapy treatment, and exposure to radiation may affect the risk of adult AML.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of adult AML include fever, feeling tired, and easy bruising or bleeding.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the blood and bone marrow are used to detect (find) and diagnose adult AML.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>Adult acute myeloid leukemia (AML) is a type of cancer in which the bone marrow makes abnormal myeloblasts (a type of white blood cell), red blood cells, or platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>Adult acute myeloid leukemia (AML) is a cancer of the blood and bone marrow.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of cancer usually gets worse quickly if it is not treated.<EOS>
<BOS>It is the most common type of acute leukemia in adults.<EOS>
<BOS>AML is also called acute myelogenous leukemia, acute myeloblastic leukemia, acute granulocytic leukemia, and acute nonlymphocytic leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>Leukemia may affect red blood cells, white blood cells, and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>Normally, the bone marrow makes blood stem cells (immature cells) that become mature blood cells over time.<EOS>
<BOS>A blood stem cell may become a myeloid stem cell or a lymphoid stem cell.<EOS>
<BOS>A lymphoid stem cell becomes a white blood cell.<EOS>
<BOS>A myeloid stem cell becomes one of three types of mature blood cells: Red blood cells that carry oxygen and other substances to all tissues of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>White blood cells that fight infection and disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Platelets that form blood clots to stop bleeding.<EOS>
<BOS>In AML, the myeloid stem cells usually become a type of immature white blood cell called myeloblasts (or myeloid blasts).<EOS>
<BOS>The myeloblasts in AML are abnormal and do not become healthy white blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Sometimes in AML, too many stem cells become abnormal red blood cells or platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>These abnormal white blood cells, red blood cells, or platelets are also called leukemia cells or blasts.<EOS>
<BOS>Leukemia cells can build up in the bone marrow and blood so there is less room for healthy white blood cells, red blood cells, and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>When this happens, infection, anemia, or easy bleeding may occur.<EOS>
<BOS>The leukemia cells can spread outside the blood to other parts of the body, including the central nervous system (brain and spinal cord), skin, and gums.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary is about adult AML.<EOS>
<BOS>See the following PDQ summaries for information about other types of leukemia: Childhood Acute Myeloid Leukemia/Other Myeloid Malignancies Treatment Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia Treatment Adult Acute Lymphoblastic Leukemia Treatment Childhood Acute Lymphoblastic Leukemia Treatment Chronic Lymphocytic Leukemia Treatment Hairy Cell Leukemia Treatment There are different subtypes of AML.<EOS>
<BOS>Most AML subtypes are based on how mature (developed) the cancer cells are at the time of diagnosis and how different they are from normal cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Acute promyelocytic leukemia (APL) is a subtype of AML that occurs when parts of two genes stick together.<EOS>
<BOS>APL usually occurs in middleaged adults.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs of APL may include both bleeding and forming blood clots.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of chronic lymphocytic leukemia include swollen lymph nodes and tiredness.<EOS>
<BOS>Usually CLL does not cause any signs or symptoms and is found during a routine blood test.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms may be caused by CLL or by other conditions.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your doctor if you have any of the following: Painless swelling of the lymph nodes in the neck, underarm, stomach, or groin.<EOS>
<BOS>Feeling very tired.<EOS>
<BOS>Pain or fullness below the ribs.<EOS>
<BOS>Fever and infection.<EOS>
<BOS>Weight loss for no known reason.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points There are different types of treatment for patients with chronic myelogenous leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>Six types of standard treatment are used: Targeted therapy Chemotherapy Biologic therapy Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant Donor lymphocyte infusion (DLI) Surgery New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of treatment for patients with chronic myelogenous leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>Different types of treatment are available for patients with chronic myelogenous leukemia (CML).<EOS>
<BOS>Some treatments are standard (the currently used treatment), and some are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>A treatment clinical trial is a research study meant to help improve current treatments or obtain information about new treatments for patients with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>When clinical trials show that a new treatment is better than the standard treatment, the new treatment may become the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials are open only to patients who have not started treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Six types of standard treatment are used: Targeted therapy Targeted therapy is a type of treatment that uses drugs or other substances to identify and attack specific cancer cells without harming normal cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Tyrosine kinase inhibitors are targeted therapy drugs used to treat chronic myelogenous leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>Imatinib mesylate, nilotinib, dasatinib, and ponatinib are tyrosine kinase inhibitors that are used to treat CML.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy Chemotherapy is a cancer treatment that uses drugs to stop the growth of cancer cells, either by killing the cells or by stopping them from dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is taken by mouth or injected into a vein or muscle, the drugs enter the bloodstream and can reach cancer cells throughout the body (systemic chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is placed directly into the cerebrospinal fluid, an organ, or a body cavity such as the abdomen, the drugs mainly affect cancer cells in those areas (regional chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>The way the chemotherapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Biologic therapy Biologic therapy is a treatment that uses the patients immune system to fight cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Substances made by the body or made in a laboratory are used to boost, direct, or restore the bodys natural defenses against cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of cancer treatment is also called biotherapy or immunotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant is a method of giving high doses of chemotherapy and replacing bloodforming cells destroyed by the cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Stem cells (immature blood cells) are removed from the blood or bone marrow of the patient or a donor and are frozen and stored.<EOS>
<BOS>After the chemotherapy is completed, the stored stem cells are thawed and given back to the patient through an infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>These reinfused stem cells grow into (and restore) the bodys blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Donor lymphocyte infusion (DLI) Donor lymphocyte infusion (DLI) is a cancer treatment that may be used after stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymphocytes (a type of white blood cell) from the stem cell transplant donor are removed from the donors blood and may be frozen for storage.<EOS>
<BOS>The donors lymphocytes are thawed if they were frozen and then given to the patient through one or more infusions.<EOS>
<BOS>The lymphocytes see the patients cancer cells as not belonging to the body and attack them.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery Splenectomy is surgery to remove the spleen.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests that were done to diagnose the cancer or to find out the stage of the cancer may be repeated.<EOS>
<BOS>Some tests will be repeated in order to see how well the treatment is working.<EOS>
<BOS>Decisions about whether to continue, change, or stop treatment may be based on the results of these tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests will continue to be done from time to time after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of these tests can show if your condition has changed or if the cancer has recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>These tests are sometimes called followup tests or checkups.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options for Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia Chronic Phase Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia Treatment of chronic phase chronic myelogenous leukemia may include the following: Targeted therapy with a tyrosine kinase inhibitor.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy with donor stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Splenectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of lowerdose chemotherapy with donor stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with chronic phase chronic myelogenous leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Accelerated Phase Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia Treatment of accelerated phase chronic myelogenous leukemia may include the following: Donor stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy with a tyrosine kinase inhibitor.<EOS>
<BOS>Tyrosine kinase inhibitor therapy followed by a donor stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Biologic therapy (interferon) with or without chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Transfusion therapy to replace red blood cells, platelets, and sometimes white blood cells, to relieve symptoms and improve quality of life.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with accelerated phase chronic myelogenous leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Blastic Phase Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia Treatment of blastic phase chronic myelogenous leukemia may include the following: Targeted therapy with a tyrosine kinase inhibitor.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy using one or more drugs.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Donor stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy as palliative therapy to relieve symptoms and improve quality of life.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with blastic phase chronic myelogenous leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Relapsed Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia Treatment of relapsed chronic myelogenous leukemia may include the following: Targeted therapy with a tyrosine kinase inhibitor.<EOS>
<BOS>Donor stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Donor lymphocyte infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>Biologic therapy (interferon).<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of new types or higher doses of targeted therapy or donor stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with relapsing chronic myelogenous leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary section describes treatments that are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>It may not mention every new treatment being studied.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Chimeric antigen receptor (CAR) Tcell therapy CAR Tcell therapy is a type of immunotherapy that changes the patient's T cells (a type of immune system cell) so they will attack certain proteins on the surface of cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>T cells are taken from the patient and special receptors are added to their surface in the laboratory.<EOS>
<BOS>The changed cells are called chimeric antigen receptor (CAR) T cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The CAR T cells are grown in the laboratory and given to the patient by infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>The CAR T cells multiply in the patient's blood and attack cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>CAR Tcell therapy is being studied in the treatment of childhood ALL that has relapsed (come back) a second time.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>

<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
