<BOS>Key Points There are different types of treatment for patients with childhood rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Children with rhabdomyosarcoma should have their treatment planned by a team of health care providers who are experts in treating cancer in children.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment for childhood rhabdomyosarcoma may cause side effects.<EOS>
<BOS>Three types of standard treatment are used: Surgery Radiation therapy Chemotherapy New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant Immunotherapy Targeted therapy Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of treatment for patients with childhood rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Some treatments are standard (the currently used treatment), and some are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>A treatment clinical trial is a research study meant to help improve current treatments or obtain information on new treatments for patients with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>When clinical trials show that a new treatment is better than the standard treatment, the new treatment may become the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Because cancer in children is rare, taking part in a clinical trial should be considered.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials are open only to patients who have not started treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Children with rhabdomyosarcoma should have their treatment planned by a team of health care providers who are experts in treating cancer in children.<EOS>
<BOS>Because rhabdomyosarcoma can form in many different parts of the body, many different kinds of treatments are used.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment will be overseen by a pediatric oncologist, a doctor who specializes in treating children with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The pediatric oncologist works with other health care providers who are experts in treating children with rhabdomyosarcoma and who specialize in certain areas of medicine.<EOS>
<BOS>These may include the following specialists: Pediatrician.<EOS>
<BOS>Pediatric surgeon.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation oncologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Pediatric hematologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Pediatric radiologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Pediatric nurse specialist.<EOS>
<BOS>Geneticist or cancer genetics risk counselor.<EOS>
<BOS>Social worker.<EOS>
<BOS>Rehabilitation specialist.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment for childhood rhabdomyosarcoma may cause side effects.<EOS>
<BOS>For information about side effects that begin during treatment for cancer, see our Side Effects page.<EOS>
<BOS>Side effects from cancer treatment that begin after treatment and continue for months or years are called late effects.<EOS>
<BOS>Late effects of cancer treatment for rhabdomyosarcoma may include: Physical problems.<EOS>
<BOS>Changes in mood, feelings, thinking, learning, or memory.<EOS>
<BOS>Second cancers (new types of cancer).<EOS>
<BOS>Some late effects may be treated or controlled.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to talk with your child's doctors about the effects cancer treatment can have on your child.<EOS>
<BOS>(See the PDQ summary on Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>) Three types of standard treatment are used: Surgery Surgery (removing the cancer in an operation) is used to treat childhood rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>A type of surgery called wide local excision is often done.<EOS>
<BOS>A wide local excision is the removal of tumor and some of the tissue around it, including the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>A second surgery may be needed to remove all the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether surgery is done and the type of surgery done depends on the following: Where in the body the tumor started.<EOS>
<BOS>The effect the surgery will have on the way the child will look.<EOS>
<BOS>The effect the surgery will have on the child's important body functions.<EOS>
<BOS>How the tumor responded to chemotherapy or radiation therapy that may have been given first.<EOS>
<BOS>In most children with rhabdomyosarcoma, it is not possible to remove all of the tumor by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Rhabdomyosarcoma can form in many different places in the body and the surgery will be different for each site.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to treat rhabdomyosarcoma of the eye or genital areas is usually a biopsy.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy, and sometimes radiation therapy, may be given before surgery to shrink large tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Even if the doctor removes all the cancer that can be seen at the time of the surgery, patients will be given chemotherapy after surgery to kill any cancer cells that are left.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy may also be given.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment given after the surgery to lower the risk that the cancer will come back, is called adjuvant therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy Radiation therapy is a cancer treatment that uses highenergy xrays or other types of radiation to kill cancer cells or stop them from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two types of radiation therapy: External radiation therapy uses a machine outside the body to send radiation toward the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain ways of giving radiation therapy can help keep radiation from damaging nearby healthy tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>These types of external radiation therapy include the following: Conformal radiation therapy: Conformal radiation therapy is a type of external radiation therapy that uses a computer to make a 3dimensional (3D) picture of the tumor and shapes the radiation beams to fit the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>This allows a high dose of radiation to reach the tumor and causes less damage to nearby healthy tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Intensitymodulated radiation therapy (IMRT): IMRT is a type of 3dimensional (3D) radiation therapy that uses a computer to make pictures of the size and shape of the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Thin beams of radiation of different intensities (strengths) are aimed at the tumor from many angles.<EOS>
<BOS>Volumetrical modulated arc therapy (VMAT): VMAT is type of 3D radiation therapy that uses a computer to make pictures of the size and shape of the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The radiation machine moves in a circle around the patient once during treatment and sends thin beams of radiation of different intensities (strengths) at the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment with VMAT is delivered faster than treatment with IMRT.<EOS>
<BOS>Stereotactic body radiation therapy: Stereotactic body radiation therapy is a type of external radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Special equipment is used to place the patient in the same position for each radiation treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Once a day for several days, a radiation machine aims a larger than usual dose of radiation directly at the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>By having the patient in the same position for each treatment, there is less damage to nearby healthy tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called stereotactic externalbeam radiation therapy and stereotaxic radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Proton beam radiation therapy: Protonbeam therapy is a type of highenergy, external radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>A radiation therapy machine aims streams of protons (tiny, invisible, positivelycharged particles) at the cancer cells to kill them.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of treatment causes less damage to nearby healthy tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy uses a radioactive substance sealed in needles, seeds, wires, or catheters that are placed directly into or near the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>It is used to treat cancer in areas such as the vagina, vulva, uterus, bladder, prostate, head, or neck.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy is also called brachytherapy, internal radiation, implant radiation, or interstitial radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>The type and amount of radiation therapy and when it is given depends on the age of the child, the type of rhabdomyosarcoma, where in the body the tumor started, how much tumor remained after surgery, and whether there is tumor in the nearby lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>External radiation therapy is usually used to treat childhood rhabdomyosarcoma but in certain cases internal radiation therapy is used.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy Chemotherapy is a cancer treatment that uses drugs to stop the growth of cancer cells, either by killing the cells or by stopping them from dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is taken by mouth or injected into a vein or muscle, the drugs enter the bloodstream and can reach cancer cells throughout the body (systemic chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is placed directly into the cerebrospinal fluid, an organ, or a body cavity such as the abdomen, the drugs mainly affect cancer cells in those areas (regional chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy may also be given to shrink the tumor before surgery in order to save as much healthy tissue as possible.<EOS>
<BOS>This is called neoadjuvant chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Every child treated for rhabdomyosarcoma should receive systemic chemotherapy to decrease the chance the cancer will recur.<EOS>
<BOS>The type of anticancer drug, dose, and the number of treatments given depends on whether the child has lowrisk, intermediaterisk, or highrisk rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Rhabdomyosarcoma for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests that were done to diagnose the cancer or to find out the stage of the cancer may be repeated.<EOS>
<BOS>Some tests will be repeated in order to see how well the treatment is working.<EOS>
<BOS>Decisions about whether to continue, change, or stop treatment may be based on the results of these tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests will continue to be done from time to time after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of these tests can show if your child's condition has changed or if the cancer has recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>These tests are sometimes called followup tests or checkups.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options for Childhood Rhabdomyosarcoma Previously Untreated Childhood Rhabdomyosarcoma The treatment of childhood rhabdomyosarcoma often includes surgery, radiation therapy, and chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>The order that these treatments are given depends on where in the body the tumor started, the size of the tumor, the type of tumor, and whether the tumor has spread to lymph nodes or other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Option Overview section of this summary for more information about surgery, radiation therapy, and chemotherapy used to treat children with rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Rhabdomyosarcoma of the brain and head and neck For tumors of the brain: Treatment may include surgery to remove the tumor, radiation therapy, and chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the head and neck that are in or near the eye: Treatment may include chemotherapy and radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>If the tumor remains or comes back after treatment with chemotherapy and radiation therapy, surgery to remove the eye and some tissues around the eye may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the head and neck that are near the ear, nose, sinuses, or base of the skull but not in or near the eye: Treatment may include radiation therapy and chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the head and neck that are not in or near the eye and not near the ear, nose, sinuses, or base of the skull: Treatment may include chemotherapy, radiation therapy, and surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the head and neck that cannot be removed by surgery: Treatment may include chemotherapy and radiation therapy including stereotactic body radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the larynx (voice box): Treatment may include chemotherapy and radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove the larynx is usually not done, so that the voice is not harmed.<EOS>
<BOS>Rhabdomyosarcoma of the arms or legs Chemotherapy followed by surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>If the tumor was not completely removed, a second surgery to remove the tumor may be done.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy may also be given.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the hand or foot, radiation therapy and chemotherapy may be given.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor may not be removed because it would affect the function of the hand or foot.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph node dissection (one or more lymph nodes are removed and a sample of tissue is checked under a microscope for signs of cancer).<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors in the arms, lymph nodes near the tumor and in the armpit area are removed.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors in the legs, lymph nodes near the tumor and in the groin area are removed.<EOS>
<BOS>Rhabdomyosarcoma of the chest, abdomen, or pelvis For tumors in the chest or abdomen (including the chest wall or abdominal wall): Surgery (wide local excision) may be done.<EOS>
<BOS>If the tumor is large, chemotherapy and radiation therapy are given to shrink the tumor before surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the pelvis: Surgery (wide local excision) may be done.<EOS>
<BOS>If the tumor is large, chemotherapy is given to shrink the tumor before surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy may be given after surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the diaphragm: A biopsy of the tumor is followed by chemotherapy and radiation therapy to shrink the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery may be done later to remove any remaining cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the gallbladder or bile ducts: A biopsy of the tumor is followed by chemotherapy and radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the muscles or tissues around the anus or between the vulva and the anus or the scrotum and the anus: Surgery is done to remove as much of the tumor as possible and some nearby lymph nodes, followed by chemotherapy and radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Rhabdomyosarcoma of the kidney For tumors of the kidney: Surgery to remove as much of the tumor as possible.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy and radiation therapy may also be given.<EOS>
<BOS>Rhabdomyosarcoma of the bladder and prostate For tumors that are only at the top of the bladder: Surgery (wide local excision) is done.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the prostate or bladder (other than the top of the bladder): Chemotherapy and radiation therapy are given first to shrink the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>If cancer cells remain after chemotherapy and radiation therapy, the tumor is removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery may include removal of the prostate, part of the bladder, or pelvic exenteration without removal of the rectum.<EOS>
<BOS>(This may include removal of the lower colon and bladder.<EOS>
<BOS>In girls, the cervix, vagina, ovaries, and nearby lymph nodes may be removed).<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy is given first to shrink the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove the tumor, but not the bladder or prostate, is done.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal or external radiation therapy may be given after surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Rhabdomyosarcoma of the area near the testicles Surgery to remove the testicle and spermatic cord.<EOS>
<BOS>The lymph nodes in the back of the abdomen may be checked for cancer, especially if the lymph nodes are large or the child is 10 years or older.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy may be given if the tumor cannot be completely removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Rhabdomyosarcoma of the vulva, vagina, uterus, cervix, or ovary For tumors of the vulva and vagina: Treatment may include chemotherapy followed by surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal or external radiation therapy may be given after surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the uterus: Treatment may include chemotherapy with or without radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Sometimes surgery may be needed to remove any remaining cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the cervix: Treatment may include chemotherapy followed by surgery to remove any remaining tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For tumors of the ovary: Treatment may include chemotherapy followed by surgery to remove any remaining tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Metastatic rhabdomyosarcoma Treatment, such as chemotherapy, radiation therapy, or surgery to remove the tumor, is given to the site where the tumor first formed.<EOS>
<BOS>If the cancer has spread to the brain, spinal cord, or lungs, radiation therapy may also be given to the sites where the cancer has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>The following treatment is being studied for metastatic rhabdomyosarcoma: A clinical trial of immunotherapy (vaccine therapy).<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with previously untreated childhood rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your child's doctor about clinical trials that may be right for your child.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Recurrent Childhood Rhabdomyosarcoma Treatment options for recurrent childhood rhabdomyosarcoma are based on many factors, including where in the body the cancer has come back, what type of treatment the child had before, and the needs of the child.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of recurrent rhabdomyosarcoma may include one or more of the following: Surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of highdose chemotherapy followed by stem cell transplant using the patient's own stem cells.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of targeted therapy or immunotherapy (sirolimus, lorvotuzumab, ipilimumab, nivolumab, or pembrolizumab).<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of targeted therapy with a tyrosine kinase inhibitor (MK1775) and chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with recurrent childhood rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your child's doctor about clinical trials that may be right for your child.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary section describes treatments that are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>It may not mention every new treatment being studied.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant is a way of giving high doses of chemotherapy and replacing blood forming cells destroyed by the cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Stem cells (immature blood cells) are removed from the blood or bone marrow of the patient or a donor and are frozen and stored.<EOS>
<BOS>After the chemotherapy is completed, the stored stem cells are thawed and given back to the patient through an infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>These reinfused stem cells grow into (and restore) the body's blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Immunotherapy Immunotherapy is a treatment that uses the patients immune system to fight cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Substances made by the body or made in a laboratory are used to boost, direct, or restore the bodys natural defenses against cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of cancer treatment is also called biologic therapy or biotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of immunotherapy: Immune checkpoint inhibitor therapy uses the body's immune system to kill cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Two types of immune checkpoint inhibitors are being studied in the treatment of childhood rhabdomyosarcoma that has come back after treatment: CTLA4 is a protein on the surface of T cells that helps keep the bodys immune responses in check.<EOS>
<BOS>When CTLA4 attaches to another protein called B7 on a cancer cell, it stops the T cell from killing the cancer cell.<EOS>
<BOS>CTLA4 inhibitors attach to CTLA4 and allow the T cells to kill cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Ipilimumab is a type of CTLA4 inhibitor.<EOS>
<BOS>PD1 is a protein on the surface of T cells that helps keep the bodys immune responses in check.<EOS>
<BOS>When PD1 attaches to another protein called PDL1 on a cancer cell, it stops the T cell from killing the cancer cell.<EOS>
<BOS>PD1 inhibitors attach to PDL1 and allow the T cells to kill cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Nivolumab and pembrolizumab are PD1 inhibitors.<EOS>
<BOS>Vaccine therapy is a type of immunotherapy being studied to treat metastatic rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy Targeted therapy is a type of treatment that uses drugs or other substances to attack cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapies usually cause less harm to normal cells than chemotherapy or radiation do.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of targeted therapy: mTOR inhibitors stop the protein that helps cells divide and survive.<EOS>
<BOS>Sirolimus is a type of mTOR inhibitor therapy being studied in the treatment of recurrent rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Tyrosine kinase inhibitors are smallmolecule drugs that go through the cell membrane and work inside cancer cells to block signals that cancer cells need to grow and divide.<EOS>
<BOS>MK1775 is a tyrosine kinase inhibitor being studied in the treatment of recurrent rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Antibodydrug conjugates are made up of a monoclonal antibody attached to a drug.<EOS>
<BOS>The monoclonal antibody binds to specific proteins or receptors found on certain cells, including cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The drug enters these cells and kills them without harming other cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Lorvotuzumab mertansine is an antibodydrug conjugate being studied in the treatment of recurrent rhabdomyosarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Diagnostic tests are used to detect (find) and diagnose childhood soft tissue sarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used: Physical exam and history : An exam of the body to check general signs of health, including checking for signs of disease, such as lumps or anything else that seems unusual.<EOS>
<BOS>A history of the patients health habits and past illnesses and treatments will also be taken.<EOS>
<BOS>Xrays : An xray is a type of energy beam that can go through the body onto film, making pictures of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>MRI (magnetic resonance imaging): A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas of the body, such as the chest, abdomen, arms, or legs.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, such as the chest or abdomen, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>Ultrasound exam: A procedure in which highenergy sound waves (ultrasound) are bounced off internal tissues or organs and make echoes.<EOS>
<BOS>The echoes form a picture of body tissues called a sonogram.<EOS>
<BOS>The picture can be printed to be looked at later.<EOS>
<BOS>If tests show there may be a soft tissue sarcoma, a biopsy is done.<EOS>
<BOS>One of the following types of biopsies is usually used: Core needle biopsy : The removal of tissue using a wide needle.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure may be guided using ultrasound, CT scan, or MRI.<EOS>
<BOS>Incisional biopsy : The removal of part of a lump or a sample of tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Excisional biopsy : The removal of an entire lump or area of tissue that doesnt look normal.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the tissue under a microscope to look for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>An excisional biopsy may be used to completely remove smaller tumors that are near the surface of the skin.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of biopsy is rarely used because cancer cells may remain after the biopsy.<EOS>
<BOS>If cancer cells remain, the cancer may come back or it may spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>An MRI of the tumor is done before the excisional biopsy.<EOS>
<BOS>This is done to show where the original tumor is and may be used to guide future surgery or radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>The placement of needles or incisions for the biopsy can affect the success of later surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>If possible, the surgeon who will remove any tumor that is found should be involved in planning the biopsy.<EOS>
<BOS>In order to plan the best treatment, the sample of tissue removed during the biopsy must be large enough to find out the type of soft tissue sarcoma and do other laboratory tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Tissue samples will be taken from the primary tumor, lymph nodes, and other areas that may have cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the tissue under a microscope to look for cancer cells and to find out the type and grade of the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The grade of a tumor depends on how abnormal the cancer cells look under a microscope and how quickly the cells are dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>Highgrade and midgrade tumors usually grow and spread more quickly than lowgrade tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Because soft tissue sarcoma can be hard to diagnose, the tissue sample should be checked by a pathologist who has experience in diagnosing soft tissue sarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>One or more of the following laboratory tests may be done to study the tissue samples: Molecular test : A laboratory test to check for certain genes, proteins, or other molecules in a sample of tissue, blood, or other body fluid.<EOS>
<BOS>A molecular test may be done with other procedures, such as biopsies, to help diagnose some types of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Molecular tests check for certain gene or chromosome changes that occur in some soft tissue sarcomas.<EOS>
<BOS>Reverse transcriptionpolymerase chain reaction (RTPCR) test: A laboratory test in which cells in a sample of tissue are studied using chemicals to look for changes in the expression of certain genes.<EOS>
<BOS>When genes are expressed they make specific proteins that are needed for the structure, function, and monitoring of the bodys tissues and organs.<EOS>
<BOS>This test is done in order to identify the type of tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Cytogenetic analysis : A laboratory test in which cells in a sample of bone marrow, blood, amniotic fluid, tumor or other tissue is viewed under a microscope to look for changes in the chromosomes.<EOS>
<BOS>Fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH) is a type of cytogenetic analysis.<EOS>
<BOS>Immunocytochemistry : A test that uses antibodies to check for certain antigens (markers) in a sample of cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The antibody is usually linked to an enzyme or fluorescent dye that causes the cells that have that marker to become visible under a microscope.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of test may be used to tell the difference between different types of soft tissue sarcoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Adult Hodgkin lymphoma is a disease in which malignant (cancer) cells form in the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two main types of Hodgkin lymphoma: classical and nodular lymphocytepredominant.<EOS>
<BOS>Age, gender, and EpsteinBarr infection can affect the risk of adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs of adult Hodgkin lymphoma include swollen lymph nodes, fever, night sweats, and weight loss.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the lymph nodes are used to detect (find) and diagnose adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>Adult Hodgkin lymphoma is a disease in which malignant (cancer) cells form in the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>Adult Hodgkin lymphoma is a type of cancer that develops in the lymph system, part of the body's immune system.<EOS>
<BOS>The immune system protects the body from foreign substances, infection, and diseases.<EOS>
<BOS>The lymph system is made up of the following: Lymph: Colorless, watery fluid that carries white blood cells called lymphocytes through the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymphocytes protect the body against infections and the growth of tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph vessels: A network of thin tubes that collect lymph from different parts of the body and return it to the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph nodes: Small, beanshaped structures that filter lymph and store white blood cells that help fight infection and disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph nodes are located along the network of lymph vessels found throughout the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Clusters of lymph nodes are found in the neck, underarm, abdomen, pelvis, and groin.<EOS>
<BOS>Spleen: An organ that makes lymphocytes, filters the blood, stores blood cells, and destroys old blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>It is located on the left side of the abdomen near the stomach.<EOS>
<BOS>Thymus: An organ in which lymphocytes grow and multiply.<EOS>
<BOS>The thymus is in the chest behind the breastbone.<EOS>
<BOS>Tonsils: Two small masses of lymph tissue at the back of the throat.<EOS>
<BOS>The tonsils make lymphocytes.<EOS>
<BOS>Bone marrow: The soft, spongy tissue in the center of large bones.<EOS>
<BOS>Bone marrow makes white blood cells, red blood cells, and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph tissue is also found in other parts of the body such as the stomach, thyroid gland, brain, and skin.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread to the liver and lungs.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymphomas are divided into two general types: Hodgkin lymphoma and nonHodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary is about the treatment of adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>(See the PDQ summary on Adult NonHodgkin Lymphoma Treatment for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>) Hodgkin lymphoma can occur in both adults and children.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment for adults is different than treatment for children.<EOS>
<BOS>Hodgkin lymphoma may also occur in patients who have acquired immunodeficiency syndrome (AIDS); these patients require special treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>See the following PDQ summaries for more information: Childhood Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment AIDSRelated Lymphoma Treatment Hodgkin lymphoma in pregnant women is the same as the disease in nonpregnant women of childbearing age.<EOS>
<BOS>However, treatment is different for pregnant women.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary includes information about treating Hodgkin lymphoma during pregnancy.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two main types of Hodgkin lymphoma: classical and nodular lymphocytepredominant.<EOS>
<BOS>Most Hodgkin lymphomas are the classical type.<EOS>
<BOS>The classical type is broken down into the following four subtypes: Nodular sclerosing Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Mixed cellularity Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymphocyte depletion Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymphocyterich classical Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points There are different types of treatment for patients with adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients with Hodgkin lymphoma should have their treatment planned by a team of health care providers with expertise in treating lymphomas.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may develop late effects that appear months or years after their treatment for Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Three types of standard treatment are used: Chemotherapy Radiation therapy Surgery For pregnant patients with Hodgkin lymphoma, treatment options also include: Watchful waiting Steroid therapy New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy and radiation therapy with stem cell transplant Monoclonal antibody therapy Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of treatment for patients with adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Different types of treatment are available for patients with adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Some treatments are standard (the currently used treatment), and some are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>A treatment clinical trial is a research study meant to help improve current treatments or obtain information on new treatments for patients with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>When clinical trials show that a new treatment is better than the standard treatment, the new treatment may become the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials are open only to patients who have not started treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>For pregnant women with Hodgkin lymphoma, treatment is carefully chosen to protect the fetus.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment decisions are based on the mothers wishes, the stage of the Hodgkin lymphoma, and the age of the fetus.<EOS>
<BOS>The treatment plan may change as the signs and symptoms, cancer, and pregnancy change.<EOS>
<BOS>Choosing the most appropriate cancer treatment is a decision that ideally involves the patient, family, and health care team.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients with Hodgkin lymphoma should have their treatment planned by a team of health care providers with expertise in treating lymphomas.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment will be overseen by a medical oncologist, a doctor who specializes in treating cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The medical oncologist may refer you to other health care providers who have experience and expertise in treating adult Hodgkin lymphoma and who specialize in certain areas of medicine.<EOS>
<BOS>These may include the following specialists: Neurosurgeon.<EOS>
<BOS>Neurologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Rehabilitation specialist.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation oncologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Endocrinologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Hematologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Other oncology specialists.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may develop late effects that appear months or years after their treatment for Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment with chemotherapy and/or radiation therapy for Hodgkin lymphoma may increase the risk of second cancers and other health problems for many months or years after treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>These late effects depend on the type of treatment and the patient's age when treated, and may include: Acute myelogenous leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer of the breast, bone, cervix, gastrointestinal tract, head and neck, lung, soft tissue, and thyroid.<EOS>
<BOS>Heart, lung, and thyroid disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Avascular necrosis of bone (death of bone cells caused by lack of blood flow).<EOS>
<BOS>Herpes zoster (shingles) or severe infection.<EOS>
<BOS>Depression and fatigue.<EOS>
<BOS>Infertility.<EOS>
<BOS>Hypogonadism (low levels of testosterone and estrogen).<EOS>
<BOS>Regular followup by doctors who are expert in finding and treating late effects is important for the longterm health of patients treated for Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Three types of standard treatment are used: Chemotherapy Chemotherapy is a cancer treatment that uses drugs to stop the growth of cancer cells, either by killing the cells or by stopping them from dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is taken by mouth or injected into a vein or muscle, the drugs enter the bloodstream and can reach cancer cells throughout the body (systemic chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is placed directly into the cerebrospinal fluid, an organ, or a body cavity such as the abdomen, the drugs mainly affect cancer cells in those areas (regional chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>The way the chemotherapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy is treatment with more than one anticancer drug.<EOS>
<BOS>When a pregnant woman is treated with chemotherapy for Hodgkin lymphoma, it isn't possible to protect the fetus from being exposed to the chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Some chemotherapy regimens may cause birth defects if given in the first trimester.<EOS>
<BOS>Vinblastine is an anticancer drug that has not been linked with birth defects when given in the second half of pregnancy.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Hodgkin Lymphoma for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy Radiation therapy is a cancer treatment that uses highenergy xrays or other types of radiation to kill cancer cells or keep them from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two types of radiation therapy: External radiation therapy uses a machine outside the body to send radiation toward the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy uses a radioactive substance sealed in needles, seeds, wires, or catheters that are placed directly into or near the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the radiation therapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>External radiation therapy is used to treat adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For a pregnant woman with Hodgkin lymphoma, radiation therapy should be postponed until after delivery, if possible, to avoid any risk to the fetus.<EOS>
<BOS>If immediate treatment is needed, the woman may decide to continue the pregnancy and receive radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>However, lead used to shield the fetus may not protect it from scattered radiation that could possibly cause cancer in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery Laparotomy is a procedure in which an incision (cut) is made in the wall of the abdomen to check the inside of the abdomen for signs of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>The size of the incision depends on the reason the laparotomy is being done.<EOS>
<BOS>Sometimes organs are removed or tissue samples are taken and checked under a microscope for signs of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>If cancer is found, the tissue or organ is removed during the laparotomy.<EOS>
<BOS>For pregnant patients with Hodgkin lymphoma, treatment options also include: Watchful waiting Watchful waiting is closely monitoring a patients condition without giving any treatment unless signs or symptoms appear or change.<EOS>
<BOS>Delivery may be induced when the fetus is 32 to 36 weeks old, so that the mother can begin treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Steroid therapy Steroids are hormones made naturally in the body by the adrenal glands and by reproductive organs.<EOS>
<BOS>Some types of steroids are made in a laboratory.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain steroid drugs have been found to help chemotherapy work better and help stop the growth of cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Steroids can also help the lungs of the fetus develop faster than normal.<EOS>
<BOS>This is important when delivery is induced early.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Hodgkin Lymphoma for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary section describes treatments that are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>It may not mention every new treatment being studied.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy and radiation therapy with stem cell transplant Highdose chemotherapy and radiation therapy with stem cell transplant is a way of giving high doses of chemotherapy and radiation therapy and replacing blood forming cells destroyed by the cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Stem cells (immature blood cells) are removed from the blood or bone marrow of the patient or a donor and are frozen and stored.<EOS>
<BOS>After therapy is completed, the stored stem cells are thawed and given back to the patient through an infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>These reinfused stem cells grow into (and restore) the body's blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The use of lowerdose chemotherapy and radiation therapy with stem cell transplant is also being studied.<EOS>
<BOS>Monoclonal antibody therapy Monoclonal antibody therapy is a cancer treatment that uses antibodies made in the laboratory, from a single type of immune system cell.<EOS>
<BOS>These antibodies can identify substances on cancer cells or normal substances that may help cancer cells grow.<EOS>
<BOS>The antibodies attach to the substances and kill the cancer cells, block their growth, or keep them from spreading.<EOS>
<BOS>Monoclonal antibodies are given by infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>They may be used alone or to carry drugs, toxins, or radioactive material directly to cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests that were done to diagnose the cancer or to find out the stage of the cancer may be repeated.<EOS>
<BOS>Some tests will be repeated in order to see how well the treatment is working.<EOS>
<BOS>Decisions about whether to continue, change, or stop treatment may be based on the results of these tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests will continue to be done from time to time after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of these tests can show if your condition has changed or if the cancer has recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>These tests are sometimes called followup tests or checkups.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options for Adult Hodgkin Lymphoma Early Favorable Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment of early favorable Hodgkin lymphoma may include the following: Combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy with radiation therapy to parts of the body with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy alone to areas of the body with cancer or to the mantle field (neck, chest, armpits).<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage I adult Hodgkin lymphoma and stage II adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Early Unfavorable Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment of early unfavorable Hodgkin lymphoma may include the following: Combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy with radiation therapy to parts of the body with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage I adult Hodgkin lymphoma and stage II adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Advanced Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment of advanced Hodgkin lymphoma may include the following: Combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage III adult Hodgkin lymphoma and stage IV adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Recurrent Adult Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment of recurrent Hodgkin lymphoma may include the following: Combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy followed by highdose chemotherapy and stem cell transplant with or without radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy with radiation therapy to parts of the body with cancer in patients older than 60 years.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy with or without chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy as palliative therapy to relieve symptoms and improve quality of life.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of highdose chemotherapy and stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of lowerdose chemotherapy and radiation therapy followed by stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a monoclonal antibody.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with recurrent adult Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options for Hodgkin Lymphoma During Pregnancy Hodgkin Lymphoma During the First Trimester of Pregnancy When Hodgkin lymphoma is diagnosed in the first trimester of pregnancy, it does not necessarily mean that the woman will be advised to end the pregnancy.<EOS>
<BOS>Each woman's treatment will depend on the stage of the lymphoma, how fast it is growing, and her wishes.<EOS>
<BOS>For women who choose to continue the pregnancy, treatment of Hodgkin lymphoma during the first trimester of pregnancy may include the following: Watchful waiting when the cancer is above the diaphragm and is slowgrowing.<EOS>
<BOS>Delivery may be induced when the fetus is 32 to 36 weeks old so the mother can begin treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy above the diaphragm.<EOS>
<BOS>(A lead shield is used to protect the fetus from the radiation as much as possible.<EOS>
<BOS>) Systemic chemotherapy using one or more drugs.<EOS>
<BOS>Hodgkin Lymphoma During the Second Half of Pregnancy When Hodgkin lymphoma is diagnosed in the second half of pregnancy, most women can delay treatment until after the baby is born.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of Hodgkin lymphoma during the second half of pregnancy may include the following: Watchful waiting, with plans to induce delivery when the fetus is 32 to 36 weeks old.<EOS>
<BOS>Systemic chemotherapy using one or more drugs.<EOS>
<BOS>Steroid therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy to relieve breathing problems caused by a large tumor in the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After adult nonHodgkin lymphoma has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the lymph system or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Stages of adult nonHodgkin lymphoma may include E and S.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for adult nonHodgkin lymphoma: Stage I Stage II Stage III Stage IV Adult nonHodgkin lymphomas may be grouped for treatment according to whether the cancer is indolent or aggressive and whether affected lymph nodes are next to each other in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>After adult nonHodgkin lymphoma has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the lymph system or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out the type of cancer and if cancer cells have spread within the lymph system or to other parts of the body is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>The information gathered from the staging process determines the stage of the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to know the stage of the disease in order to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of the tests and procedures done to diagnose nonHodgkin lymphoma are used to help make decisions about treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may also be used in the staging process: Complete blood count (CBC) with differential : A procedure in which a sample of blood is drawn and checked for the following: The number of red blood cells and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>The number and type of white blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The amount of hemoglobin (the protein that carries oxygen) in the red blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The portion of the blood sample made up of red blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood chemistry studies : A procedure in which a blood sample is checked to measure the amounts of certain substances released into the blood by organs and tissues in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>An unusual (higher or lower than normal) amount of a substance can be a sign of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, such as the lung, lymph nodes, and liver, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>PET scan (positron emission tomography scan): A procedure to find malignant tumor cells in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A small amount of radioactive glucose (sugar) is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The PET scanner rotates around the body and makes a picture of where glucose is being used in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant tumor cells show up brighter in the picture because they are more active and take up more glucose than normal cells do.<EOS>
<BOS>MRI (magnetic resonance imaging): A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>Bone marrow aspiration and biopsy : The removal of bone marrow and a small piece of bone by inserting a needle into the hipbone or breastbone.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the bone marrow and bone under a microscope to look for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Lumbar puncture : A procedure used to collect cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) from the spinal column.<EOS>
<BOS>This is done by placing a needle between two bones in the spine and into the CSF around the spinal cord and removing a sample of the fluid.<EOS>
<BOS>The sample of CSF is checked under a microscope for signs that the cancer has spread to the brain and spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called an LP or spinal tap.<EOS>
<BOS>For pregnant women with nonHodgkin lymphoma, staging tests and procedures that protect the baby from the harms of radiation are used.<EOS>
<BOS>These tests and procedures include MRI, bone marrow aspiration and biopsy, lumbar puncture, and ultrasound.<EOS>
<BOS>An ultrasound exam is a procedure in which highenergy sound waves (ultrasound) are bounced off internal tissues or organs and make echoes.<EOS>
<BOS>The echoes form a picture of body tissues called a sonogram.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Stages of adult nonHodgkin lymphoma may include E and S.<EOS>
<BOS>Adult nonHodgkin lymphoma may be described as follows: E: "E" stands for extranodal and means the cancer is found in an area or organ other than the lymph nodes or has spread to tissues beyond, but near, the major lymphatic areas.<EOS>
<BOS>S: "S" stands for spleen and means the cancer is found in the spleen.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for adult nonHodgkin lymphoma: Stage I Stage I adult nonHodgkin lymphoma is divided into stage I and stage IE.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I: Cancer is found in one lymphatic area (lymph node group, tonsils and nearby tissue, thymus, or spleen).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IE: Cancer is found in one organ or area outside the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II Stage II adult nonHodgkin lymphoma is divided into stage II and stage IIE.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II: Cancer is found in two or more lymph node groups either above or below the diaphragm (the thin muscle below the lungs that helps breathing and separates the chest from the abdomen).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIE: Cancer is found in one or more lymph node groups either above or below the diaphragm.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer is also found outside the lymph nodes in one organ or area on the same side of the diaphragm as the affected lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III Stage III adult nonHodgkin lymphoma is divided into stage III, stage IIIE, stage IIIS, and stage IIIE+S.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III: Cancer is found in lymph node groups above and below the diaphragm (the thin muscle below the lungs that helps breathing and separates the chest from the abdomen).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIE: Cancer is found in lymph node groups above and below the diaphragm and outside the lymph nodes in a nearby organ or area.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIS: Cancer is found in lymph node groups above and below the diaphragm, and in the spleen.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIE+S: Cancer is found in lymph node groups above and below the diaphragm, outside the lymph nodes in a nearby organ or area, and in the spleen.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV In stage IV adult nonHodgkin lymphoma, the cancer: is found throughout one or more organs that are not part of a lymphatic area (lymph node group, tonsils and nearby tissue, thymus, or spleen), and may be in lymph nodes near those organs; or is found in one organ that is not part of a lymphatic area and has spread to organs or lymph nodes far away from that organ; or is found in the liver, bone marrow, cerebrospinal fluid (CSF), or lungs (other than cancer that has spread to the lungs from nearby areas).<EOS>
<BOS>Adult nonHodgkin lymphomas may be grouped for treatment according to whether the cancer is indolent or aggressive and whether affected lymph nodes are next to each other in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>See the General Information section for more information on the types of indolent (slowgrowing) and aggressive (fastgrowing) nonHodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>NonHodgkin lymphoma can also be described as contiguous or noncontiguous: Contiguous lymphomas: Lymphomas in which the lymph nodes with cancer are next to each other.<EOS>
<BOS>Noncontiguous lymphomas: Lymphomas in which the lymph nodes with cancer are not next to each other, but are on the same side of the diaphragm.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After AIDSrelated lymphoma has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the lymph system or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Stages of AIDSrelated lymphoma may include E and S.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for AIDSrelated lymphoma: Stage I Stage II Stage III Stage IV For treatment, AIDSrelated lymphomas are grouped based on where they started in the body, as follows: Peripheral/systemic lymphoma Primary CNS lymphoma After AIDSrelated lymphoma has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the lymph system or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out if cancer cells have spread within the lymph system or to other parts of the body is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>The information gathered from the staging process determines the stage of the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to know the stage in order to plan treatment, but AIDSrelated lymphoma is usually advanced when it is diagnosed.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used in the staging process: Blood chemistry studies : A procedure in which a blood sample is checked to measure the amounts of certain substances released into the blood by organs and tissues in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>An unusual (higher or lower than normal) amount of a substance can be a sign of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>The blood sample will be checked for the level of LDH (lactate dehydrogenase).<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, such as the lung, lymph nodes, and liver, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>PET scan (positron emission tomography scan): A procedure to find malignant tumor cells in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A small amount of radioactive glucose (sugar) is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The PET scanner rotates around the body and makes a picture of where glucose is being used in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant tumor cells show up brighter in the picture because they are more active and take up more glucose than normal cells do.<EOS>
<BOS>MRI (magnetic resonance imaging) with gadolinium : A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A substance called gadolinium is injected into the patient through a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The gadolinium collects around the cancer cells so they show up brighter in the picture.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>Lumbar puncture : A procedure used to collect cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) from the spinal column.<EOS>
<BOS>This is done by placing a needle between two bones in the spine and into the CSF around the spinal cord and removing a sample of the fluid.<EOS>
<BOS>The sample of CSF is checked under a microscope for signs that the cancer has spread to the brain and spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>The sample may also be checked for EpsteinBarr virus.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called an LP or spinal tap.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Stages of AIDSrelated lymphoma may include E and S.<EOS>
<BOS>AIDSrelated lymphoma may be described as follows: E: "E" stands for extranodal and means the cancer is found in an area or organ other than the lymph nodes or has spread to tissues beyond, but near, the major lymphatic areas.<EOS>
<BOS>S: "S" stands for spleen and means the cancer is found in the spleen.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for AIDSrelated lymphoma: Stage I Stage I AIDSrelated lymphoma is divided into stage I and stage IE.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I: Cancer is found in one lymphatic area (lymph node group, tonsils and nearby tissue, thymus, or spleen).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IE: Cancer is found in one organ or area outside the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II Stage II AIDSrelated lymphoma is divided into stage II and stage IIE.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II: Cancer is found in two or more lymph node groups either above or below the diaphragm (the thin muscle below the lungs that helps breathing and separates the chest from the abdomen).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIE: Cancer is found in one or more lymph node groups either above or below the diaphragm.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer is also found outside the lymph nodes in one organ or area on the same side of the diaphragm as the affected lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III Stage III AIDSrelated lymphoma is divided into stage III, stage IIIE, stage IIIS, and stage IIIE+S.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III: Cancer is found in lymph node groups above and below the diaphragm (the thin muscle below the lungs that helps breathing and separates the chest from the abdomen).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIE: Cancer is found in lymph node groups above and below the diaphragm and outside the lymph nodes in a nearby organ or area.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIS: Cancer is found in lymph node groups above and below the diaphragm, and in the spleen.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIE+S: Cancer is found in lymph node groups above and below the diaphragm, outside the lymph nodes in a nearby organ or area, and in the spleen.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV In stage IV AIDSrelated lymphoma, the cancer: is found throughout one or more organs that are not part of a lymphatic area (lymph node group, tonsils and nearby tissue, thymus, or spleen) and may be in lymph nodes near those organs; or is found in one organ that is not part of a lymphatic area and has spread to organs or lymph nodes far away from that organ; or is found in the liver, bone marrow, cerebrospinal fluid (CSF), or lungs (other than cancer that has spread to the lungs from nearby areas).<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who are infected with the EpsteinBarr virus or whose AIDSrelated lymphoma affects the bone marrow have an increased risk of the cancer spreading to the central nervous system (CNS).<EOS>
<BOS>For treatment, AIDSrelated lymphomas are grouped based on where they started in the body, as follows: Peripheral/systemic lymphoma Lymphoma that starts in the lymph system or elsewhere in the body, other than the brain, is called peripheral/systemic lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>It may spread throughout the body, including to the brain or bone marrow.<EOS>
<BOS>It is often diagnosed in an advanced stage.<EOS>
<BOS>Primary CNS lymphoma Primary CNS lymphoma starts in the central nervous system (brain and spinal cord).<EOS>
<BOS>It is linked to the EpsteinBarr virus.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymphoma that starts somewhere else in the body and spreads to the central nervous system is not primary CNS lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the lymph system are used to detect (find) and diagnose childhood Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used: Physical exam and history : An exam of the body to check general signs of health, including checking for signs of disease, such as lumps or anything else that seems unusual.<EOS>
<BOS>A history of the patient's health habits and past illnesses and treatments will also be taken.<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, such as the neck, chest, abdomen, or pelvis, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>PET scan (positron emission tomography scan): A procedure to find malignant tumor cells in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A small amount of radioactive glucose (sugar) is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The PET scanner rotates around the body and makes a picture of where glucose is being used in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant tumor cells show up brighter in the picture because they are more active and take up more glucose than normal cells do.<EOS>
<BOS>Sometimes a PET scan and a CT scan are done at the same time.<EOS>
<BOS>If there is any cancer, this increases the chance that it will be found.<EOS>
<BOS>Chest xray : An xray of the organs and bones inside the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>An xray is a type of energy beam that can go through the body and onto film, making a picture of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Complete blood count (CBC): A procedure in which a sample of blood is drawn and checked for the following: The number of red blood cells, white blood cells, and platelets.<EOS>
<BOS>The amount of hemoglobin (the protein that carries oxygen) in the red blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The portion of the blood sample made up of red blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood chemistry studies : A procedure in which a blood sample is checked to measure the amounts of certain substances released into the blood by organs and tissues in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>An unusual (higher or lower than normal) amount of a substance can be a sign of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Sedimentation rate : A procedure in which a sample of blood is drawn and checked for the rate at which the red blood cells settle to the bottom of the test tube.<EOS>
<BOS>The sedimentation rate is a measure of how much inflammation is in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A higher than normal sedimentation rate may be a sign of lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Also called erythrocyte sedimentation rate, sed rate, or ESR.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph node biopsy : The removal of all or part of a lymph node.<EOS>
<BOS>The lymph node may be removed during an imageguided CT scan or a thoracoscopy, mediastinoscopy, or laparoscopy.<EOS>
<BOS>One of the following types of biopsies may be done: Excisional biopsy : The removal of an entire lymph node.<EOS>
<BOS>Incisional biopsy : The removal of part of a lymph node.<EOS>
<BOS>Core biopsy : The removal of tissue from a lymph node using a wide needle.<EOS>
<BOS>Fineneedle aspiration (FNA) biopsy : The removal of tissue from a lymph node using a thin needle.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the tissue under a microscope to look for cancer cells, especially ReedSternberg cells.<EOS>
<BOS>ReedSternberg cells are common in classical Hodgkin lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>The following test may be done on tissue that was removed: Immunophenotyping : A laboratory test used to identify cells, based on the types of antigens or markers on the surface of the cell.<EOS>
<BOS>This test is used to diagnose the specific type of lymphoma by comparing the cancer cells to normal cells of the immune system.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary section describes treatments that are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>It may not mention every new treatment being studied.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Proton beam radiation therapy Protonbeam therapy is a type of highenergy, external radiation therapy that uses streams of protons (small, positivelycharged particles of matter) to make radiation.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of radiation therapy may help lessen the damage to healthy tissue near the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
