<BOS>Highdose steroids.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy (denileukin diftitox).<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Retinoid therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment options for cutaneous anaplastic large cell lymphoma may include: Surgery, radiation therapy, or both.<EOS>
<BOS>In children, treatment options for mycosis fungoides may include: Steroids applied to the skin.<EOS>
<BOS>Retinoid therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Phototherapy (light therapy using ultraviolet B radiation).<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the rectum and anus are used to detect (find) and diagnose anal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used: Physical exam and history : An exam of the body to check general signs of health, including checking for signs of disease, such as lumps or anything else that seems unusual.<EOS>
<BOS>A history of the patients health habits and past illnesses and treatments will also be taken.<EOS>
<BOS>Digital rectal examination (DRE): An exam of the anus and rectum.<EOS>
<BOS>The doctor or nurse inserts a lubricated, gloved finger into the lower part of the rectum to feel for lumps or anything else that seems unusual.<EOS>
<BOS>Anoscopy: An exam of the anus and lower rectum using a short, lighted tube called an anoscope.<EOS>
<BOS>Proctoscopy : An exam of the rectum using a short, lighted tube called a proctoscope.<EOS>
<BOS>Endoanal or endorectal ultrasound : A procedure in which an ultrasound transducer (probe) is inserted into the anus or rectum and used to bounce highenergy sound waves (ultrasound) off internal tissues or organs and make echoes.<EOS>
<BOS>The echoes form a picture of body tissues called a sonogram.<EOS>
<BOS>Biopsy : The removal of cells or tissues so they can be viewed under a microscope by a pathologist to check for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>If an abnormal area is seen during the anoscopy, a biopsy may be done at that time.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points There is no standard staging system for adult brain and spinal cord tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Imaging tests may be repeated after surgery to help plan more treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>There is no standard staging system for adult brain and spinal cord tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>The extent or spread of cancer is usually described as stages.<EOS>
<BOS>There is no standard staging system for brain and spinal cord tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Brain tumors that begin in the brain may spread to other parts of the brain and spinal cord, but they rarely spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of primary brain and spinal cord tumors is based on the following: The type of cell in which the tumor began.<EOS>
<BOS>Where the tumor formed in the brain or spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>The amount of cancer left after surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>The grade of the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of tumors that have spread to the brain from other parts of the body is based on the number of tumors in the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>Imaging tests may be repeated after surgery to help plan more treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests and procedures used to diagnose a brain or spinal cord tumor may be repeated after treatment to find out how much tumor is left.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points A childhood brain or spinal cord tumor is a disease in which abnormal cells form in the tissues of the brain or spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>The brain controls many important body functions.<EOS>
<BOS>The spinal cord connects the brain with nerves in most parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Brain and spinal cord tumors are a common type of childhood cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The cause of most childhood brain and spinal cord tumors is unknown.<EOS>
<BOS>The signs and symptoms of childhood brain and spinal cord tumors are not the same in every child.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the brain and spinal cord are used to detect (find) childhood brain and spinal cord tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Most childhood brain tumors are diagnosed and removed in surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Some childhood brain and spinal cord tumors are diagnosed by imaging tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery).<EOS>
<BOS>A childhood brain or spinal cord tumor is a disease in which abnormal cells form in the tissues of the brain or spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>There are many types of childhood brain and spinal cord tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumors are formed by the abnormal growth of cells and may begin in different areas of the brain or spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumors may be benign (not cancer) or malignant (cancer).<EOS>
<BOS>Benign brain tumors grow and press on nearby areas of the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>They rarely spread into other tissues.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant brain tumors are likely to grow quickly and spread into other brain tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>When a tumor grows into or presses on an area of the brain, it may stop that part of the brain from working the way it should.<EOS>
<BOS>Both benign and malignant brain tumors can cause signs or symptoms and need treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Together, the brain and spinal cord make up the central nervous system (CNS).<EOS>
<BOS>The brain controls many important body functions.<EOS>
<BOS>The brain has three major parts: The cerebrum is the largest part of the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>It is at the top of the head.<EOS>
<BOS>The cerebrum controls thinking, learning, problem solving, emotions, speech, reading, writing, and voluntary movement.<EOS>
<BOS>The cerebellum is in the lower back of the brain (near the middle of the back of the head).<EOS>
<BOS>It controls movement, balance, and posture.<EOS>
<BOS>The brain stem connects the brain to the spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>It is in the lowest part of the brain (just above the back of the neck).<EOS>
<BOS>The brain stem controls breathing, heart rate, and the nerves and muscles used in seeing, hearing, walking, talking, and eating.<EOS>
<BOS>The spinal cord connects the brain with nerves in most parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The spinal cord is a column of nerve tissue that runs from the brain stem down the center of the back.<EOS>
<BOS>It is covered by three thin layers of tissue called membranes.<EOS>
<BOS>These membranes are surrounded by the vertebrae (back bones).<EOS>
<BOS>Spinal cord nerves carry messages between the brain and the rest of the body, such as a message from the brain to cause muscles to move or a message from the skin to the brain to feel touch.<EOS>
<BOS>Brain and spinal cord tumors are a common type of childhood cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Although cancer is rare in children, brain and spinal cord tumors are the third most common type of childhood cancer, after leukemia and lymphoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Brain tumors can occur in both children and adults.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment for children is usually different than treatment for adults.<EOS>
<BOS>(See the PDQ summary on Adult Central Nervous System Tumors Treatment for more information about the treatment of adults.<EOS>
<BOS>) This summary describes the treatment of primary brain and spinal cord tumors (tumors that begin in the brain and spinal cord).<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of metastatic brain and spinal cord tumors is not covered in this summary.<EOS>
<BOS>Metastatic tumors are formed by cancer cells that begin in other parts of the body and spread to the brain or spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>The signs and symptoms of childhood brain and spinal cord tumors are not the same in every child.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms depend on the following: Where the tumor forms in the brain or spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>The size of the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>How fast the tumor grows.<EOS>
<BOS>The child's age and development.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms may be caused by childhood brain and spinal cord tumors or by other conditions, including cancer that has spread to the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your child's doctor if your child has any of the following: Brain Tumor Signs and Symptoms Morning headache or headache that goes away after vomiting.<EOS>
<BOS>Frequent nausea and vomiting.<EOS>
<BOS>Vision, hearing, and speech problems.<EOS>
<BOS>Loss of balance and trouble walking.<EOS>
<BOS>Unusual sleepiness or change in activity level.<EOS>
<BOS>Unusual changes in personality or behavior.<EOS>
<BOS>Seizures.<EOS>
<BOS>Increase in the head size (in infants).<EOS>
<BOS>Spinal Cord Tumor Signs and Symptoms Back pain or pain that spreads from the back towards the arms or legs.<EOS>
<BOS>A change in bowel habits or trouble urinating.<EOS>
<BOS>Weakness in the legs.<EOS>
<BOS>Trouble walking.<EOS>
<BOS>In addition to these signs and symptoms of brain and spinal cord tumors, some children are unable to reach certain growth and development milestones such as sitting up, walking, and talking in sentences.<EOS>
<BOS>The signs and symptoms of brain stem glioma are not the same in every child.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms depend on the following: Where the tumor forms in the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>The size of the tumor and whether it has spread all through the brain stem.<EOS>
<BOS>How fast the tumor grows.<EOS>
<BOS>The child's age and development.<EOS>
<BOS>Some tumors do not cause signs or symptoms.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms may be caused by childhood brain stem gliomas or by other conditions.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your child's doctor if your child has any of the following: Loss of ability to move one side of the face and/or body.<EOS>
<BOS>Loss of balance and trouble walking.<EOS>
<BOS>Vision and hearing problems.<EOS>
<BOS>Morning headache or headache that goes away after vomiting.<EOS>
<BOS>Nausea and vomiting.<EOS>
<BOS>Unusual sleepiness.<EOS>
<BOS>More or less energy than usual.<EOS>
<BOS>Changes in behavior.<EOS>
<BOS>Trouble learning in school.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points The plan for cancer treatment depends on whether the tumor is in one area of the brain or has spread all through the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>The plan for cancer treatment depends on whether the tumor is in one area of the brain or has spread all through the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>Staging is the process used to find out how much cancer there is and if cancer has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to know the stage in order to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>There is no standard staging system for childhood brain stem glioma.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment is based on the following: Whether the tumor is newly diagnosed or recurrent (has come back after treatment).<EOS>
<BOS>The type of tumor (either a diffuse intrinsic pontine glioma or a focal glioma).<EOS>
<BOS>Certain genetic conditions increase the risk of childhood CNS embryonal tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Anything that increases the risk of getting a disease is called a risk factor.<EOS>
<BOS>Having a risk factor does not mean that you will get cancer; not having risk factors doesnt mean that you will not get cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your childs doctor if you think your child may be at risk.<EOS>
<BOS>Risk factors for CNS embryonal tumors include having the following inherited diseases: Turcot syndrome.<EOS>
<BOS>RubinsteinTaybi syndrome.<EOS>
<BOS>Nevoid basal cell carcinoma (Gorlin) syndrome.<EOS>
<BOS>LiFraumeni syndrome.<EOS>
<BOS>Fanconi anemia.<EOS>
<BOS>In most cases, the cause of CNS embryonal tumors is not known.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the brain, vision, and hormone levels are used to detect (find) childhood craniopharyngiomas.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used: Physical exam and history : An exam of the body to check general signs of health, including checking for signs of disease, such as lumps or anything else that seems unusual.<EOS>
<BOS>A history of the patients health habits and past illnesses and treatments will also be taken.<EOS>
<BOS>Neurological exam : A series of questions and tests to check the brain, spinal cord, and nerve function.<EOS>
<BOS>The exam checks a persons mental status, coordination, and ability to walk normally, and how well the muscles, senses, and reflexes work.<EOS>
<BOS>This may also be called a neuro exam or a neurologic exam.<EOS>
<BOS>Visual field exam: An exam to check a persons field of vision (the total area in which objects can be seen).<EOS>
<BOS>This test measures both central vision (how much a person can see when looking straight ahead) and peripheral vision (how much a person can see in all other directions while staring straight ahead).<EOS>
<BOS>Any loss of vision may be a sign of a tumor that has damaged or pressed on the parts of the brain that affect eyesight.<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>MRI (magnetic resonance imaging) of the brain and spinal cord with gadolinium : A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>A substance called gadolinium is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The gadolinium collects around the tumor cells so they show up brighter in the picture.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>Blood chemistry studies : A procedure in which a blood sample is checked to measure the amounts of certain substances released into the blood by organs and tissues in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>An unusual (higher or lower than normal) amount of a substance can be a sign of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood hormone studies: A procedure in which a blood sample is checked to measure the amounts of certain hormones released into the blood by organs and tissues in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>An unusual (higher or lower than normal) amount of a substance can be a sign of disease in the organ or tissue that makes it.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, the blood may be checked for unusual levels of thyroidstimulating hormone (TSH) or adrenocorticotropic hormone (ACTH).<EOS>
<BOS>TSH and ACTH are made by the pituitary gland in the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>Childhood craniopharyngiomas are diagnosed and may be removed in the same surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Doctors may think a mass is a craniopharyngioma based on where it is in the brain and how it looks on a CT scan or MRI.<EOS>
<BOS>In order to be sure, a sample of tissue is needed.<EOS>
<BOS>One of the following types of biopsy procedures may be used to take the sample of tissue: Open biopsy: A hollow needle is inserted through a hole in the skull into the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>Computerguided needle biopsy: A hollow needle guided by a computer is inserted through a small hole in the skull into the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>Transsphenoidal biopsy: Instruments are inserted through the nose and sphenoid bone (a butterflyshaped bone at the base of the skull) and into the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the tissue under a microscope to look for tumor cells.<EOS>
<BOS>If tumor cells are found, as much tumor as safely possible may be removed during the same surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>The following laboratory test may be done on the sample of tissue that is removed: Immunohistochemistry : A test that uses antibodies to check for certain antigens in a sample of tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The antibody is usually linked to a radioactive substance or a dye that causes the tissue to light up under a microscope.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of test may be used to tell the difference between different types of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The signs and symptoms of childhood ependymoma are not the same in every child.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms depend on the following: The child's age.<EOS>
<BOS>Where the tumor has formed.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms may be caused by childhood ependymoma or by other conditions.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your child's doctor if your child has any of the following: Frequent headaches.<EOS>
<BOS>Seizures.<EOS>
<BOS>Nausea and vomiting.<EOS>
<BOS>Pain or stiffness in the neck.<EOS>
<BOS>Loss of balance or trouble walking.<EOS>
<BOS>Weakness in the legs.<EOS>
<BOS>Blurry vision.<EOS>
<BOS>Back pain.<EOS>
<BOS>A change in bowel function.<EOS>
<BOS>Trouble urinating.<EOS>
<BOS>Confusion or irritability.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>The prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options depend on: Where the tumor has formed in the central nervous system (CNS).<EOS>
<BOS>Whether there are certain changes in the genes or chromosomes.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether any cancer cells remain after surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The type of ependymoma.<EOS>
<BOS>The age of the child when the tumor is diagnosed.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer has spread to other parts of the brain or spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the tumor has just been diagnosed or has recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>Prognosis also depends on the type and dose of radiation therapy that is given.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Childhood liver cancer is a disease in which malignant (cancer) cells form in the tissues of the liver.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain diseases and disorders can increase the risk of childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of childhood liver cancer include a lump or pain in the abdomen.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the liver and the blood are used to detect (find) and diagnose childhood liver cancer and find out whether the cancer has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>Childhood liver cancer is a disease in which malignant (cancer) cells form in the tissues of the liver.<EOS>
<BOS>The liver is one of the largest organs in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>It has four lobes and fills the upper right side of the abdomen inside the rib cage.<EOS>
<BOS>Three of the many important functions of the liver are: To filter harmful substances from the blood so they can be passed from the body in stools and urine.<EOS>
<BOS>To make bile to help digest fats from food.<EOS>
<BOS>To store glycogen (sugar), which the body uses for energy.<EOS>
<BOS>Liver cancer is rare in children and adolescents.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two main types of childhood liver cancer: Hepatoblastoma: Hepatoblastoma is the most common type of childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>It usually affects children younger than 3 years of age.<EOS>
<BOS>In hepatoblastoma, the histology (how the cancer cells look under a microscope) affects the way the cancer is treated.<EOS>
<BOS>The histology for hepatoblastoma may be one of the following: Pure fetal histology.<EOS>
<BOS>Small cell undifferentiated histology.<EOS>
<BOS>Nonpure fetal histology, nonsmall cell undifferentiated histology.<EOS>
<BOS>Hepatocellular carcinoma: Hepatocellular carcinoma usually affects older children and adolescents.<EOS>
<BOS>It is more common in areas of Asia that have high rates of hepatitis infection than in the U.<EOS>
<BOS>S.<EOS>
<BOS>The treatment of two less common types of childhood liver cancer is also discussed in this summary: Undifferentiated embryonal sarcoma of the liver: This type of liver cancer usually occurs in children between 5 and 10 years of age.<EOS>
<BOS>It often spreads all through the liver and/or to the lungs.<EOS>
<BOS>Infantile choriocarcinoma of the liver is a very rare tumor that starts in the placenta and spreads to the fetus.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor is usually found during the first few months of life.<EOS>
<BOS>Also, the mother of the child may be diagnosed with choriocarcinoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Choriocarcinoma is a type of gestational trophoblastic disease and needs treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Gestational Trophoblastic Disease Treatment summary for information on the treatment of choriocarcinoma.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary is about the treatment of primary liver cancer (cancer that begins in the liver).<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of metastatic liver cancer, which is cancer that begins in other parts of the body and spreads to the liver, is not discussed in this summary.<EOS>
<BOS>Primary liver cancer can occur in both adults and children.<EOS>
<BOS>However, treatment for children is different than treatment for adults.<EOS>
<BOS>See the PDQ summary on Adult Primary Liver Cancer Treatment for more information on the treatment of adults.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>The prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options for hepatoblastoma depend on the following: The PRETEXT or POSTTEXT group.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer has spread to other places in the body, such as the lungs or certain large blood vessels.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer can be removed completely by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>How the cancer responds to chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>How the cancer cells look under a microscope.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the AFP blood levels go down after treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer has just been diagnosed or has recurred.<EOS>
<BOS>Age of the child.<EOS>
<BOS>The prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options for hepatocellular carcinoma depend on the following: The PRETEXT or POSTTEXT group.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer has spread to other places in the body, such as the lungs.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer can be removed completely by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>How the cancer responds to chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>How the cancer cells look under a microscope.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the child has hepatitis B infection.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer has just been diagnosed or has recurred.<EOS>
<BOS>For childhood liver cancer that recurs (comes back) after initial treatment, the prognosis and treatment options depend on: Where in the body the tumor recurred.<EOS>
<BOS>The type of treatment used to treat the initial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Childhood liver cancer may be cured if the tumor is small and can be completely removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Complete removal is possible more often for hepatoblastoma than for hepatocellular carcinoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After childhood liver cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the liver or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two grouping systems for childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>There are four PRETEXT and POSTTEXT groups: PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group I PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group II PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group III PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group IV There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>After childhood liver cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the liver or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out if cancer has spread within the liver, to nearby tissues or organs, or to other parts of the body is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>In childhood liver cancer, the PRETEXT and POSTTEXT groups are used instead of stage to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of the tests and procedures done to detect, diagnose, and find out whether the cancer has spread are used to determine the PRETEXT and POSTTEXT groups.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two grouping systems for childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Two grouping systems are used for childhood liver cancer: The PRETEXT group describes the tumor before the patient has treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The POSTTEXT group describes the tumor after the patient has treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>There are four PRETEXT and POSTTEXT groups: The liver is divided into 4 sections.<EOS>
<BOS>The PRETEXT and POSTTEXT groups depend on which sections of the liver have cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group I In group I, the cancer is found in one section of the liver.<EOS>
<BOS>Three sections of the liver that are next to each other do not have cancer in them.<EOS>
<BOS>PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group II In group II, cancer is found in one or two sections of the liver.<EOS>
<BOS>Two sections of the liver that are next to each other do not have cancer in them.<EOS>
<BOS>PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group III In group III, one of the following is true: Cancer is found in three sections of the liver and one section does not have cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer is found in two sections of the liver and two sections that are not next to each other do not have cancer in them.<EOS>
<BOS>PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group IV In group IV, cancer is found in all four sections of the liver.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>When cancer spreads to another part of the body, it is called metastasis.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells break away from where they began (the primary tumor) and travel through the lymph system or blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the lymph system, travels through the lymph vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the blood, travels through the blood vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The metastatic tumor is the same type of cancer as the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, if childhood liver cancer spreads to the lung, the cancer cells in the lung are actually liver cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The disease is metastatic liver cancer, not lung cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points There are different types of treatment for patients with childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Children with liver cancer should have their treatment planned by a team of healthcare providers who are experts in treating this rare childhood cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some cancer treatments cause side effects months or years after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>Six types of standard treatment are used: Surgery Watchful waiting Chemotherapy Radiation therapy Ablation therapy Antiviral treatment New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of treatment for patients with childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Different types of treatments are available for children with liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some treatments are standard (the currently used treatment), and some are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>A treatment clinical trial is a research study meant to help improve current treatments or obtain information on new treatments for patients with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>When clinical trials show that a new treatment is better than the standard treatment, the new treatment may become the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Taking part in a clinical trial should be considered for all children with liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials are open only to patients who have not started treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Children with liver cancer should have their treatment planned by a team of healthcare providers who are experts in treating this rare childhood cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment will be overseen by a pediatric oncologist, a doctor who specializes in treating children with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The pediatric oncologist works with other healthcare providers who are experts in treating children with liver cancer and who specialize in certain areas of medicine.<EOS>
<BOS>It is especially important to have a pediatric surgeon with experience in liver surgery who can send patients to a liver transplant program if needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Other specialists may include the following: Pediatrician.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation oncologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Pediatric nurse specialist.<EOS>
<BOS>Rehabilitation specialist.<EOS>
<BOS>Psychologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Social worker.<EOS>
<BOS>Some cancer treatments cause side effects months or years after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>Side effects from cancer treatment that begin during or after treatment and continue for months or years are called late effects.<EOS>
<BOS>Late effects of cancer treatment may include: Physical problems.<EOS>
<BOS>Changes in mood, feelings, thinking, learning, or memory.<EOS>
<BOS>Second cancers (new types of cancer).<EOS>
<BOS>Some late effects may be treated or controlled.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to talk with your child's doctors about the effects cancer treatment can have on your child.<EOS>
<BOS>(See the PDQ summary on Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer for more information).<EOS>
<BOS>Six types of standard treatment are used: Surgery When possible, the cancer is removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Partial hepatectomy: Removal of the part of the liver where cancer is found.<EOS>
<BOS>The part removed may be a wedge of tissue, an entire lobe, or a larger part of the liver, along with a small amount of normal tissue around it.<EOS>
<BOS>Total hepatectomy and liver transplant: Removal of the entire liver followed by a transplant of a healthy liver from a donor.<EOS>
<BOS>A liver transplant may be possible when cancer has not spread beyond the liver and a donated liver can be found.<EOS>
<BOS>If the patient has to wait for a donated liver, other treatment is given as needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Resection of metastases: Surgery to remove cancer that has spread outside of the liver, such as to nearby tissues, the lungs, or the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>Factors that affect the type of surgery used include the following: The PRETEXT group and POSTTEXT group.<EOS>
<BOS>The size of the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether there is more than one tumor in the liver.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer has spread to nearby large blood vessels.<EOS>
<BOS>The level of alphafetoprotein (AFP) in the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the tumor can be shrunk by chemotherapy so that it can be removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether a liver transplant is needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy is sometimes given before surgery, to shrink the tumor and make it easier to remove.<EOS>
<BOS>This is called neoadjuvant therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Even if the doctor removes all the cancer that can be seen at the time of the surgery, some patients may be given chemotherapy or radiation therapy after surgery to kill any cancer cells that are left.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment given after the surgery, to lower the risk that the cancer will come back, is called adjuvant therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Watchful waiting Watchful waiting is closely monitoring a patients condition without giving any treatment until signs or symptoms appear or change.<EOS>
<BOS>In hepatoblastoma, this treatment is only used for small tumors that have been completely removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy Chemotherapy is a cancer treatment that uses drugs to stop the growth of cancer cells, either by killing the cells or by stopping them from dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is taken by mouth or injected into a vein or muscle, the drugs enter the bloodstream and can reach cancer cells throughout the body (systemic chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is placed directly into the cerebrospinal fluid, an organ, or a body cavity such as the abdomen, the drugs mainly affect cancer cells in those areas (regional chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment using more than one anticancer drug is called combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemoembolization of the hepatic artery (the main artery that supplies blood to the liver) is a type of regional chemotherapy used to treat childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The anticancer drug is injected into the hepatic artery through a catheter (thin tube).<EOS>
<BOS>The drug is mixed with a substance that blocks the artery, cutting off blood flow to the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Most of the anticancer drug is trapped near the tumor and only a small amount of the drug reaches other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The blockage may be temporary or permanent, depending on the substance used to block the artery.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor is prevented from getting the oxygen and nutrients it needs to grow.<EOS>
<BOS>The liver continues to receive blood from the hepatic portal vein, which carries blood from the stomach and intestine.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called transarterial chemoembolization or TACE.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the chemotherapy is given depends on the type of the cancer being treated and the PRETEXT or POSTTEXT group.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy Radiation therapy is a cancer treatment that uses highenergy xrays or other types of radiation to kill cancer cells or keep them from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two types of radiation therapy: External radiation therapy uses a machine outside the body to send radiation toward the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy uses a radioactive substance sealed in needles, seeds, wires, or catheters that are placed directly into or near the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the radiation therapy is given depends on the type of the cancer being treated and the PRETEXT or POSTTEXT group.<EOS>
<BOS>Radioembolization of the hepatic artery (the main artery that supplies blood to the liver) is a type of internal radiation therapy used to treat hepatocellular carcinoma.<EOS>
<BOS>A very small amount of a radioactive substance is attached to tiny beads that are injected into the hepatic artery through a catheter (thin tube).<EOS>
<BOS>The beads are mixed with a substance that blocks the artery, cutting off blood flow to the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Most of the radiation is trapped near the tumor to kill the cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>This is done to relieve symptoms and improve quality of life for children with hepatocellular carcinoma.<EOS>
<BOS>External radiation therapy is used to treat hepatoblastoma that cannot be removed by surgery or has spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Ablation therapy Ablation therapy removes or destroys tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Different types of ablation therapy are used for liver cancer: Radiofrequency ablation: The use of special needles that are inserted directly through the skin or through an incision in the abdomen to reach the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Highenergy radio waves heat the needles and tumor which kills cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiofrequency ablation is being used to treat recurrent hepatoblastoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Percutaneous ethanol injection: A small needle is used to inject ethanol (pure alcohol) directly into a tumor to kill cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Several treatments may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Percutaneous ethanol injection is being used to treat recurrent hepatoblastoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Antiviral treatment Hepatocellular carcinoma that is linked to the hepatitis B virus may be treated with antiviral drugs.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary section describes treatments that are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>It may not mention every new treatment being studied.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy Targeted therapy is a type of treatment that uses drugs or other substances to attack specific cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapies usually cause less harm to normal cells than chemotherapy or radiation therapy do.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy is being studied for the treatment of undifferentiated embryonal sarcoma of the liver and liver cancer that has come back.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests that were done to diagnose the cancer or to find out the treatment group may be repeated.<EOS>
<BOS>Some tests will be repeated in order to see how well the treatment is working.<EOS>
<BOS>Decisions about whether to continue, change, or stop treatment may be based on the results of these tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests will continue to be done from time to time after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of these tests can show if your condition has changed or if the cancer has recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>These tests are sometimes called followup tests or checkups.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options for Childhood Liver Cancer Hepatoblastoma Treatment options for hepatoblastoma that can be removed by surgery at the time of diagnosis may include the following: Surgery to remove the tumor, followed by watchful waiting or chemotherapy, for hepatoblastoma with pure fetal histology.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove the tumor, with combination chemotherapy given either before surgery, after surgery, or both, for hepatoblastoma that is not pure fetal histology.<EOS>
<BOS>For hepatoblastoma with small cell undifferentiated histology, aggressive chemotherapy is given.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment options for hepatoblastoma that cannot be removed by surgery or is not removed at the time of diagnosis may include the following: Combination chemotherapy to shrink the tumor, followed by surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy followed by a liver transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemoembolization of the hepatic artery to shrink the tumor, followed by surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For hepatoblastoma that has spread to other parts of the body at the time of diagnosis, combination chemotherapy is given to shrink the cancer in the liver and cancer that has spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>After chemotherapy, imaging tests are done to check whether the cancer can be removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment options may include the following: If the cancer in the liver and other parts of the body can be removed, surgery will be done to remove the tumors followed by chemotherapy to kill any cancer cells that may remain.<EOS>
<BOS>If the cancer in the liver cannot be removed by surgery but there are no signs of cancer in other parts of the body, the treatment may be a liver transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>If the cancer in other parts of the body cannot be removed or a liver transplant is not possible, chemotherapy, chemoembolization of the hepatic artery, or radiation therapy may be given.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment options in clinical trials for newly diagnosed hepatoblastoma include: A clinical trial of new treatment regimens based on how likely it is the cancer will recur after initial treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Hepatocellular Carcinoma Treatment options for hepatocellular carcinoma that can be removed by surgery at the time of diagnosis may include the following: Surgery alone to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove the tumor, followed by chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy followed by surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment options for hepatocellular carcinoma that cannot be removed by surgery at the time of diagnosis may include the following: Chemotherapy to shrink the tumor, followed by surgery to completely remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy to shrink the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>If surgery to completely remove the tumor is not possible, further treatment may include the following: Liver transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemoembolization of the hepatic artery to shrink the tumor, followed by surgery to remove as much of the tumor as possible or liver transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemoembolization of the hepatic artery alone.<EOS>
<BOS>Radioembolization of the hepatic artery as palliative therapy to relieve symptoms and improve the quality of life.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment for hepatocellular carcinoma that has spread to other parts of the body at the time of diagnosis may include: Combination chemotherapy to shrink the tumor, followed by surgery to remove as much of the tumor as possible from the liver and other places where cancer has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>Studies have not shown that this treatment works well but some patients may have some benefit.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment options for hepatocellular carcinoma related to hepatitis B virus (HBV) infection include: Surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Antiviral drugs that treat infection caused by the hepatitis B virus.<EOS>
<BOS>Undifferentiated Embryonal Sarcoma of the Liver Treatment options for undifferentiated embryonal sarcoma of the liver (UESL) may include the following: Combination chemotherapy to shrink the tumor, followed by surgery to remove as much of the tumor as possible.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy may also be given after surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove the tumor followed by chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>A second surgery may be done to remove tumor that remains, followed by more chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Liver transplant if surgery to remove the tumor is not possible.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a combination of targeted therapy, chemotherapy and/or radiation therapy before surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Infantile Choriocarcinoma of the Liver Treatment options for choriocarcinoma of the liver in infants may include the following: Combination chemotherapy to shrink the tumor, followed by surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Recurrent Childhood Liver Cancer Treatment of recurrent hepatoblastoma may include the following: Surgery to remove isolated (single and separate) metastatic tumors with or without chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Liver transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Ablation therapy (radiofrequency ablation or percutaneous ethanol injection).<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of progressive or recurrent hepatocellular carcinoma may include the following: Chemoembolization of the hepatic artery to shrink the tumor before liver transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Liver transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options in Clinical Trials Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with childhood liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your child's doctor about clinical trials that may be right for your child.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Avoiding risk factors and increasing protective factors may help prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following risk factors may increase the risk of liver cancer: Hepatitis B and C Cirrhosis Aflatoxin The following protective factor may decrease the risk of liver cancer: Hepatitis B vaccine Cancer prevention clinical trials are used to study ways to prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>New ways to prevent liver cancer are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Avoiding risk factors and increasing protective factors may help prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Avoiding cancer risk factors may help prevent certain cancers.<EOS>
<BOS>Risk factors include smoking, being overweight, and not getting enough exercise.<EOS>
<BOS>Increasing protective factors such as quitting smoking and exercising may also help prevent some cancers.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk to your doctor or other health care professional about how you might lower your risk of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following risk factors may increase the risk of liver cancer: Hepatitis B and C Having chronic hepatitis B or chronic hepatitis C increases the risk of developing liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk is even greater for people with both hepatitis B and C.<EOS>
<BOS>Also, the longer the hepatitis infection lasts (especially hepatitis C), the greater the risk.<EOS>
<BOS>In a study of patients with chronic hepatitis C, those who were treated to lower their iron levels by having blood drawn and eating a lowiron diet were less likely to develop liver cancer than those who did not have this treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Cirrhosis The risk of developing liver cancer is increased for people who have cirrhosis, a disease in which healthy liver tissue is replaced by scar tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The scar tissue blocks the flow of blood through the liver and keeps it from working as it should.<EOS>
<BOS>Chronic alcoholism and chronic hepatitis C are the most common causes of cirrhosis.<EOS>
<BOS>Aflatoxin The risk of developing liver cancer may be increased by eating foods that contain aflatoxin (poison from a fungus that can grow on foods, such as grains and nuts, that have not been stored properly).<EOS>
<BOS>The following protective factor may decrease the risk of liver cancer: Hepatitis B vaccine Preventing hepatitis B infection (by being vaccinated for hepatitis B) has been shown to lower the risk of liver cancer in children.<EOS>
<BOS>It is not yet known if it lowers the risk in adults.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer prevention clinical trials are used to study ways to prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer prevention clinical trials are used to study ways to lower the risk of developing certain types of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some cancer prevention trials are conducted with healthy people who have not had cancer but who have an increased risk for cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Other prevention trials are conducted with people who have had cancer and are trying to prevent another cancer of the same type or to lower their chance of developing a new type of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials are done with healthy volunteers who are not known to have any risk factors for cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The purpose of some cancer prevention clinical trials is to find out whether actions people take can prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>These may include eating fruits and vegetables, exercising, quitting smoking, or taking certain medicines, vitamins, minerals, or food supplements.<EOS>
<BOS>New ways to prevent liver cancer are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials can be found in the Clinical Trials section of the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Check NCI's list of cancer clinical trials for liver cancer prevention trials that are now accepting patients.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>The prognosis (chance of recovery) is affected by certain factors before and after treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The prognosis of untreated osteosarcoma and MFH depends on the following: Where the tumor is in the body and whether tumors formed in more than one bone.<EOS>
<BOS>The size of the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer has spread to other parts of the body and where it has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>The type of tumor (based on how the cancer cells look under a microscope).<EOS>
<BOS>The patient's age and weight at diagnosis.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the tumor has caused a break in the bone.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the patient has certain genetic diseases.<EOS>
<BOS>After osteosarcoma or MFH is treated, prognosis also depends on the following: How much of the cancer was killed by chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>How much of the tumor was taken out by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether chemotherapy is delayed for more than 3 weeks after surgery takes place.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer has recurred (come back) within 2 years of diagnosis.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment options for osteosarcoma and MFH depend on the following: Where the tumor is in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The size of the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The stage of the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the bones are still growing.<EOS>
<BOS>The patient's age and general health.<EOS>
<BOS>The desire of the patient and family for the patient to be able to participate in activities such as sports or have a certain appearance.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer is newly diagnosed or has recurred after treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After a gastrointestinal carcinoid tumor has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the stomach and intestines or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The plan for cancer treatment depends on where the carcinoid tumor is found and whether it can be removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>After a gastrointestinal carcinoid tumor has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the stomach and intestines or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Staging is the process used to find out how far the cancer has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>The information gathered from the staging process determines the stage of the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of tests and procedures used to diagnose gastrointestinal (GI) carcinoid tumors may also be used for staging.<EOS>
<BOS>See the General Information section for a description of these tests and procedures.<EOS>
<BOS>A bone scan may be done to check if there are rapidly dividing cells, such as cancer cells, in the bone.<EOS>
<BOS>A very small amount of radioactive material is injected into a vein and travels through the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>The radioactive material collects in the bones and is detected by a scanner.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>When cancer spreads to another part of the body, it is called metastasis.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells break away from where they began (the primary tumor) and travel through the lymph system or blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the lymph system, travels through the lymph vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the blood, travels through the blood vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The metastatic tumor is the same type of tumor as the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, if a gastrointestinal (GI) carcinoid tumor spreads to the liver, the tumor cells in the liver are actually GI carcinoid tumor cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The disease is metastatic GI carcinoid tumor, not liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The plan for cancer treatment depends on where the carcinoid tumor is found and whether it can be removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>For many cancers it is important to know the stage of the cancer in order to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>However, the treatment of gastrointestinal carcinoid tumors is not based on the stage of the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment depends mainly on whether the tumor can be removed by surgery and if the tumor has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment is based on whether the tumor: Can be completely removed by surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Has spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Has come back after treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor may come back in the stomach or intestines or in other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Has not gotten better with treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Polycythemia vera is a disease in which too many red blood cells are made in the bone marrow.<EOS>
<BOS>Symptoms of polycythemia vera include headaches and a feeling of fullness below the ribs on the left side.<EOS>
<BOS>Special blood tests are used to diagnose polycythemia vera.<EOS>
<BOS>Polycythemia vera is a disease in which too many red blood cells are made in the bone marrow.<EOS>
<BOS>In polycythemia vera, the blood becomes thickened with too many red blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The number of white blood cells and platelets may also increase.<EOS>
<BOS>These extra blood cells may collect in the spleen and cause it to swell.<EOS>
<BOS>The increased number of red blood cells, white blood cells, or platelets in the blood can cause bleeding problems and make clots form in blood vessels.<EOS>
<BOS>This can increase the risk of stroke or heart attack.<EOS>
<BOS>In patients who are older than 65 years or who have a history of blood clots, the risk of stroke or heart attack is higher.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients also have an increased risk of acute myeloid leukemia or primary myelofibrosis.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of chronic myelomonocytic leukemia include fever, weight loss, and feeling very tired.<EOS>
<BOS>These and other signs and symptoms may be caused by CMML or by other conditions.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your doctor if you have any of the following: Fever for no known reason.<EOS>
<BOS>Infection.<EOS>
<BOS>Feeling very tired.<EOS>
<BOS>Weight loss for no known reason.<EOS>
<BOS>Easy bruising or bleeding.<EOS>
<BOS>Pain or a feeling of fullness below the ribs.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery).<EOS>
<BOS>The prognosis (chance of recovery) for atypical CML depends on the number of red blood cells and platelets in the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points There are different types of treatment for patients with endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Five types of standard treatment are used: Surgery Radiation therapy Chemotherapy Hormone therapy Targeted therapy New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of treatment for patients with endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Different types of treatment are available for patients with endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some treatments are standard (the currently used treatment), and some are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>A treatment clinical trial is a research study meant to help improve current treatments or obtain information on new treatments for patients with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>When clinical trials show that a new treatment is better than the standard treatment, the new treatment may become the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials are open only to patients who have not started treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Five types of standard treatment are used: Surgery Surgery (removing the cancer in an operation) is the most common treatment for endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following surgical procedures may be used: Total hysterectomy: Surgery to remove the uterus, including the cervix.<EOS>
<BOS>If the uterus and cervix are taken out through the vagina, the operation is called a vaginal hysterectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>If the uterus and cervix are taken out through a large incision (cut) in the abdomen, the operation is called a total abdominal hysterectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>If the uterus and cervix are taken out through a small incision (cut) in the abdomen using a laparoscope, the operation is called a total laparoscopic hysterectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>Bilateral salpingooophorectomy: Surgery to remove both ovaries and both fallopian tubes.<EOS>
<BOS>Radical hysterectomy: Surgery to remove the uterus, cervix, and part of the vagina.<EOS>
<BOS>The ovaries, fallopian tubes, or nearby lymph nodes may also be removed.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph node dissection: A surgical procedure in which the lymph nodes are removed from the pelvic area and a sample of tissue is checked under a microscope for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called lymphadenectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>Even if the doctor removes all the cancer that can be seen at the time of the surgery, some patients may be given radiation therapy or hormone treatment after surgery to kill any cancer cells that are left.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment given after the surgery, to lower the risk that the cancer will come back, is called adjuvant therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy Radiation therapy is a cancer treatment that uses highenergy xrays or other types of radiation to kill cancer cells or keep them from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two types of radiation therapy: External radiation therapy uses a machine outside the body to send radiation toward the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy uses a radioactive substance sealed in needles, seeds, wires, or catheters that are placed directly into or near the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the radiation therapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>External and internal radiation therapy are used to treat endometrial cancer, and may also be used as palliative therapy to relieve symptoms and improve quality of life.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy Chemotherapy is a cancer treatment that uses drugs to stop the growth of cancer cells, either by killing the cells or by stopping the cells from dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is taken by mouth or injected into a vein or muscle, the drugs enter the bloodstream and can reach cancer cells throughout the body (systemic chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is placed directly into the cerebrospinal fluid, an organ, or a body cavity such as the abdomen, the drugs mainly affect cancer cells in those areas (regional chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>The way the chemotherapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy Hormone therapy is a cancer treatment that removes hormones or blocks their action and stops cancer cells from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormones are substances made by glands in the body and circulated in the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>Some hormones can cause certain cancers to grow.<EOS>
<BOS>If tests show that the cancer cells have places where hormones can attach (receptors), drugs, surgery, or radiation therapy is used to reduce the production of hormones or block them from working.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy Targeted therapy is a type of treatment that uses drugs or other substances to identify and attack specific cancer cells without harming normal cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Monoclonal antibodies, mTOR inhibitors, and signal transduction inhibitors are three types of targeted therapy used to treat endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Monoclonal antibody therapy is a cancer treatment that uses antibodies made in the laboratory from a single type of immune system cell.<EOS>
<BOS>These antibodies can identify substances on cancer cells or normal substances that may help cancer cells grow.<EOS>
<BOS>The antibodies attach to the substances and kill the cancer cells, block their growth, or keep them from spreading.<EOS>
<BOS>Monoclonal antibodies are given by infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>They may be used alone or to carry drugs, toxins, or radioactive material directly to cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Bevacizumab is used to treat stage III, stage IV, and recurrent endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>mTOR inhibitors block a protein called mTOR, which helps control cell division.<EOS>
<BOS>mTOR inhibitors may keep cancer cells from growing and prevent the growth of new blood vessels that tumors need to grow.<EOS>
<BOS>Everolimus and ridaforalimus are used to treat stage III, stage IV, and recurrent endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Signal transduction inhibitors block signals that are passed from one molecule to another inside a cell.<EOS>
<BOS>Blocking these signals may kill cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Metformin is being studied to treat stage III, stage IV, and recurrent endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests that were done to diagnose the cancer or to find out the stage of the cancer may be repeated.<EOS>
<BOS>Some tests will be repeated in order to see how well the treatment is working.<EOS>
<BOS>Decisions about whether to continue, change, or stop treatment may be based on the results of these tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests will continue to be done from time to time after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of these tests can show if your condition has changed or if the cancer has recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>These tests are sometimes called followup tests or checkups.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options by Stage Stage I and Stage II Endometrial Cancer Lowrisk endometrial cancer (grade 1 or grade 2) Treatment of lowrisk stage I endometrial cancer and stage II endometrial cancer may include the following: Surgery (total hysterectomy and bilateral salpingooophorectomy).<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph nodes in the pelvis and abdomen may also be removed and viewed under a microscope to check for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery (total hysterectomy and bilateral salpingooophorectomy, with or without removal of lymph nodes in the pelvis and abdomen) followed by internal radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>In certain cases, external radiation therapy to the pelvis may be used in place of internal radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy alone for patients who cannot have surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new chemotherapy regimen.<EOS>
<BOS>If cancer has spread to the cervix, a radical hysterectomy with bilateral salpingooophorectomy may be done.<EOS>
<BOS>Highrisk endometrial cancer (grade 3) Treatment of highrisk stage I endometrial cancer and stage II endometrial cancer may include the following: Surgery (radical hysterectomy and bilateral salpingooophorectomy).<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph nodes in the pelvis and abdomen may also be removed and viewed under a microscope to check for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery (radical hysterectomy and bilateral salpingooophorectomy) followed by chemotherapy and sometimes radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new chemotherapy regimen.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage I endometrial carcinoma and stage II endometrial carcinoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III, Stage IV, and Recurrent Endometrial Cancer Treatment of stage III endometrial cancer, stage IV endometrial cancer, and recurrent endometrial cancer may include the following: Surgery (radical hysterectomy and removal of lymph nodes in the pelvis so they can be viewed under a microscope to check for cancer cells) followed by adjuvant chemotherapy and/or radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy and internal and external radiation therapy for patients who cannot have surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy for patients who cannot have surgery or radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy with mTOR inhibitors (everolimus or ridaforolimus) or a monoclonal antibody (bevacizumab).<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new treatment regimen that may include combination chemotherapy, targeted therapy, such as an mTOR inhibitor (everolimus) or signal transduction inhibitor (metformin), and/or hormone therapy, for patients with advanced or recurrent endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage III endometrial carcinoma, stage IV endometrial carcinoma and recurrent endometrial carcinoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>

<BOS>Some clinical trials are open only to patients who have not started treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Children with extracranial germ cell tumors should have their treatment planned by a team of health care providers who are experts in treating cancer in children.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment will be overseen by a pediatric oncologist, a doctor who specializes in treating children with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The pediatric oncologist works with other health care providers who are experts in treating children with extracranial germ cell tumors and who specialize in certain areas of medicine.<EOS>
<BOS>These may include the following specialists: Pediatrician.<EOS>
<BOS>Pediatric surgeon.<EOS>
<BOS>Pediatric hematologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation oncologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Endocrinologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Pediatric nurse specialist.<EOS>
<BOS>Rehabilitation specialist.<EOS>
<BOS>Child life professional.<EOS>
<BOS>Psychologist.<EOS>
<BOS>Social worker.<EOS>
<BOS>Geneticist.<EOS>
<BOS>Some cancer treatments cause side effects months or years after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>Side effects from cancer treatment that begin during or after treatment and continue for months or years are called late effects.<EOS>
<BOS>Late effects of cancer treatment may include the following: Physical problems.<EOS>
<BOS>Changes in mood, feelings, thinking, learning, or memory.<EOS>
<BOS>Second cancers (new types of cancer).<EOS>
<BOS>For example, late effects of surgery to remove tumors in the sacrum or coccyx include constipation, loss of bowel and bladder control, and scars.<EOS>
<BOS>Some late effects may be treated or controlled.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to talk with your child's doctors about the effects cancer treatment can have on your child.<EOS>
<BOS>(See the PDQ summary on Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer for more information).<EOS>
<BOS>Three types of standard treatment are used: Surgery Surgery to completely remove the tumor is done whenever possible.<EOS>
<BOS>If the tumor is very large, chemotherapy may be given first, to make the tumor smaller and decrease the amount of tissue that needs to be removed during surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>A goal of surgery is to keep reproductive function.<EOS>
<BOS>The following types of surgery may be used: Resection: Surgery to remove tissue or part or all of an organ.<EOS>
<BOS>Radical inguinal orchiectomy: Surgery to remove one or both testicles through an incision (cut) in the groin.<EOS>
<BOS>Unilateral salpingooophorectomy: Surgery to remove one ovary and one fallopian tube on the same side.<EOS>
<BOS>Even if the doctor removes all the cancer that can be seen at the time of the surgery, some patients may be given chemotherapy after surgery to kill any cancer cells that are left.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment given after the surgery, to lower the risk that the cancer will come back, is called adjuvant therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Observation Observation is closely monitoring a patients condition without giving any treatment until signs or symptoms appear or change.<EOS>
<BOS>For childhood extracranial germ cell tumors, this includes physical exams, imaging tests, and tumor marker tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy Chemotherapy is a cancer treatment that uses drugs to stop the growth of cancer cells, either by killing the cells or by stopping them from dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is taken by mouth or injected into a vein or muscle, the drugs enter the bloodstream and can reach cancer cells throughout the body (systemic chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is placed directly into the cerebrospinal fluid, an organ, or a body cavity such as the abdomen, the drugs mainly affect cancer cells in those areas (regional chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy is treatment using more than one anticancer drug.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the chemotherapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary section describes treatments that are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>It may not mention every new treatment being studied.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant is a way of giving high doses of chemotherapy and replacing blood forming cells destroyed by the cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Stem cells (immature blood cells) are removed from the blood or bone marrow of the patient or a donor and are frozen and stored.<EOS>
<BOS>After the chemotherapy is completed, the stored stem cells are thawed and given back to the patient through an infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>These reinfused stem cells grow into (and restore) the body's blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy Radiation therapy is a cancer treatment that uses highenergy xrays or other types of radiation to kill cancer cells or keep them from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two types of radiation therapy: External radiation therapy uses a machine outside the body to send radiation toward the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy uses a radioactive substance sealed in needles, seeds, wires, or catheters that are placed directly into or near the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the radiation therapy is given depends on the type of cancer and whether it has come back.<EOS>
<BOS>External radiation therapy is being studied for the treatment of childhood extracranial germ cell tumors that have come back.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests that were done to diagnose the cancer or to find out the stage of the cancer may be repeated.<EOS>
<BOS>Some tests will be repeated in order to see how well the treatment is working.<EOS>
<BOS>Decisions about whether to continue, change, or stop treatment may be based on the results of these tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests will continue to be done from time to time after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of these tests can show if your child's condition has changed or if the cancer has recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>These tests are sometimes called followup tests or checkups.<EOS>
<BOS>For childhood extracranial germ cell tumors, alphafetoprotein (AFP) tests and betahuman chorionic gonadotropin (hCG) tests are done to see if treatment is working.<EOS>
<BOS>Continued high levels of AFP or hCG may mean the cancer is still growing.<EOS>
<BOS>For at least 3 years after surgery, followup will include regular physical exams, imaging tests, and tumor marker tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options for Childhood Extracranial Germ Cell Tumors Mature and Immature Teratomas Treatment of mature teratomas that are not in the sacrum or coccyx (bottom part of the spine) includes the following: Surgery to remove the tumor followed by observation.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of immature teratomas that are not in the sacrum or coccyx includes the following: Surgery to remove the tumor followed by observation for stage I tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove the tumor for stage IIIV tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of immature teratomas that are in the sacrum or coccyx includes the following: Surgery (removal of the sacrum and coccyx) followed by observation.<EOS>
<BOS>Sometimes a mature or immature teratoma also has malignant cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The teratoma and malignant cells may need to be treated differently.<EOS>
<BOS>Regular followup exams with imaging tests and the alphafetoprotein (AFP) tumor marker test will be done for at least 3 years.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with childhood teratoma.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your child's doctor about clinical trials that may be right for your child.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant Gonadal Germ Cell Tumors Malignant Testicular Germ Cell Tumors Treatment of malignant testicular germ cell tumors may include the following: For boys younger than 15 years: Surgery (radical inguinal orchiectomy) followed by observation for stage I tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery (radical inguinal orchiectomy) followed by combination chemotherapy for stage IIIV tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>A second surgery may be done to remove any tumor remaining after chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>For boys 15 years and older: Malignant testicular germ cell tumors in boys 15 years and older are treated differently than they are in young boys.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery may include removal of lymph nodes in the abdomen.<EOS>
<BOS>(See the PDQ summary on Testicular Cancer Treatment for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>) Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with childhood malignant testicular germ cell tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
