<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant Ovarian Germ Cell Tumors Dysgerminomas Treatment of stage I dysgerminomas in young girls may include the following: Surgery (unilateral salpingooophorectomy) followed by observation.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy may be given if the tumor comes back.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of stages IIIV dysgerminomas in young girls may include the following: Surgery (unilateral salpingooophorectomy) followed by combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy to shrink the tumor, followed by surgery (unilateral salpingooophorectomy).<EOS>
<BOS>Nongerminomas Treatment of stage I nongerminomas in young girls may include the following: Surgery followed by observation.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery followed by combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of stages IIIV nongerminomas in young girls may include the following: Surgery followed by combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>A second surgery may be done to remove any remaining cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Biopsy followed by combination chemotherapy to shrink the tumor and sometimes surgery for tumors that cannot be removed by surgery when cancer is diagnosed.<EOS>
<BOS>The treatment for adolescents and young adults with ovarian germ cell tumor is much like the treatment for adults.<EOS>
<BOS>(See the PDQ treatment summary on Ovarian Germ Cell Tumors for more information.<EOS>

<BOS>Malignant Extragonadal Extracranial Germ Cell Tumors Treatment of childhood malignant extragonadal extracranial germ cell tumors may include the following: Combination chemotherapy to shrink the tumor followed by surgery to remove the sacrum and coccyx (bottom part of the spine) for tumors that are in the sacrum or coccyx.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy to shrink the tumor followed by surgery to remove tumors that are in the mediastinum.<EOS>
<BOS>Biopsy followed by combination chemotherapy to shrink the tumor and surgery to remove tumors that are in the abdomen.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove the tumor followed by combination chemotherapy for tumors of the head and neck.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of malignant extragonadal extracranial germ cell tumors in places not already described includes the following: Surgery followed by combination chemotherapy.<EOS>

<BOS>Recurrent Childhood Malignant Extracranial Germ Cell Tumors There is no standard treatment for recurrent childhood malignant extracranial germ cell tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment depends on the following: The type of treatment given when the cancer was diagnosed.<EOS>
<BOS>How the tumor responded to the initial treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment is usually within a clinical trial and may include the following: Surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery followed by combination chemotherapy, for most malignant extracranial germ cell tumors including immature teratomas, malignant testicular germ cell tumors, and malignant ovarian germ cell tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery for tumors that come back in the sacrum or coccyx (bottom part of the spine), if surgery to remove the sacrum and coccyx was not done when the cancer was diagnosed.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy may be given before surgery, to shrink the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>If any tumor remains after surgery, radiation therapy may also be given.<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy for stage I malignant testicular germ cell tumors and stage I ovarian dysgerminomas.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy and stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy followed by surgery to remove the tumor in the brain for cancer that has spread to the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of combination chemotherapy alone compared with highdose chemotherapy followed by stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with recurrent childhood malignant germ cell tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your child's doctor about clinical trials that may be right for your child.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Extragonadal germ cell tumors form from developing sperm or egg cells that travel from the gonads to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Age and gender can affect the risk of extragonadal germ cell tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of extragonadal germ cell tumors include breathing problems and chest pain.<EOS>
<BOS>Imaging and blood tests are used to detect (find) and diagnose extragonadal germ cell tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>Extragonadal germ cell tumors form from developing sperm or egg cells that travel from the gonads to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>" Extragonadal" means outside of the gonads (sex organs).<EOS>
<BOS>When cells that are meant to form sperm in the testicles or eggs in the ovaries travel to other parts of the body, they may grow into extragonadal germ cell tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>These tumors may begin to grow anywhere in the body but usually begin in organs such as the pineal gland in the brain, in the mediastinum (area between the lungs), or in the retroperitoneum (the back wall of the abdomen).<EOS>
<BOS>Extragonadal germ cell tumors can be benign (noncancer) or malignant (cancer).<EOS>
<BOS>Benign extragonadal germ cell tumors are called benign teratomas.<EOS>
<BOS>These are more common than malignant extragonadal germ cell tumors and often are very large.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant extragonadal germ cell tumors are divided into two types, nonseminoma and seminoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Nonseminomas tend to grow and spread more quickly than seminomas.<EOS>
<BOS>They usually are large and cause signs and symptoms.<EOS>
<BOS>If untreated, malignant extragonadal germ cell tumors may spread to the lungs, lymph nodes, bones, liver, or other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>For information about germ cell tumors in the ovaries and testicles, see the following PDQ summaries: Ovarian Germ Cell Tumors Treatment Testicular Cancer Treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of extragonadal germ cell tumors include breathing problems and chest pain.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant extragonadal germ cell tumors may cause signs and symptoms as they grow into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Other conditions may cause the same signs and symptoms.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your doctor if you have any of the following: Chest pain.<EOS>
<BOS>Breathing problems.<EOS>
<BOS>Cough.<EOS>
<BOS>Fever.<EOS>
<BOS>Headache.<EOS>
<BOS>Change in bowel habits.<EOS>
<BOS>Feeling very tired.<EOS>
<BOS>Trouble walking.<EOS>
<BOS>Trouble in seeing or moving the eyes.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After intraocular melanoma has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The following sizes are used to describe intraocular melanoma: Small Medium Large There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two staging systems for intraocular melanoma.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for intraocular melanoma of the iris: Stage I Stage II Stage III Stage IV The following stages are used for intraocular melanoma of the ciliary body and choroid: Stage I Stage II Stage III Stage IV After intraocular melanoma has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out if cancer has spread to other parts of the body is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>The information gathered from the staging process determines the stage of the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to know the stage in order to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used in the staging process: Blood chemistry studies : A procedure in which a blood sample is checked to measure the amounts of certain substances released into the blood by organs and tissues in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>An unusual (higher or lower than normal) amount of a substance can be a sign of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Liver function tests : A procedure in which a blood sample is checked to measure the amounts of certain substances released into the blood by the liver.<EOS>
<BOS>A higher than normal amount of a substance can be a sign the cancer has spread to the liver.<EOS>
<BOS>Ultrasound exam: A procedure in which highenergy sound waves (ultrasound) are bounced off internal tissues or organs, such as the liver, and make echoes.<EOS>
<BOS>The echoes form a picture of body tissues called a sonogram.<EOS>
<BOS>Chest xray : An xray of the organs and bones inside the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>An xray is a type of energy beam that can go through the body and onto film, making a picture of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>MRI (magnetic resonance imaging): A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, such as the chest, abdomen, or pelvis, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>PET scan (positron emission tomography scan): A procedure to find malignant tumor cells in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A very small amount of radioactive glucose (sugar) is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The PET scanner rotates around the body and makes a picture of where glucose is being used in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant tumor cells show up brighter in the picture because they are more active and take up more glucose than normal cells do.<EOS>
<BOS>Sometimes a PET scan and a CT scan are done at the same time.<EOS>
<BOS>If there is any cancer, this increases the chance that it will be found.<EOS>
<BOS>The following sizes are used to describe intraocular melanoma: Small The tumor is 5 to 16 millimeters in diameter and from 1 to 3 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>Medium The tumor is 16 millimeters or smaller in diameter and from 3.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 8 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>Large The tumor is: more than 8 millimeters thick and any diameter; or at least 2 millimeters thick and more than 16 millimeters in diameter.<EOS>
<BOS>Though most intraocular melanoma tumors are raised, some are flat.<EOS>
<BOS>These diffuse tumors grow widely across the uvea.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>When cancer spreads to another part of the body, it is called metastasis.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells break away from where they began (the primary tumor) and travel through the lymph system or blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the lymph system, travels through the lymph vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the blood, travels through the blood vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The metastatic tumor is the same type of cancer as the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, if intraocular melanoma spreads to the liver, the cancer cells in the liver are actually intraocular melanoma cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The disease is metastatic intraocular melanoma, not liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two staging systems for intraocular melanoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Intraocular melanoma has two staging systems.<EOS>
<BOS>The staging system used depends on where in the eye the cancer first formed: Iris.<EOS>
<BOS>Ciliary body and choroid.<EOS>
<BOS>If intraocular melanoma spreads to the optic nerve or nearby tissue of the eye socket, it is called extraocular extension.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for intraocular melanoma of the iris: Stage I In stage I, the tumor is in the iris only and is not more than one fourth the size of the iris.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II Stage II is divided into stages IIA and IIB.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIA, the tumor: is in the iris only and is more than one fourth the size of the iris; or is in the iris only and has caused glaucoma; or has spread next to and/or into the ciliary body, choroid, or both.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor has caused glaucoma.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIB, the tumor has spread next to and/or into the ciliary body, choroid, or both, and has also spread into the sclera.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor has caused glaucoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III Stage III is divided into stages IIIA and IIIB.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIIA, the tumor has spread through the sclera to the outside of the eyeball.<EOS>
<BOS>The part of the tumor outside the eyeball is not more than 5 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIIB, the tumor has spread through the sclera to the outside of the eyeball.<EOS>
<BOS>The part of the tumor outside the eyeball is more than 5 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV In stage IV, the tumor may be any size and has spread: to nearby lymph nodes; or to other parts of the body, such as the liver, lung, or bone, or to areas under the skin.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for intraocular melanoma of the ciliary body and choroid: Intraocular melanoma of the ciliary body and choroid is grouped into four size categories.<EOS>
<BOS>The category depends on how wide and thick the tumor is.<EOS>
<BOS>Category 1 tumors are the smallest and category 4 tumors are the biggest.<EOS>
<BOS>Category 1: The tumor is not more than 12 millimeters wide and not more than 3 millimeters thick; or the tumor is not more than 9 millimeters wide and 3.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 6 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>Category 2: The tumor is 12.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 18 millimeters wide and not more than 3 millimeters thick; or the tumor is 9.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 15 millimeters wide and 3.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 6 millimeters thick; or the tumor is not more than 12 millimeters wide and 6.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 9 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>Category 3: The tumor is 15.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 18 millimeters wide and 3.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 6 millimeters thick; or the tumor is 12.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 18 millimeters wide and 6.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 9 millimeters thick; or the tumor is 3.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 18 millimeters wide and 9.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 12 millimeters thick; or the tumor is 9.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 15 millimeters wide and 12.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 15 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>Category 4: The tumor is more than 18 millimeters wide and may be any thickness; or the tumor is 15.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 18 millimeters wide and more than 12 millimeters thick; or the tumor is 12.<EOS>
<BOS>1 to 15 millimeters wide and more than 15 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I In stage I, the tumor is size category 1 and is in the choroid only.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II Stage II is divided into stages IIA and IIB.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIA, the tumor: is size category 1 and has spread to the ciliary body; or is size category 1 and has spread through the sclera to the outside of the eyeball.<EOS>
<BOS>The part of the tumor outside the eyeball is not more than 5 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor may have spread to the ciliary body; or is size category 2 and is in the choroid only.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIB, the tumor: is size category 2 and has spread to the ciliary body; or is size category 3 and is in the choroid only.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III Stage III is divided into stages IIIA, IIIB, and IIIC.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIIA, the tumor: is size category 2 and has spread through the sclera to the outside of the eyeball.<EOS>
<BOS>The part of the tumor outside the eyeball is not more than 5 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor may have spread to the ciliary body; or is size category 3 and has spread to the ciliary body; or is size category 3 and has spread through the sclera to the outside of the eyeball.<EOS>
<BOS>The part of the tumor outside the eyeball is not more than 5 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor has not spread to the ciliary body; or is size category 4 and is in the choroid only.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIIB, the tumor: is size category 3 and has spread through the sclera to the outside of the eyeball.<EOS>
<BOS>The part of the tumor outside the eyeball is not more than 5 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor has spread to the ciliary body; or is size category 4 and has spread to the ciliary body; or is size category 4 and has spread through the sclera to the outside of the eyeball.<EOS>
<BOS>The part of the tumor outside the eyeball is not more than 5 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor has not spread to the ciliary body.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIIC, the tumor: is size category 4 and has spread through the sclera to the outside of the eyeball.<EOS>
<BOS>The part of the tumor outside the eyeball is not more than 5 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor has spread to the ciliary body; or may be any size and has spread through the sclera to the outside of the eyeball.<EOS>
<BOS>The part of the tumor outside the eyeball is more than 5 millimeters thick.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor has not spread to the ciliary body.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV In stage IV, the tumor may be any size and has spread: to nearby lymph nodes; or to other parts of the body, such as the liver, lung, or bone, or to areas under the skin.<EOS>
<BOS>The prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options depend on the following: Whether the cancer is in one or both eyes.<EOS>
<BOS>The size and number of tumors.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the tumor has spread to the area around the eye, to the brain, or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether there are symptoms at the time of diagnosis, for trilateral retinoblastoma.<EOS>
<BOS>The age of the child.<EOS>
<BOS>How likely it is that vision can be saved in one or both eyes.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether a second type of cancer has formed.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI Web site.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the medical research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for disease are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way diseases will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose disease has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop a disease from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's clinical trials database.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Gallbladder cancer is a disease in which malignant (cancer) cells form in the tissues of the gallbladder.<EOS>
<BOS>Being female can increase the risk of developing gallbladder cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of gallbladder cancer include jaundice, fever, and pain.<EOS>
<BOS>Gallbladder cancer is difficult to detect (find) and diagnose early.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the gallbladder and nearby organs are used to detect (find), diagnose, and stage gallbladder cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect the prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>Gallbladder cancer is a disease in which malignant (cancer) cells form in the tissues of the gallbladder.<EOS>
<BOS>Gallbladder cancer is a rare disease in which malignant (cancer) cells are found in the tissues of the gallbladder.<EOS>
<BOS>The gallbladder is a pearshaped organ that lies just under the liver in the upper abdomen.<EOS>
<BOS>The gallbladder stores bile, a fluid made by the liver to digest fat.<EOS>
<BOS>When food is being broken down in the stomach and intestines, bile is released from the gallbladder through a tube called the common bile duct, which connects the gallbladder and liver to the first part of the small intestine.<EOS>
<BOS>The wall of the gallbladder has 3 main layers of tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Mucosal (inner) layer.<EOS>
<BOS>Muscularis (middle, muscle) layer.<EOS>
<BOS>Serosal (outer) layer.<EOS>
<BOS>Between these layers is supporting connective tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Primary gallbladder cancer starts in the inner layer and spreads through the outer layers as it grows.<EOS>
<BOS>Gallbladder cancer is difficult to detect (find) and diagnose early.<EOS>
<BOS>Gallbladder cancer is difficult to detect and diagnose for the following reasons: There are no signs or symptoms in the early stages of gallbladder cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The symptoms of gallbladder cancer, when present, are like the symptoms of many other illnesses.<EOS>
<BOS>The gallbladder is hidden behind the liver.<EOS>
<BOS>Gallbladder cancer is sometimes found when the gallbladder is removed for other reasons.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients with gallstones rarely develop gallbladder cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of gallbladder cancer include jaundice, fever, and pain.<EOS>
<BOS>These and other signs and symptoms may be caused by gallbladder cancer or by other conditions.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your doctor if you have any of the following: Jaundice (yellowing of the skin and whites of the eyes).<EOS>
<BOS>Pain above the stomach.<EOS>
<BOS>Fever.<EOS>
<BOS>Nausea and vomiting.<EOS>
<BOS>Bloating.<EOS>
<BOS>Lumps in the abdomen.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After gestational trophoblastic neoplasia has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer has spread from where it started to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There is no staging system for hydatidiform moles.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for GTN: Stage I Stage II Stage III Stage IV The treatment of gestational trophoblastic neoplasia is based on the type of disease, stage, or risk group.<EOS>
<BOS>After gestational trophoblastic neoplasia has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer has spread from where it started to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out the extent or spread of cancer is called staging, The information gathered from the staging process helps determine the stage of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>For GTN, stage is one of the factors used to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be done to help find out the stage of the disease: Chest xray : An xray of the organs and bones inside the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>An xray is a type of energy beam that can go through the body onto film, making pictures of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>MRI (magnetic resonance imaging) with gadolinium : A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, such as brain and spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>A substance called gadolinium is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The gadolinium collects around the cancer cells so they show up brighter in the picture.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>Lumbar puncture : A procedure used to collect cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) from the spinal column.<EOS>
<BOS>This is done by placing a needle between two bones in the spine and into the CSF around the spinal cord and removing a sample of the fluid.<EOS>
<BOS>The sample of CSF is checked under a microscope for signs that the cancer has spread to the brain and spinal cord.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called an LP or spinal tap.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>When cancer spreads to another part of the body, it is called metastasis.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells break away from where they began (the primary tumor) and travel through the lymph system or blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the lymph system, travels through the lymph vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the blood, travels through the blood vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The metastatic tumor is the same type of cancer as the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, if choriocarcinoma spreads to the lung, the cancer cells in the lung are actually choriocarcinoma cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The disease is metastatic choriocarcinoma, not lung cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>There is no staging system for hydatidiform moles.<EOS>
<BOS>Hydatidiform moles (HM) are found in the uterus only and do not spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for GTN: Stage I In stage I, the tumor is in the uterus only.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II In stage II, cancer has spread outside of the uterus to the ovary, fallopian tube, vagina, and/or the ligaments that support the uterus.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III In stage III, cancer has spread to the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV In stage IV, cancer has spread to distant parts of the body other than the lungs.<EOS>
<BOS>The treatment of gestational trophoblastic neoplasia is based on the type of disease, stage, or risk group.<EOS>
<BOS>Invasive moles and choriocarcinomas are treated based on risk groups.<EOS>
<BOS>The stage of the invasive mole or choriocarcinoma is one factor used to determine risk group.<EOS>
<BOS>Other factors include the following: The age of the patient when the diagnosis is made.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the GTN occurred after a molar pregnancy, miscarriage, or normal pregnancy.<EOS>
<BOS>How soon the tumor was diagnosed after the pregnancy began.<EOS>
<BOS>The level of beta human chorionic gonadotropin (hCG) in the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The size of the largest tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Where the tumor has spread to and the number of tumors in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>How many chemotherapy drugs the tumor has been treated with (for recurrent or resistant tumors).<EOS>
<BOS>There are two risk groups for invasive moles and choriocarcinomas: low risk and high risk.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients with lowrisk disease usually receive less aggressive treatment than patients with highrisk disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Placentalsite trophoblastic tumor (PSTT) and epithelioid trophoblastic tumor (ETT) treatments depend on the stage of disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the throat and neck are used to help detect (find), diagnose, and stage laryngeal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used: Physical exam of the throat and neck: An exam to check the throat and neck for abnormal areas.<EOS>
<BOS>The doctor will feel the inside of the mouth with a gloved finger and examine the mouth and throat with a small longhandled mirror and light.<EOS>
<BOS>This will include checking the insides of the cheeks and lips; the gums; the back, roof, and floor of the mouth; the top, bottom, and sides of the tongue; and the throat.<EOS>
<BOS>The neck will be felt for swollen lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>A history of the patients health habits and past illnesses and medical treatments will also be taken.<EOS>
<BOS>Biopsy : The removal of cells or tissues so they can be viewed under a microscope by a pathologist to check for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The sample of tissue may be removed during one of the following procedures: Laryngoscopy : A procedure to look at the larynx (voice box) for abnormal areas.<EOS>
<BOS>A mirror or a laryngoscope (a thin, tubelike instrument with a light and a lens for viewing) is inserted through the mouth to see the larynx.<EOS>
<BOS>A special tool on the laryngoscope may be used to remove samples of tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Endoscopy : A procedure to look at organs and tissues inside the body, such as the throat, esophagus, and trachea to check for abnormal areas.<EOS>
<BOS>An endoscope (a thin, lighted tube with a light and a lens for viewing) is inserted through an opening in the body, such as the mouth.<EOS>
<BOS>A special tool on the endoscope may be used to remove samples of tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>MRI (magnetic resonance imaging): A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>PET scan (positron emission tomography scan): A procedure to find malignant tumor cells in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A small amount of radioactive glucose (sugar) is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The PET scanner rotates around the body and makes a picture of where glucose is being used in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant tumor cells show up brighter in the picture because they are more active and take up more glucose than normal cells do.<EOS>
<BOS>Bone scan : A procedure to check if there are rapidly dividing cells, such as cancer cells, in the bone.<EOS>
<BOS>A very small amount of radioactive material is injected into a vein and travels through the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>The radioactive material collects in the bones and is detected by a scanner.<EOS>
<BOS>Barium swallow : A series of xrays of the esophagus and stomach.<EOS>
<BOS>The patient drinks a liquid that contains barium (a silverwhite metallic compound).<EOS>
<BOS>The liquid coats the esophagus and stomach, and xrays are taken.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called an upper GI series.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of metastatic squamous neck cancer with occult primary include a lump or pain in the neck or throat.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your doctor if you have a lump or pain in your neck or throat that doesn't go away.<EOS>
<BOS>These and other signs and symptoms may be caused by metastatic squamous neck cancer with occult primary.<EOS>
<BOS>Other conditions may cause the same signs and symptoms.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs of salivary gland cancer include a lump or trouble swallowing.<EOS>
<BOS>Salivary gland cancer may not cause any symptoms.<EOS>
<BOS>It may be found during a regular dental checkup or physical exam.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms may be caused by salivary gland cancer or by other conditions.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your doctor if you have any of the following: A lump (usually painless) in the area of the ear, cheek, jaw, lip, or inside the mouth.<EOS>
<BOS>Fluid draining from the ear.<EOS>
<BOS>Trouble swallowing or opening the mouth widely.<EOS>
<BOS>Numbness or weakness in the face.<EOS>
<BOS>Pain in the face that does not go away.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Avoiding risk factors and increasing protective factors may help prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following are risk factors for oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer: Tobacco use Alcohol use Tobacco and alcohol use Betel quid or gutka chewing Personal history of head and neck cancer The following is a risk factor for oropharyngeal cancer: HPV infection The following is a protective factor for oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer: Quitting smoking It is not clear whether avoiding certain risk factors will decrease the risk of oral cavity cancer or oropharyngeal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer prevention clinical trials are used to study ways to prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>New ways to prevent oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Avoiding risk factors and increasing protective factors may help prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Avoiding cancer risk factors may help prevent certain cancers.<EOS>
<BOS>Risk factors include smoking, being overweight, and not getting enough exercise.<EOS>
<BOS>Increasing protective factors such as quitting smoking and exercising may also help prevent some cancers.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk to your doctor or other health care professional about how you might lower your risk of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer are two different diseases, but they have some risk factors in common.<EOS>
<BOS>The following are risk factors for oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer: Tobacco use Using tobacco is the most common cause of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of these cancers is about 5 to 10 times higher for current smokers than for people who have never smoked.<EOS>
<BOS>The use of all types of tobacco, including cigarettes, pipes, cigars, and smokeless tobacco (snuff and chewing tobacco) can cause cancer of the oral cavity and oropharynx.<EOS>
<BOS>For cigarette smokers, the risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer increases with the number of cigarettes smoked per day.<EOS>
<BOS>Alcohol use Using alcohol is also an important risk factor for oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer increases with the number of alcoholic drinks consumed per day.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer is about twice as high in people who have 3 to 4 alcoholic drinks per day and 5 times higher in people who have 5 or more alcoholic drinks per day compared with those who don't drink alcohol.<EOS>
<BOS>Tobacco and alcohol use The risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer is 2 to 3 times higher in people who use both tobacco and alcohol than it is in people who use only tobacco or only alcohol.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer is about 35 times higher in people who smoke 2 or more packs of cigarettes per day and have more than 4 alcoholic drinks per day than it is in people who have never smoked cigarettes or consumed alcohol.<EOS>
<BOS>Betel quid or gutka chewing Chewing betel quid or gutka (betel quid mixed with tobacco) has been shown to increase the risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Betel quid contains areca nut, which is a cancercausing substance.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer increases with how long and how often betel quid or gutka are chewed.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk for oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer is higher when chewing gutka than when chewing betel quid alone.<EOS>
<BOS>Betel quid and gutka chewing is common in many countries in South Asia and Southeast Asia, including China and India.<EOS>
<BOS>Personal history of head and neck cancer A personal history of head and neck cancer increases the risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following is a risk factor for oropharyngeal cancer: HPV infection Being infected with certain types of HPV, especially HPV type 16, increases the risk of oropharyngeal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>HPV infection is spread mainly through sexual contact.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of oropharyngeal cancer is about 15 times higher in people who have oral HPV 16 infection compared with people who do not have oral HPV 16 infection.<EOS>
<BOS>The following is a protective factor for oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer: Quitting smoking Studies have shown that when people stop smoking cigarettes, their risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer decreases by one half (50%) within 5 years.<EOS>
<BOS>Within 20 years of quitting, their risk of oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer is the same as for a person who never smoked cigarettes.<EOS>
<BOS>It is not clear whether avoiding certain risk factors will decrease the risk of oral cavity cancer or oropharyngeal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>It has not been proven that stopping alcohol use will decrease the risk of oral cavity cancer or oropharyngeal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Getting an HPV vaccination greatly lessens the risk of oral HPV infection.<EOS>
<BOS>It is not yet known whether getting an HPV vaccination at any age will decrease the risk of oropharyngeal cancer from HPV infection.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer prevention clinical trials are used to study ways to prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer prevention clinical trials are used to study ways to lower the risk of certain types of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some cancer prevention trials are done with healthy people who have not had cancer but who have an increased risk for cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Other prevention trials are done with people who have had cancer and are trying to prevent another cancer of the same type or to lower their chance of developing a new type of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials are done with healthy volunteers who are not known to have any risk factors for cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The purpose of some cancer prevention clinical trials is to find out whether actions people take can prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>These may include eating fruits and vegetables, exercising, quitting smoking, or taking certain medicines, vitamins, minerals, or food supplements.<EOS>
<BOS>New ways to prevent oral cavity cancer and oropharyngeal cancer are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials can be found in the Clinical Trials section of the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Check NCI's list of cancer clinical trials for oral cavity cancer prevention trials and oropharyngeal cancer prevention trials that are now accepting patients.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Tests and procedures to stage pancreatic cancer are usually done at the same time as diagnosis.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for pancreatic cancer: Stage 0 (Carcinoma in Situ) Stage I Stage II Stage III Stage IV Tests and procedures to stage pancreatic cancer are usually done at the same time as diagnosis.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out if cancer has spread within the pancreas or to other parts of the body is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>The information gathered from the staging process determines the stage of the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to know the stage of the disease in order to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of some of the tests used to diagnose pancreatic cancer are often also used to stage the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>See the General Information section for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>When cancer spreads to another part of the body, it is called metastasis.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells break away from where they began (the primary tumor) and travel through the lymph system or blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the lymph system, travels through the lymph vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the blood, travels through the blood vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The metastatic tumor is the same type of cancer as the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, if pancreatic cancer spreads to the liver, the cancer cells in the liver are actually pancreatic cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The disease is metastatic pancreatic cancer, not liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for pancreatic cancer: Stage 0 (Carcinoma in Situ) In stage 0, abnormal cells are found in the lining of the pancreas.<EOS>
<BOS>These abnormal cells may become cancer and spread into nearby normal tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage 0 is also called carcinoma in situ.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I In stage I, cancer has formed and is found in the pancreas only.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I is divided into stage IA and stage IB, based on the size of the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IA: The tumor is 2 centimeters or smaller.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IB: The tumor is larger than 2 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II In stage II, cancer may have spread to nearby tissue and organs, and may have spread to lymph nodes near the pancreas.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II is divided into stage IIA and stage IIB, based on where the cancer has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIA: Cancer has spread to nearby tissue and organs but has not spread to nearby lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIB: Cancer has spread to nearby lymph nodes and may have spread to nearby tissue and organs.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III In stage III, cancer has spread to the major blood vessels near the pancreas and may have spread to nearby lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV In stage IV, cancer may be of any size and has spread to distant organs, such as the liver, lung, and peritoneal cavity.<EOS>
<BOS>It may have also spread to organs and tissues near the pancreas or to lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points There are different types of treatment for patients with pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Five types of standard treatment are used: Surgery Radiation therapy Chemotherapy Chemoradiation therapy Targeted therapy There are treatments for pain caused by pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients with pancreatic cancer have special nutritional needs.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Biologic therapy Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed There are different types of treatment for patients with pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Different types of treatment are available for patients with pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some treatments are standard (the currently used treatment), and some are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>A treatment clinical trial is a research study meant to help improve current treatments or obtain information on new treatments for patients with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>When clinical trials show that a new treatment is better than the standard treatment, the new treatment may become the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials are open only to patients who have not started treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Five types of standard treatment are used: Surgery One of the following types of surgery may be used to take out the tumor: Whipple procedure: A surgical procedure in which the head of the pancreas, the gallbladder, part of the stomach, part of the small intestine, and the bile duct are removed.<EOS>
<BOS>Enough of the pancreas is left to produce digestive juices and insulin.<EOS>
<BOS>Total pancreatectomy: This operation removes the whole pancreas, part of the stomach, part of the small intestine, the common bile duct, the gallbladder, the spleen, and nearby lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Distal pancreatectomy: The body and the tail of the pancreas and usually the spleen are removed.<EOS>
<BOS>If the cancer has spread and cannot be removed, the following types of palliative surgery may be done to relieve symptoms and improve quality of life: Surgical biliary bypass: If cancer is blocking the small intestine and bile is building up in the gallbladder, a biliary bypass may be done.<EOS>
<BOS>During this operation, the doctor will cut the gallbladder or bile duct and sew it to the small intestine to create a new pathway around the blocked area.<EOS>
<BOS>Endoscopic stent placement: If the tumor is blocking the bile duct, surgery may be done to put in a stent (a thin tube) to drain bile that has built up in the area.<EOS>
<BOS>The doctor may place the stent through a catheter that drains to the outside of the body or the stent may go around the blocked area and drain the bile into the small intestine.<EOS>
<BOS>Gastric bypass: If the tumor is blocking the flow of food from the stomach, the stomach may be sewn directly to the small intestine so the patient can continue to eat normally.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy Radiation therapy is a cancer treatment that uses highenergy xrays or other types of radiation to kill cancer cells or keep them from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two types of radiation therapy: External radiation therapy uses a machine outside the body to send radiation toward the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy uses a radioactive substance sealed in needles, seeds, wires, or catheters that are placed directly into or near the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the radiation therapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>External radiation therapy is used to treat pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy Chemotherapy is a cancer treatment that uses drugs to stop the growth of cancer cells, either by killing the cells or by stopping them from dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is taken by mouth or injected into a vein or muscle, the drugs enter the bloodstream and can reach cancer cells throughout the body (systemic chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is placed directly into the cerebrospinal fluid, an organ, or a body cavity such as the abdomen, the drugs mainly affect cancer cells in those areas (regional chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>Combination chemotherapy is treatment using more than one anticancer drug.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the chemotherapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Pancreatic Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemoradiation therapy Chemoradiation therapy combines chemotherapy and radiation therapy to increase the effects of both.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy Targeted therapy is a type of treatment that uses drugs or other substances to identify and attack specific cancer cells without harming normal cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Tyrosine kinase inhibitors (TKIs) are targeted therapy drugs that block signals needed for tumors to grow.<EOS>
<BOS>Erlotinib is a type of TKI used to treat pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Pancreatic Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>There are treatments for pain caused by pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Pain can occur when the tumor presses on nerves or other organs near the pancreas.<EOS>
<BOS>When pain medicine is not enough, there are treatments that act on nerves in the abdomen to relieve the pain.<EOS>
<BOS>The doctor may inject medicine into the area around affected nerves or may cut the nerves to block the feeling of pain.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy with or without chemotherapy can also help relieve pain by shrinking the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>See the PDQ summary on Cancer Pain for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients with pancreatic cancer have special nutritional needs.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove the pancreas may affect its ability to make pancreatic enzymes that help to digest food.<EOS>
<BOS>As a result, patients may have problems digesting food and absorbing nutrients into the body.<EOS>
<BOS>To prevent malnutrition, the doctor may prescribe medicines that replace these enzymes.<EOS>
<BOS>See the PDQ summary on Nutrition in Cancer Care for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary section describes treatments that are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>It may not mention every new treatment being studied.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Biologic therapy Biologic therapy is a treatment that uses the patients immune system to fight cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Substances made by the body or made in a laboratory are used to boost, direct, or restore the bodys natural defenses against cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of cancer treatment is also called biotherapy or immunotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed Some of the tests that were done to diagnose the cancer or to find out the stage of the cancer may be repeated.<EOS>
<BOS>Some tests will be repeated in order to see how well the treatment is working.<EOS>
<BOS>Decisions about whether to continue, change, or stop treatment may be based on the results of these tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests will continue to be done from time to time after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of these tests can show if your condition has changed or if the cancer has recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>These tests are sometimes called followup tests or checkups.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options by Stage Stages I and II Pancreatic Cancer Treatment of stage I and stage II pancreatic cancer may include the following: Surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery followed by chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery followed by chemoradiation.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of combination chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of chemotherapy and targeted therapy, with or without chemoradiation.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of chemotherapy and/or radiation therapy before surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage I pancreatic cancer and stage II pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage III Pancreatic Cancer Treatment of stage III pancreatic cancer may include the following: Palliative surgery or stent placement to bypass blocked areas in ducts or the small intestine.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy followed by chemoradiation.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemoradiation followed by chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy with or without targeted therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of new anticancer therapies together with chemotherapy or chemoradiation.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of radiation therapy given during surgery or internal radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage III pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV Pancreatic Cancer Treatment of stage IV pancreatic cancer may include the following: Palliative treatments to relieve pain, such as nerve blocks, and other supportive care.<EOS>
<BOS>Palliative surgery or stent placement to bypass blocked areas in ducts or the small intestine.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy with or without targeted therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials of new anticancer agents with or without chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage IV pancreatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points The plan for cancer treatment depends on where the NET is found in the pancreas and whether it has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The plan for cancer treatment depends on where the NET is found in the pancreas and whether it has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out if cancer has spread within the pancreas or to other parts of the body is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of the tests and procedures used to diagnose pancreatic neuroendocrine tumors (NETs) are also used to find out whether the cancer has spread.<EOS>
<BOS>See the General Information section for a description of these tests and procedures.<EOS>
<BOS>Although there is a standard staging system for pancreatic NETs, it is not used to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment of pancreatic NETs is based on the following: Whether the cancer is found in one place in the pancreas.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer is found in several places in the pancreas.<EOS>
<BOS>Whether the cancer has spread to lymph nodes near the pancreas or to other parts of the body such as the liver, lung, peritoneum, or bone.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>When cancer spreads to another part of the body, it is called metastasis.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells break away from where they began (the primary tumor) and travel through the lymph system or blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the lymph system, travels through the lymph vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the blood, travels through the blood vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The metastatic tumor is the same type of tumor as the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, if a pancreatic neuroendocrine tumor spreads to the liver, the tumor cells in the liver are actually neuroendocrine tumor cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The disease is metastatic pancreatic neuroendocrine tumor, not liver cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points There are different types of treatment for patients with breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Five types of standard treatment are used: Surgery Radiation therapy Chemotherapy Hormone therapy Targeted therapy New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant Treatment for breast cancer may cause side effects.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of treatment for patients with breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Different types of treatment are available for patients with breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some treatments are standard (the currently used treatment), and some are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>A treatment clinical trial is a research study meant to help improve current treatments or obtain information on new treatments for patients with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>When clinical trials show that a new treatment is better than the standard treatment, the new treatment may become the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials are open only to patients who have not started treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Five types of standard treatment are used: Surgery Most patients with breast cancer have surgery to remove the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Sentinel lymph node biopsy is the removal of the sentinel lymph node during surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>The sentinel lymph node is the first lymph node to receive lymphatic drainage from a tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>It is the first lymph node where the cancer is likely to spread.<EOS>
<BOS>A radioactive substance and/or blue dye is injected near the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The substance or dye flows through the lymph ducts to the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>The first lymph node to receive the substance or dye is removed.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the tissue under a microscope to look for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>After the sentinel lymph node biopsy, the surgeon removes the tumor using breastconserving surgery or mastectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>If cancer cells were not found in the sentinel lymph node, it may not be necessary to remove more lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>If cancer cells were found, more lymph nodes will be removed through a separate incision.<EOS>
<BOS>This is called a lymph node dissection.<EOS>
<BOS>Types of surgery include the following: Breastconserving surgery is an operation to remove the cancer and some normal tissue around it, but not the breast itself.<EOS>
<BOS>Part of the chest wall lining may also be removed if the cancer is near it.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of surgery may also be called lumpectomy, partial mastectomy, segmental mastectomy, quadrantectomy, or breastsparing surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Total mastectomy: Surgery to remove the whole breast that has cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called a simple mastectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the lymph nodes under the arm may be removed and checked for cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>This may be done at the same time as the breast surgery or after.<EOS>
<BOS>This is done through a separate incision.<EOS>
<BOS>Modified radical mastectomy: Surgery to remove the whole breast that has cancer, many of the lymph nodes under the arm, the lining over the chest muscles, and sometimes, part of the chest wall muscles.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy may be given before surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>When given before surgery, chemotherapy will shrink the tumor and reduce the amount of tissue that needs to be removed during surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment given before surgery is called preoperative therapy or neoadjuvant therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Even if the doctor removes all the cancer that can be seen at the time of the surgery, some patients may be given radiation therapy, chemotherapy, or hormone therapy after surgery, to kill any cancer cells that are left.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment given after the surgery, to lower the risk that the cancer will come back, is called postoperative therapy or adjuvant therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>If a patient is going to have a mastectomy, breast reconstruction (surgery to rebuild a breasts shape after a mastectomy) may be considered.<EOS>
<BOS>Breast reconstruction may be done at the time of the mastectomy or at some time after.<EOS>
<BOS>The reconstructed breast may be made with the patients own (nonbreast) tissue or by using implants filled with saline or silicone gel.<EOS>
<BOS>Before the decision to get an implant is made, patients can call the Food and Drug Administration's (FDA) Center for Devices and Radiologic Health at 1888INFOFDA (18884636332) or visit the FDA website for more information on breast implants.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy Radiation therapy is a cancer treatment that uses highenergy xrays or other types of radiation to kill cancer cells or keep them from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two types of radiation therapy: External radiation therapy uses a machine outside the body to send radiation toward the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy uses a radioactive substance sealed in needles, seeds, wires, or catheters that are placed directly into or near the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the radiation therapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>External radiation therapy is used to treat breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy with strontium89 (a radionuclide) is used to relieve bone pain caused by breast cancer that has spread to the bones.<EOS>
<BOS>Strontium89 is injected into a vein and travels to the surface of the bones.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation is released and kills cancer cells in the bones.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy Chemotherapy is a cancer treatment that uses drugs to stop the growth of cancer cells, either by killing the cells or by stopping them from dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is taken by mouth or injected into a vein or muscle, the drugs enter the bloodstream and can reach cancer cells throughout the body (systemic chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is placed directly into the cerebrospinal fluid, an organ, or a body cavity such as the abdomen, the drugs mainly affect cancer cells in those areas (regional chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>The way the chemotherapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>Systemic chemotherapy is used in the treatment of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Breast Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy Hormone therapy is a cancer treatment that removes hormones or blocks their action and stops cancer cells from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormones are substances made by glands in the body and circulated in the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>Some hormones can cause certain cancers to grow.<EOS>
<BOS>If tests show that the cancer cells have places where hormones can attach (receptors), drugs, surgery, or radiation therapy is used to reduce the production of hormones or block them from working.<EOS>
<BOS>The hormone estrogen, which makes some breast cancers grow, is made mainly by the ovaries.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment to stop the ovaries from making estrogen is called ovarian ablation.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy with tamoxifen is often given to patients with early localized breast cancer that can be removed by surgery and those with metastatic breast cancer (cancer that has spread to other parts of the body).<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy with tamoxifen or estrogens can act on cells all over the body and may increase the chance of developing endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Women taking tamoxifen should have a pelvic exam every year to look for any signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Any vaginal bleeding, other than menstrual bleeding, should be reported to a doctor as soon as possible.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy with a luteinizing hormonereleasing hormone (LHRH) agonist is given to some premenopausal women who have just been diagnosed with hormone receptor positive breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>LHRH agonists decrease the body's estrogen and progesterone.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy with an aromatase inhibitor is given to some postmenopausal women who have hormone receptor positive breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Aromatase inhibitors decrease the body's estrogen by blocking an enzyme called aromatase from turning androgen into estrogen.<EOS>
<BOS>Anastrozole, letrozole, and exemestane are types of aromatase inhibitors.<EOS>
<BOS>For the treatment of early localized breast cancer that can be removed by surgery, certain aromatase inhibitors may be used as adjuvant therapy instead of tamoxifen or after 2 to 3 years of tamoxifen use.<EOS>
<BOS>For the treatment of metastatic breast cancer, aromatase inhibitors are being tested in clinical trials to compare them to hormone therapy with tamoxifen.<EOS>
<BOS>Other types of hormone therapy include megestrol acetate or antiestrogen therapy such as fulvestrant.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Breast Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy Targeted therapy is a type of treatment that uses drugs or other substances to identify and attack specific cancer cells without harming normal cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Monoclonal antibodies, tyrosine kinase inhibitors, cyclindependent kinase inhibitors, mammalian target of rapamycin (mTOR) inhibitors, and PARP inhibitors are types of targeted therapies used in the treatment of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Monoclonal antibody therapy is a cancer treatment that uses antibodies made in the laboratory, from a single type of immune system cell.<EOS>
<BOS>These antibodies can identify substances on cancer cells or normal substances that may help cancer cells grow.<EOS>
<BOS>The antibodies attach to the substances and kill the cancer cells, block their growth, or keep them from spreading.<EOS>
<BOS>Monoclonal antibodies are given by infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>They may be used alone or to carry drugs, toxins, or radioactive material directly to cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Monoclonal antibodies may be used in combination with chemotherapy as adjuvant therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Types of monoclonal antibody therapy include the following: Trastuzumab is a monoclonal antibody that blocks the effects of the growth factor protein HER2, which sends growth signals to breast cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>It may be used with other therapies to treat HER2 positive breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Pertuzumab is a monoclonal antibody that may be combined with trastuzumab and chemotherapy to treat breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>It may be used to treat certain patients with HER2 positive breast cancer that has metastasized (spread to other parts of the body).<EOS>
<BOS>It may also be used as neoadjuvant therapy in certain patients with early stage HER2 positive breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Adotrastuzumab emtansine is a monoclonal antibody linked to an anticancer drug.<EOS>
<BOS>This is called an antibodydrug conjugate.<EOS>
<BOS>It is used to treat HER2 positive breast cancer that has spread to other parts of the body or recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>Tyrosine kinase inhibitors are targeted therapy drugs that block signals needed for tumors to grow.<EOS>
<BOS>Tyrosine kinase inhibitors may be used with other anticancer drugs as adjuvant therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Tyrosine kinase inhibitors include the following: Lapatinib is a tyrosine kinase inhibitor that blocks the effects of the HER2 protein and other proteins inside tumor cells.<EOS>
<BOS>It may be used with other drugs to treat patients with HER2 positive breast cancer that has progressed after treatment with trastuzumab.<EOS>
<BOS>Cyclindependent kinase inhibitors are targeted therapy drugs that block proteins called cyclindependent kinases, which cause the growth of cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Cyclindependent kinase inhibitors include the following: Palbociclib is a cyclindependent kinase inhibitor used with the drug letrozole to treat breast cancer that is estrogen receptor positive and HER2 negative and has spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>It is used in postmenopausal women whose cancer has not been treated with hormone therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Palbociclib may also be used with fulvestrant in women whose disease has gotten worse after treatment with hormone therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Ribociclib is a cyclindependent kinase inhibitor used with letrozole to treat breast cancer that is hormone receptor positive and HER2 negative and has come back or spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>It is used in postmenopausal women whose cancer has not been treated with hormone therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Mammalian target of rapamycin (mTOR) inhibitors block a protein called mTOR, which may keep cancer cells from growing and prevent the growth of new blood vessels that tumors need to grow.<EOS>
<BOS>mTOR inhibitors include the following: Everolimus is an mTOR inhibitor used in postmenopausal women with advanced hormone receptor positive breast cancer that is also HER2 negative and has not gotten better with other treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>PARP inhibitors are a type of targeted therapy that block DNA repair and may cause cancer cells to die.<EOS>
<BOS>PARP inhibitor therapy is being studied for the treatment of patients with triple negative breast cancer or tumors with BRCA1 or BRCA2 mutations.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Breast Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>This summary section describes treatments that are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>It may not mention every new treatment being studied.<EOS>
<BOS>Information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant Highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant is a way of giving high doses of chemotherapy and replacing blood forming cells destroyed by the cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Stem cells (immature blood cells) are removed from the blood or bone marrow of the patient or a donor and are frozen and stored.<EOS>
<BOS>After the chemotherapy is completed, the stored stem cells are thawed and given back to the patient through an infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>These reinfused stem cells grow into (and restore) the bodys blood cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Studies have shown that highdose chemotherapy followed by stem cell transplant does not work better than standard chemotherapy in the treatment of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Doctors have decided that, for now, highdose chemotherapy should be tested only in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Before taking part in such a trial, women should talk with their doctors about the serious side effects, including death, that may be caused by highdose chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment for breast cancer may cause side effects.<EOS>
<BOS>For information about side effects that begin during treatment for cancer, see our Side Effects page.<EOS>
<BOS>Some treatments for breast cancer may cause side effects that continue or appear months or years after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>These are called late effects.<EOS>
<BOS>Late effects of radiation therapy are not common, but may include: Inflammation of the lung after radiation therapy to the breast, especially when chemotherapy is given at the same time.<EOS>
<BOS>Arm lymphedema, especially when radiation therapy is given after lymph node dissection.<EOS>
<BOS>In women younger than 45 years who receive radiation therapy to the chest wall after mastectomy, there may be a higher risk of developing breast cancer in the other breast.<EOS>
<BOS>Late effects of chemotherapy depend on the drugs used, but may include: Heart failure.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood clots.<EOS>
<BOS>Premature menopause.<EOS>
<BOS>Second cancer, such as leukemia.<EOS>
<BOS>Late effects of targeted therapy with trastuzumab, lapatinib, or pertuzumab may include: Heart problems such as heart failure.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>For some patients, taking part in a clinical trial may be the best treatment choice.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are part of the cancer research process.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are done to find out if new cancer treatments are safe and effective or better than the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Many of today's standard treatments for cancer are based on earlier clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in a clinical trial may receive the standard treatment or be among the first to receive a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients who take part in clinical trials also help improve the way cancer will be treated in the future.<EOS>
<BOS>Even when clinical trials do not lead to effective new treatments, they often answer important questions and help move research forward.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials only include patients who have not yet received treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials test treatments for patients whose cancer has not gotten better.<EOS>
<BOS>There are also clinical trials that test new ways to stop cancer from recurring (coming back) or reduce the side effects of cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials are taking place in many parts of the country.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Treatment Options section that follows for links to current treatment clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>These have been retrieved from NCI's listing of clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests that were done to diagnose the cancer or to find out the stage of the cancer may be repeated.<EOS>
<BOS>Some tests will be repeated in order to see how well the treatment is working.<EOS>
<BOS>Decisions about whether to continue, change, or stop treatment may be based on the results of these tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests will continue to be done from time to time after treatment has ended.<EOS>
<BOS>The results of these tests can show if your condition has changed or if the cancer has recurred (come back).<EOS>
<BOS>These tests are sometimes called followup tests or checkups.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment Options for Breast Cancer Early, Localized, or Operable Breast Cancer Treatment of early, localized, or operable breast cancer may include the following: Surgery Breastconserving surgery and sentinel lymph node biopsy.<EOS>
<BOS>If cancer is found in the lymph nodes, a lymph node dissection may be done.<EOS>
<BOS>Modified radical mastectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>Breast reconstruction surgery may also be done.<EOS>
<BOS>Postoperative radiation therapy For women who had breastconserving surgery, radiation therapy is given to the whole breast to lessen the chance the cancer will come back.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy may also be given to lymph nodes in the area.<EOS>
<BOS>For women who had a modified radical mastectomy, radiation therapy may be given to lessen the chance the cancer will come back if any of the following are true: Cancer was found in 4 or more lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer had spread to tissue around the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor was large.<EOS>
<BOS>There is tumor close to or remaining in the tissue near the edges of where the tumor was removed.<EOS>
<BOS>Postoperative systemic therapy Systemic therapy is the use of drugs that can enter the bloodstream and reach cancer cells throughout the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Postoperative systemic therapy is given to lessen the chance the cancer will come back after surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Postoperative systemic therapy is given depending on whether: The tumor is hormone receptor negative or positive.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor is HER2/neu negative or positive.<EOS>
<BOS>The tumor is hormone receptor negative and HER2/neu negative (triple negative).<EOS>
<BOS>The size of the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>In premenopausal women with hormone receptor positive tumors, no more treatment may be needed or postoperative therapy may include: Tamoxifen therapy with or without chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Tamoxifen therapy and treatment to stop or lessen how much estrogen is made by the ovaries.<EOS>
<BOS>Drug therapy, surgery to remove the ovaries, or radiation therapy to the ovaries may be used.<EOS>
<BOS>Aromatase inhibitor therapy and treatment to stop or lessen how much estrogen is made by the ovaries.<EOS>
<BOS>Drug therapy, surgery to remove the ovaries, or radiation therapy to the ovaries may be used.<EOS>
<BOS>In postmenopausal women with hormone receptor positive tumors, no more treatment may be needed or postoperative therapy may include: Aromatase inhibitor therapy with or without chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Tamoxifen followed by aromatase inhibitor therapy, with or without chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>In women with hormone receptor negative tumors, no more treatment may be needed or postoperative therapy may include: Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>In women with HER2/neu negative tumors, postoperative therapy may include: Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>In women with small, HER2/neu positive tumors, and no cancer in the lymph nodes, no more treatment may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>If there is cancer in the lymph nodes, or the tumor is large, postoperative therapy may include: Chemotherapy and targeted therapy (trastuzumab).<EOS>
