<BOS>Hormone therapy, such as tamoxifen or aromatase inhibitor therapy, for tumors that are also hormone receptor positive.<EOS>
<BOS>In women with small, hormone receptor negative and HER2/neu negative tumors (triple negative) and no cancer in the lymph nodes, no more treatment may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>If there is cancer in the lymph nodes or the tumor is large, postoperative therapy may include: Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new chemotherapy regimen.<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of PARP inhibitor therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Preoperative systemic therapy Systemic therapy is the use of drugs that can enter the bloodstream and reach cancer cells throughout the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Preoperative systemic therapy is given to shrink the tumor before surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>In postmenopausal women with hormone receptor positive tumors, preoperative therapy may include: Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy, such as tamoxifen or aromatase inhibitor therapy, for women who cannot have chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>In premenopausal women with hormone receptor positive tumors, preoperative therapy may include: A clinical trial of hormone therapy, such as tamoxifen or aromatase inhibitor therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>In women with HER2/neu positive tumors, preoperative therapy may include: Chemotherapy and targeted therapy (trastuzumab).<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy (pertuzumab).<EOS>
<BOS>In women with HER2/neu negative tumors or triple negative tumors, preoperative therapy may include: Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage I breast cancer, stage II breast cancer, stage IIIA breast cancer and stage IIIC breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Locally Advanced or Inflammatory Breast Cancer Treatment of locally advanced or inflammatory breast cancer is a combination of therapies that may include the following: Surgery (breastconserving surgery or total mastectomy) with lymph node dissection.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy before and/or after surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy after surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy after surgery for tumors that are estrogen receptor positive or estrogen receptor unknown.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials testing new anticancer drugs, new drug combinations, and new ways of giving treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with stage IIIB breast cancer, stage IIIC breast cancer, stage IV breast cancer and inflammatory breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Locoregional Recurrent Breast Cancer Treatment of locoregional recurrent breast cancer (cancer that has come back after treatment in the breast, in the chest wall, or in nearby lymph nodes), may include the following: Chemotherapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy for tumors that are hormone receptor positive.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy (trastuzumab).<EOS>
<BOS>A clinical trial of a new treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>See the Metastatic Breast Cancer section for information about treatment options for breast cancer that has spread to parts of the body outside the breast, chest wall, or nearby lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with recurrent breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Metastatic Breast Cancer Treatment options for metastatic breast cancer (cancer that has spread to distant parts of the body) may include the following: Hormone therapy In postmenopausal women who have just been diagnosed with metastatic breast cancer that is hormone receptor positive or if the hormone receptor status is not known, treatment may include: Tamoxifen therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Aromatase inhibitor therapy (anastrozole, letrozole, or exemestane).<EOS>
<BOS>Sometimes cyclindependent kinase inhibitor therapy (palbociclib) is also given.<EOS>
<BOS>In premenopausal women who have just been diagnosed with metastatic breast cancer that is hormone receptor positive, treatment may include: Tamoxifen, an LHRH agonist, or both.<EOS>
<BOS>In women whose tumors are hormone receptor positive or hormone receptor unknown, with spread to the bone or soft tissue only, and who have been treated with tamoxifen, treatment may include: Aromatase inhibitor therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Other hormone therapy such as megestrol acetate, estrogen or androgen therapy, or antiestrogen therapy such as fulvestrant.<EOS>
<BOS>Targeted therapy In women with metastatic breast cancer that is hormone receptor positive and has not responded to other treatments, options may include targeted therapy such as: Trastuzumab, lapatinib, pertuzumab, or mTOR inhibitors.<EOS>
<BOS>Antibodydrug conjugate therapy with adotrastuzumab emtansine.<EOS>
<BOS>Cyclindependent kinase inhibitor therapy (palbociclib) combined with letrozole.<EOS>
<BOS>In women with metastatic breast cancer that is HER2/neu positive, treatment may include: Targeted therapy such as trastuzumab, pertuzumab, adotrastuzumab emtansine, or lapatinib.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy In women with metastatic breast cancer that is hormone receptor negative, has not responded to hormone therapy, has spread to other organs or has caused symptoms, treatment may include: Chemotherapy with one or more drugs.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery Total mastectomy for women with open or painful breast lesions.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy may be given after surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove cancer that has spread to the brain or spine.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy may be given after surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove cancer that has spread to the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to repair or help support weak or broken bones.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy may be given after surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery to remove fluid that has collected around the lungs or heart.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy Radiation therapy to the bones, brain, spinal cord, breast, or chest wall to relieve symptoms and improve quality of life.<EOS>
<BOS>Strontium89 (a radionuclide) to relieve pain from cancer that has spread to bones throughout the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Other treatment options Other treatment options for metastatic breast cancer include: Drug therapy with bisphosphonates or denosumab to reduce bone disease and pain when cancer has spread to the bone.<EOS>
<BOS>(See the PDQ summary on Cancer Pain for more information about bisphosphonates.<EOS>
<BOS>) A clinical trial of highdose chemotherapy with stem cell transplant.<EOS>
<BOS>Clinical trials testing new anticancer drugs, new drug combinations, and new ways of giving treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Check the list of NCIsupported cancer clinical trials that are now accepting patients with metastatic cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>For more specific results, refine the search by using other search features, such as the location of the trial, the type of treatment, or the name of the drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about clinical trials that may be right for you.<EOS>
<BOS>General information about clinical trials is available from the NCI website.<EOS>
<BOS>Men with breast cancer usually have lumps that can be felt.<EOS>
<BOS>Lumps and other signs may be caused by male breast cancer or by other conditions.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your doctor if you notice a change in your breasts.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After breast cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the breast or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for male breast cancer: Stage 0 (carcinoma in situ) Stage I Stage II Stage IIIA Stage IIIB Stage IIIC Stage IV After breast cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the breast or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>After breast cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the breast or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>This process is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>The information gathered from the staging process determines the stage of the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to know the stage in order to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Breast cancer in men is staged the same as it is in women.<EOS>
<BOS>The spread of cancer from the breast to lymph nodes and other parts of the body appears to be similar in men and women.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used in the staging process: Sentinel lymph node biopsy : The removal of the sentinel lymph node during surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>The sentinel lymph node is the first lymph node to receive lymphatic drainage from a tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>It is the first lymph node the cancer is likely to spread to from the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>A radioactive substance and/or blue dye is injected near the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The substance or dye flows through the lymph ducts to the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>The first lymph node to receive the substance or dye is removed.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the tissue under a microscope to look for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>If cancer cells are not found, it may not be necessary to remove more lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Chest xray : An xray of the organs and bones inside the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>An xray is a type of energy beam that can go through the body and onto film, making a picture of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>Bone scan : A procedure to check if there are rapidly dividing cells, such as cancer cells, in the bone.<EOS>
<BOS>A very small amount of radioactive material is injected into a vein and travels through the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>The radioactive material collects in the bones and is detected by a scanner.<EOS>
<BOS>PET scan (positron emission tomography scan): A procedure to find malignant tumor cells in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A small amount of radioactive glucose (sugar) is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The PET scanner rotates around the body and makes a picture of where glucose is being used in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant tumor cells show up brighter in the picture because they are more active and take up more glucose than normal cells do.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>When cancer spreads to another part of the body, it is called metastasis.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells break away from where they began (the primary tumor) and travel through the lymph system or blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the lymph system, travels through the lymph vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the blood, travels through the blood vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The metastatic tumor is the same type of cancer as the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, if breast cancer spreads to the bone, the cancer cells in the bone are actually breast cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The disease is metastatic breast cancer, not bone cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for male breast cancer: This section describes the stages of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The breast cancer stage is based on the results of testing that is done on the tumor and lymph nodes removed during surgery and other tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage 0 (carcinoma in situ) There are 3 types of breast carcinoma in situ: Ductal carcinoma in situ (DCIS) is a noninvasive condition in which abnormal cells are found in the lining of a breast duct.<EOS>
<BOS>The abnormal cells have not spread outside the duct to other tissues in the breast.<EOS>
<BOS>In some cases, DCIS may become invasive cancer and spread to other tissues.<EOS>
<BOS>At this time, there is no way to know which lesions could become invasive.<EOS>
<BOS>Paget disease of the nipple is a condition in which abnormal cells are found in the nipple only.<EOS>
<BOS>Lobular carcinoma in situ (LCIS) is a condition in which abnormal cells are found in the lobules of the breast.<EOS>
<BOS>This condition has not been seen in men.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I In stage I, cancer has formed.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I is divided into stages IA and IB.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IA, the tumor is 2 centimeters or smaller.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has not spread outside the breast.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IB, small clusters of breast cancer cells (larger than 0.<EOS>
<BOS>2 millimeter but not larger than 2 millimeters) are found in the lymph nodes and either: no tumor is found in the breast; or the tumor is 2 centimeters or smaller.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II Stage II is divided into stages IIA and IIB.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIA no tumor is found in the breast or the tumor is 2 centimeters or smaller.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer (larger than 2 millimeters) is found in 1 to 3 axillary lymph nodes or in the lymph nodes near the breastbone (found during a sentinel lymph node biopsy); or the tumor is larger than 2 centimeters but not larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has not spread to the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIB, the tumor is: larger than 2 centimeters but not larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Small clusters of breast cancer cells (larger than 0.<EOS>
<BOS>2 millimeter but not larger than 2 millimeters) are found in the lymph nodes; or larger than 2 centimeters but not larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to 1 to 3 axillary lymph nodes or to the lymph nodes near the breastbone (found during a sentinel lymph node biopsy); or larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has not spread to the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIA In stage IIIA: no tumor is found in the breast or the tumor may be any size.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer is found in 4 to 9 axillary lymph nodes or in the lymph nodes near the breastbone (found during imaging tests or a physical exam); or the tumor is larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Small clusters of breast cancer cells (larger than 0.<EOS>
<BOS>2 millimeter but not larger than 2 millimeters) are found in the lymph nodes; or the tumor is larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to 1 to 3 axillary lymph nodes or to the lymph nodes near the breastbone (found during a sentinel lymph node biopsy).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIB In stage IIIB, the tumor may be any size and cancer has spread to the chest wall and/or to the skin of the breast and caused swelling or an ulcer.<EOS>
<BOS>Also, cancer may have spread to : up to 9 axillary lymph nodes; or the lymph nodes near the breastbone.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer that has spread to the skin of the breast may also be inflammatory breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>See the section on Inflammatory Male Breast Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIC In stage IIIC, no tumor is found in the breast or the tumor may be any size.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may have spread to the skin of the breast and caused swelling or an ulcer and/or has spread to the chest wall.<EOS>
<BOS>Also, cancer has spread to: 10 or more axillary lymph nodes; or lymph nodes above or below the collarbone; or axillary lymph nodes and lymph nodes near the breastbone.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer that has spread to the skin of the breast may also be inflammatory breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>See the section on Inflammatory Male Breast Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>For treatment, stage IIIC breast cancer is divided into operable and inoperable stage IIIC.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV In stage IV, cancer has spread to other organs of the body, most often the bones, lungs, liver, or brain.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After breast cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the breast or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for breast cancer: Stage 0 (carcinoma in situ) Stage I Stage II Stage IIIA Stage IIIB Stage IIIC Stage IV After breast cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the breast or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out if the cancer has spread within the breast or to other parts of the body is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>The information gathered from the staging process determines the stage of the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to know the stage in order to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some procedures may expose the unborn baby to harmful radiation or dyes.<EOS>
<BOS>These procedures are done only if absolutely necessary.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain actions can be taken to expose the unborn baby to as little radiation as possible, such as the use of a leadlined shield to cover the abdomen.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used to stage breast cancer during pregnancy: Sentinel lymph node biopsy : The removal of the sentinel lymph node during surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>The sentinel lymph node is the first lymph node to receive lymphatic drainage from a tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>It is the first lymph node the cancer is likely to spread to from the tumor.<EOS>

<BOS>The substance or dye flows through the lymph ducts to the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>The first lymph node to receive the substance or dye is removed.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the tissue under a microscope to look for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>If cancer cells are not found, it may not be necessary to remove more lymph nodes.<EOS>

<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>Bone scan : A procedure to check if there are rapidly dividing cells, such as cancer cells, in the bone.<EOS>
<BOS>A very small amount of radioactive material is injected into a vein and travels through the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>The radioactive material collects in bones with cancer and is detected by a scanner.<EOS>
<BOS>PET scan (positron emission tomography scan): A procedure to find malignant tumor cells in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A small amount of radioactive glucose (sugar) is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The PET scanner rotates around the body and makes a picture of where glucose is being used in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant tumor cells show up brighter in the picture because they are more active and take up more glucose than normal cells do.<EOS>
<BOS>MRI (magnetic resonance imaging): A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, such as the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>Ultrasound exam: A procedure in which highenergy sound waves (ultrasound) are bounced off internal tissues or organs, such as the liver, and make echoes.<EOS>
<BOS>The echoes form a picture of body tissues called a sonogram.<EOS>
<BOS>The picture can be printed to be looked at later.<EOS>
<BOS>Chest xray : An xray of the organs and bones inside the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>An xray is a type of energy beam that can go through the body and onto film, making a picture of areas inside the body.<EOS>

<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>

<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>

<BOS>For example, if breast cancer spreads to the bone, the cancer cells in the bone are actually breast cancer cells.<EOS>

<BOS>The following stages are used for breast cancer: This section describes the stages of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The breast cancer stage is based on the results of testing that is done on the tumor and lymph nodes removed during surgery and other tests.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage 0 (carcinoma in situ) There are 3 types of breast carcinoma in situ: Ductal carcinoma in situ (DCIS) is a noninvasive condition in which abnormal cells are found in the lining of a breast duct.<EOS>
<BOS>The abnormal cells have not spread outside the duct to other tissues in the breast.<EOS>
<BOS>In some cases, DCIS may become invasive cancer and spread to other tissues.<EOS>
<BOS>At this time, there is no way to know which lesions could become invasive.<EOS>
<BOS>Lobular carcinoma in situ (LCIS) is a condition in which abnormal cells are found in the lobules of the breast.<EOS>
<BOS>This condition seldom becomes invasive cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>However, having LCIS in one breast increases the risk of developing breast cancer in either breast.<EOS>
<BOS>Paget disease of the nipple is a condition in which abnormal cells are found in the nipple only.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I In stage I, cancer has formed.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I is divided into stages IA and IB.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IA, the tumor is 2 centimeters or smaller.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has not spread outside the breast.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IB, small clusters of breast cancer cells (larger than 0.<EOS>
<BOS>2 millimeter but not larger than 2 millimeters) are found in the lymph nodes and either: no tumor is found in the breast; or the tumor is 2 centimeters or smaller.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II Stage II is divided into stages IIA and IIB.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIA: no tumor is found in the breast or the tumor is 2 centimeters or smaller.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer (larger than 2 millimeters) is found in 1 to 3 axillary lymph nodes or in the lymph nodes near the breastbone (found during a sentinel lymph node biopsy); or the tumor is larger than 2 centimeters but not larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has not spread to the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>In stage IIB, the tumor is: larger than 2 centimeters but not larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Small clusters of breast cancer cells (larger than 0.<EOS>
<BOS>2 millimeter but not larger than 2 millimeters) are found in the lymph nodes; or larger than 2 centimeters but not larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to 1 to 3 axillary lymph nodes or to the lymph nodes near the breastbone (found during a sentinel lymph node biopsy); or larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has not spread to the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIA In stage IIIA: no tumor is found in the breast or the tumor may be any size.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer is found in 4 to 9 axillary lymph nodes or in the lymph nodes near the breastbone (found during imaging tests or a physical exam); or the tumor is larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Small clusters of breast cancer cells (larger than 0.<EOS>
<BOS>2 millimeter but not larger than 2 millimeters) are found in the lymph nodes; or the tumor is larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to 1 to 3 axillary lymph nodes or to the lymph nodes near the breastbone (found during a sentinel lymph node biopsy).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIB In stage IIIB, the tumor may be any size and cancer has spread to the chest wall and/or to the skin of the breast and caused swelling or an ulcer.<EOS>
<BOS>Also, cancer may have spread to: up to 9 axillary lymph nodes; or the lymph nodes near the breastbone.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer that has spread to the skin of the breast may also be inflammatory breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>See the section on Inflammatory Breast Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIC In stage IIIC, no tumor is found in the breast or the tumor may be any size.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may have spread to the skin of the breast and caused swelling or an ulcer and/or has spread to the chest wall.<EOS>
<BOS>Also, cancer has spread to: 10 or more axillary lymph nodes; or lymph nodes above or below the collarbone; or axillary lymph nodes and lymph nodes near the breastbone.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer that has spread to the skin of the breast may also be inflammatory breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>See the section on Inflammatory Breast Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>For treatment, stage IIIC breast cancer is divided into operable and inoperable stage IIIC.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV In stage IV, cancer has spread to other organs of the body, most often the bones, lungs, liver, or brain.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Avoiding risk factors and increasing protective factors may help prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following are risk factors for breast cancer: Older age A personal history of breast cancer or benign (noncancer) breast disease Inherited risk of breast cancer Dense breasts Exposure of breast tissue to estrogen made in the body Taking hormone therapy for symptoms of menopause Radiation therapy to the breast or chest Obesity Drinking alcohol The following are protective factors for breast cancer: Less exposure of breast tissue to estrogen made by the body Taking estrogenonly hormone therapy after hysterectomy, selective estrogen receptor modulators, or aromatase inhibitors and inactivators Estrogenonly hormone therapy after hysterectomy Selective estrogen receptor modulators Aromatase inhibitors and inactivators Riskreducing mastectomy Ovarian ablation Getting enough exercise It is not clear whether the following affect the risk of breast cancer: Oral contraceptives Environment Studies have shown that some factors do not affect the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer prevention clinical trials are used to study ways to prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>New ways to prevent breast cancer are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Avoiding risk factors and increasing protective factors may help prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Avoiding cancer risk factors may help prevent certain cancers.<EOS>
<BOS>Risk factors include smoking, being overweight, and not getting enough exercise.<EOS>
<BOS>Increasing protective factors such as quitting smoking and exercising may also help prevent some cancers.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk to your doctor or other health care professional about how you might lower your risk of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>NCI's Breast Cancer Risk Assessment Tool uses a woman's risk factors to estimate her risk for breast cancer during the next five years and up to age 90.<EOS>
<BOS>This online tool is meant to be used by a health care provider.<EOS>
<BOS>For more information on breast cancer risk, call 18004CANCER.<EOS>
<BOS>The following are risk factors for breast cancer: Older age Older age is the main risk factor for most cancers.<EOS>
<BOS>The chance of getting cancer increases as you get older.<EOS>
<BOS>A personal history of breast cancer or benign (noncancer) breast disease Women with any of the following have an increased risk of breast cancer: A personal history of invasive breast cancer, ductal carcinoma in situ (DCIS), or lobular carcinoma in situ (LCIS).<EOS>
<BOS>A personal history of benign (noncancer) breast disease.<EOS>
<BOS>Inherited risk of breast cancer Women with a family history of breast cancer in a firstdegree relative (mother, sister, or daughter) have an increased risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Women who have inherited changes in the BRCA1 and BRCA2 genes or in certain other genes have a higher risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of breast cancer caused by inherited gene changes depends on the type of gene mutation, family history of cancer, and other factors.<EOS>
<BOS>Dense breasts Having breast tissue that is dense on a mammogram is a factor in breast cancer risk.<EOS>
<BOS>The level of risk depends on how dense the breast tissue is.<EOS>
<BOS>Women with very dense breasts have a higher risk of breast cancer than women with low breast density.<EOS>
<BOS>Increased breast density is often an inherited trait, but it may also occur in women who have not had children, have a first pregnancy late in life, take postmenopausal hormones, or drink alcohol.<EOS>
<BOS>Exposure of breast tissue to estrogen made in the body Estrogen is a hormone made by the body.<EOS>
<BOS>It helps the body develop and maintain female sex characteristics.<EOS>
<BOS>Being exposed to estrogen over a long time may increase the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Estrogen levels are highest during the years a woman is menstruating.<EOS>
<BOS>A woman's exposure to estrogen is increased in the following ways: Early menstruation: Beginning to have menstrual periods at age 11 or younger increases the number of years the breast tissue is exposed to estrogen.<EOS>
<BOS>Starting menopause at a later age: The more years a woman menstruates, the longer her breast tissue is exposed to estrogen.<EOS>
<BOS>Older age at first birth or never having given birth: Because estrogen levels are lower during pregnancy, breast tissue is exposed to more estrogen in women who become pregnant for the first time after age 35 or who never become pregnant.<EOS>
<BOS>Taking hormone therapy for symptoms of menopause Hormones, such as estrogen and progesterone, can be made into a pill form in a laboratory.<EOS>
<BOS>Estrogen, progestin, or both may be given to replace the estrogen no longer made by the ovaries in postmenopausal women or women who have had their ovaries removed.<EOS>
<BOS>This is called hormone replacement therapy (HRT) or hormone therapy (HT).<EOS>
<BOS>Combination HRT/HT is estrogen combined with progestin.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of HRT/HT increases the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Studies show that when women stop taking estrogen combined with progestin, the risk of breast cancer decreases.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy to the breast or chest Radiation therapy to the chest for the treatment of cancer increases the risk of breast cancer, starting 10 years after treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of breast cancer depends on the dose of radiation and the age at which it is given.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk is highest if radiation treatment was used during puberty, when breasts are forming.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy to treat cancer in one breast does not appear to increase the risk of cancer in the other breast.<EOS>
<BOS>For women who have inherited changes in the BRCA1 and BRCA2 genes, exposure to radiation, such as that from chest xrays, may further increase the risk of breast cancer, especially in women who were xrayed before 20 years of age.<EOS>
<BOS>Obesity Obesity increases the risk of breast cancer, especially in postmenopausal women who have not used hormone replacement therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Drinking alcohol Drinking alcohol increases the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The level of risk rises as the amount of alcohol consumed rises.<EOS>
<BOS>The following are protective factors for breast cancer: Less exposure of breast tissue to estrogen made by the body Decreasing the length of time a woman's breast tissue is exposed to estrogen may help prevent breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Exposure to estrogen is reduced in the following ways: Early pregnancy: Estrogen levels are lower during pregnancy.<EOS>
<BOS>Women who have a fullterm pregnancy before age 20 have a lower risk of breast cancer than women who have not had children or who give birth to their first child after age 35.<EOS>
<BOS>Breastfeeding: Estrogen levels may remain lower while a woman is breastfeeding.<EOS>
<BOS>Women who breastfed have a lower risk of breast cancer than women who have had children but did not breastfeed.<EOS>
<BOS>Taking estrogenonly hormone therapy after hysterectomy, selective estrogen receptor modulators, or aromatase inhibitors and inactivators Estrogenonly hormone therapy after hysterectomy Hormone therapy with estrogen only may be given to women who have had a hysterectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>In these women, estrogenonly therapy after menopause may decrease the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>There is an increased risk of stroke and heart and blood vessel disease in postmenopausal women who take estrogen after a hysterectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>Selective estrogen receptor modulators Tamoxifen and raloxifene belong to the family of drugs called selective estrogen receptor modulators (SERMs).<EOS>
<BOS>SERMs act like estrogen on some tissues in the body, but block the effect of estrogen on other tissues.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment with tamoxifen lowers the risk of estrogen receptorpositive (ERpositive) breast cancer and ductal carcinoma in situ in premenopausal and postmenopausal women at high risk.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment with raloxifene also lowers the risk of breast cancer in postmenopausal women.<EOS>
<BOS>With either drug, the reduced risk lasts for several years or longer after treatment is stopped.<EOS>
<BOS>Lower rates of broken bones have been noted in patients taking raloxifene.<EOS>
<BOS>Taking tamoxifen increases the risk of hot flashes, endometrial cancer, stroke, cataracts, and blood clots (especially in the lungs and legs).<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of having these problems increases markedly in women older than 50 years compared with younger women.<EOS>
<BOS>Women younger than 50 years who have a high risk of breast cancer may benefit the most from taking tamoxifen.<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of having these problems decreases after tamoxifen is stopped.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about the risks and benefits of taking this drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Taking raloxifene increases the risk of blood clots in the lungs and legs, but does not appear to increase the risk of endometrial cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>In postmenopausal women with osteoporosis (decreased bone density), raloxifene lowers the risk of breast cancer for women who have a high or low risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>It is not known if raloxifene would have the same effect in women who do not have osteoporosis.<EOS>
<BOS>Talk with your doctor about the risks and benefits of taking this drug.<EOS>
<BOS>Other SERMs are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Aromatase inhibitors and inactivators Aromatase inhibitors (anastrozole, letrozole) and inactivators (exemestane) lower the risk of recurrence and of new breast cancers in women who have a history of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Aromatase inhibitors also decrease the risk of breast cancer in women with the following conditions: Postmenopausal women with a personal history of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Women with no personal history of breast cancer who are 60 years and older, have a history of ductal carcinoma in situ with mastectomy, or have a high risk of breast cancer based on the Gail model tool (a tool used to estimate the risk of breast cancer).<EOS>
<BOS>In women with an increased risk of breast cancer, taking aromatase inhibitors decreases the amount of estrogen made by the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Before menopause, estrogen is made by the ovaries and other tissues in a woman's body, including the brain, fat tissue, and skin.<EOS>
<BOS>After menopause, the ovaries stop making estrogen, but the other tissues do not.<EOS>
<BOS>Aromatase inhibitors block the action of an enzyme called aromatase, which is used to make all of the body's estrogen.<EOS>
<BOS>Aromatase inactivators stop the enzyme from working.<EOS>
<BOS>Possible harms from taking aromatase inhibitors include muscle and joint pain, osteoporosis, hot flashes, and feeling very tired.<EOS>
<BOS>Riskreducing mastectomy Some women who have a high risk of breast cancer may choose to have a riskreducing mastectomy (the removal of both breasts when there are no signs of cancer).<EOS>
<BOS>The risk of breast cancer is much lower in these women and most feel less anxious about their risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>However, it is very important to have a cancer risk assessment and counseling about the different ways to prevent breast cancer before making this decision.<EOS>
<BOS>Ovarian ablation The ovaries make most of the estrogen that is made by the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatments that stop or lower the amount of estrogen made by the ovaries include surgery to remove the ovaries, radiation therapy, or taking certain drugs.<EOS>
<BOS>This is called ovarian ablation.<EOS>
<BOS>Premenopausal women who have a high risk of breast cancer due to certain changes in the BRCA1 and BRCA2 genes may choose to have a riskreducing oophorectomy (the removal of both ovaries when there are no signs of cancer).<EOS>
<BOS>This decreases the amount of estrogen made by the body and lowers the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Riskreducing oophorectomy also lowers the risk of breast cancer in normal premenopausal women and in women with an increased risk of breast cancer due to radiation to the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>However, it is very important to have a cancer risk assessment and counseling before making this decision.<EOS>
<BOS>The sudden drop in estrogen levels may cause the symptoms of menopause to begin.<EOS>
<BOS>These include hot flashes, trouble sleeping, anxiety, and depression.<EOS>
<BOS>Longterm effects include decreased sex drive, vaginal dryness, and decreased bone density.<EOS>
<BOS>Getting enough exercise Women who exercise four or more hours a week have a lower risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The effect of exercise on breast cancer risk may be greatest in premenopausal women who have normal or low body weight.<EOS>
<BOS>It is not clear whether the following affect the risk of breast cancer: Oral contraceptives Certain oral contraceptives contain estrogen.<EOS>
<BOS>Some studies have shown that taking oral contraceptives ("the pill") may slightly increase the risk of breast cancer in current users.<EOS>
<BOS>This risk decreases over time.<EOS>
<BOS>Other studies have not shown an increased risk of breast cancer in women who take oral contraceptives.<EOS>
<BOS>Progestin only contraceptives that are injected or implanted do not appear to increase the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>More studies are needed to know whether progestinonly oral contraceptives increase the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Environment Studies have not proven that being exposed to certain substances in the environment, such as chemicals, increases the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Studies have shown that some factors do not affect the risk of breast cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following do not affect the risk of breast cancer: Having an abortion.<EOS>
<BOS>Making diet changes such as eating less fat or more fruits and vegetables.<EOS>
<BOS>Taking vitamins, including fenretinide (a type of vitamin A).<EOS>
<BOS>Cigarette smoking, both active and passive (inhaling secondhand smoke).<EOS>
<BOS>Using underarm deodorant or antiperspirant.<EOS>
<BOS>Taking statins (cholesterol lowering drugs).<EOS>
<BOS>Taking bisphosphonates (drugs used to treat osteoporosis and hypercalcemia) by mouth or by intravenous infusion.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer prevention clinical trials are used to study ways to prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer prevention clinical trials are used to study ways to lower the risk of developing certain types of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some cancer prevention trials are conducted with healthy people who have not had cancer but who have an increased risk for cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Other prevention trials are conducted with people who have had cancer and are trying to prevent another cancer of the same type or to lower their chance of developing a new type of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Other trials are done with healthy volunteers who are not known to have any risk factors for cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The purpose of some cancer prevention clinical trials is to find out whether actions people take can prevent cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>These may include exercising more or quitting smoking or taking certain medicines, vitamins, minerals, or food supplements.<EOS>
<BOS>New ways to prevent breast cancer are being studied in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Being exposed to ultraviolet radiation may increase the risk of skin cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Anything that increases your chance of getting a disease is called a risk factor.<EOS>
<BOS>Having a risk factor does not mean that you will get cancer; not having risk factors doesn't mean that you will not get cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>People who think they may be at risk should discuss this with their doctor.<EOS>
<BOS>Being exposed to ultraviolet (UV) radiation and having skin that is sensitive to UV radiation are risk factors for skin cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>UV radiation is the name for the invisible rays that are part of the energy that comes from the sun.<EOS>
<BOS>Sunlamps and tanning beds also give off UV radiation.<EOS>
<BOS>Risk factors for nonmelanoma and melanoma cancers are not the same.<EOS>
<BOS>Nonmelanoma skin cancer risk factors include: Being exposed to natural sunlight or artificial sunlight (such as from tanning beds) over long periods of time.<EOS>
<BOS>Having a fair complexion, which includes the following: Fair skin that freckles and burns easily, does not tan, or tans poorly.<EOS>
<BOS>Blue or green or other lightcolored eyes.<EOS>
<BOS>Red or blond hair.<EOS>
<BOS>Having actinic keratosis.<EOS>
<BOS>Past treatment with radiation.<EOS>
<BOS>Having a weakened immune system.<EOS>
<BOS>Being exposed to arsenic.<EOS>
<BOS>Melanoma skin cancer risk factors include: Having a fair complexion, which includes the following: Fair skin that freckles and burns easily, does not tan, or tans poorly.<EOS>
<BOS>Blue or green or other lightcolored eyes.<EOS>
<BOS>Red or blond hair.<EOS>
<BOS>Being exposed to natural sunlight or artificial sunlight (such as from tanning beds) over long periods of time.<EOS>
<BOS>Having a history of many blistering sunburns, especially as a child or teenager.<EOS>
<BOS>Having several large or many small moles.<EOS>
<BOS>Having a family history of unusual moles (atypical nevus syndrome).<EOS>
<BOS>Having a family or personal history of melanoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Being white.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs and symptoms of neuroblastoma include bone pain and a lump in the abdomen, neck, or chest.<EOS>
<BOS>The most common signs and symptoms of neuroblastoma are caused by the tumor pressing on nearby tissues as it grows or by cancer spreading to the bone.<EOS>
<BOS>These and other signs and symptoms may be caused by neuroblastoma or by other conditions.<EOS>
<BOS>Check with your childs doctor if your child has any of the following: Lump in the abdomen, neck, or chest.<EOS>
<BOS>Bulging eyes.<EOS>
<BOS>Dark circles around the eyes ("black eyes").<EOS>
<BOS>Bone pain.<EOS>
<BOS>Swollen stomach and trouble breathing (in infants).<EOS>
<BOS>Painless, bluish lumps under the skin (in infants).<EOS>
<BOS>Weakness or paralysis (loss of ability to move a body part).<EOS>
<BOS>Less common signs and symptoms of neuroblastoma include the following: Fever.<EOS>
<BOS>Shortness of breath.<EOS>
<BOS>Feeling tired.<EOS>
<BOS>Easy bruising or bleeding.<EOS>
<BOS>Petechiae (flat, pinpoint spots under the skin caused by bleeding).<EOS>
<BOS>High blood pressure.<EOS>
<BOS>Severe watery diarrhea.<EOS>
<BOS>Horner syndrome (droopy eyelid, smaller pupil, and less sweating on one side of the face).<EOS>
<BOS>Jerky muscle movements.<EOS>
<BOS>Uncontrolled eye movements.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After lung cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the lungs or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for nonsmall cell lung cancer: Occult (hidden) stage Stage 0 (carcinoma in situ) Stage I Stage II Stage IIIA Stage IIIB Stage IV After lung cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread within the lungs or to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out if cancer has spread within the lungs or to other parts of the body is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>The information gathered from the staging process determines the stage of the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>It is important to know the stage in order to plan treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Some of the tests used to diagnose nonsmall cell lung cancer are also used to stage the disease.<EOS>
<BOS>(See the General Information section.<EOS>
<BOS>) Other tests and procedures that may be used in the staging process include the following: MRI (magnetic resonance imaging): A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, such as the brain.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, such as the brain and abdomen, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>PET scan (positron emission tomography scan): A procedure to find malignant tumor cells in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>A small amount of radioactive glucose (sugar) is injected into a vein.<EOS>
<BOS>The PET scanner rotates around the body and makes a picture of where glucose is being used in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Malignant tumor cells show up brighter in the picture because they are more active and take up more glucose than normal cells do.<EOS>
<BOS>Radionuclide bone scan : A procedure to check if there are rapidly dividing cells, such as cancer cells, in the bone.<EOS>
<BOS>A very small amount of radioactive material is injected into a vein and travels through the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>The radioactive material collects in the bones and is detected by a scanner.<EOS>
<BOS>Pulmonary function test (PFT): A test to see how well the lungs are working.<EOS>
<BOS>It measures how much air the lungs can hold and how quickly air moves into and out of the lungs.<EOS>
<BOS>It also measures how much oxygen is used and how much carbon dioxide is given off during breathing.<EOS>
<BOS>This is also called lung function test.<EOS>
<BOS>Endoscopic ultrasound (EUS): A procedure in which an endoscope is inserted into the body.<EOS>
<BOS>An endoscope is a thin, tubelike instrument with a light and a lens for viewing.<EOS>
<BOS>A probe at the end of the endoscope is used to bounce highenergy sound waves (ultrasound) off internal tissues or organs and make echoes.<EOS>
<BOS>The echoes form a picture of body tissues called a sonogram.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called endosonography.<EOS>
<BOS>EUS may be used to guide fine needle aspiration (FNA) biopsy of the lung, lymph nodes, or other areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Mediastinoscopy : A surgical procedure to look at the organs, tissues, and lymph nodes between the lungs for abnormal areas.<EOS>
<BOS>An incision (cut) is made at the top of the breastbone and a mediastinoscope is inserted into the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>A mediastinoscope is a thin, tubelike instrument with a light and a lens for viewing.<EOS>
<BOS>It may also have a tool to remove tissue or lymph node samples, which are checked under a microscope for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Anterior mediastinotomy : A surgical procedure to look at the organs and tissues between the lungs and between the breastbone and heart for abnormal areas.<EOS>
<BOS>An incision (cut) is made next to the breastbone and a mediastinoscope is inserted into the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>A mediastinoscope is a thin, tubelike instrument with a light and a lens for viewing.<EOS>
<BOS>It may also have a tool to remove tissue or lymph node samples, which are checked under a microscope for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>This is also called the Chamberlain procedure.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph node biopsy : The removal of all or part of a lymph node.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the tissue under a microscope to look for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Bone marrow aspiration and biopsy : The removal of bone marrow, blood, and a small piece of bone by inserting a hollow needle into the hipbone or breastbone.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the bone marrow, blood, and bone under a microscope to look for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>When cancer spreads to another part of the body, it is called metastasis.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells break away from where they began (the primary tumor) and travel through the lymph system or blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the lymph system, travels through the lymph vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the blood, travels through the blood vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The metastatic tumor is the same type of cancer as the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, if nonsmall cell lung cancer spreads to the brain, the cancer cells in the brain are actually lung cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The disease is metastatic lung cancer, not brain cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following stages are used for nonsmall cell lung cancer: Occult (hidden) stage In the occult (hidden) stage, cancer cannot be seen by imaging or bronchoscopy.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells are found in sputum (mucus coughed up from the lungs) or bronchial washing (a sample of cells taken from inside the airways that lead to the lung).<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may have spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage 0 (carcinoma in situ) In stage 0, abnormal cells are found in the lining of the airways.<EOS>
<BOS>These abnormal cells may become cancer and spread into nearby normal tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage 0 is also called carcinoma in situ.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I In stage I, cancer has formed.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage I is divided into stages IA and IB: Stage IA: The tumor is in the lung only and is 3 centimeters or smaller.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IB: Cancer has not spread to the lymph nodes and one or more of the following is true: The tumor is larger than 3 centimeters but not larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the main bronchus and is at least 2 centimeters below where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the innermost layer of the membrane that covers the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Part of the lung has collapsed or developed pneumonitis (inflammation of the lung) in the area where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage II Stage II is divided into stages IIA and IIB.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIA and IIB are each divided into two sections depending on the size of the tumor, where the tumor is found, and whether there is cancer in the lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIA: (1) Cancer has spread to lymph nodes on the same side of the chest as the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The lymph nodes with cancer are within the lung or near the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Also, one or more of the following is true: The tumor is not larger than 5 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the main bronchus and is at least 2 centimeters below where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the innermost layer of the membrane that covers the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Part of the lung has collapsed or developed pneumonitis (inflammation of the lung) in the area where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>or (2) Cancer has not spread to lymph nodes and one or more of the following is true: The tumor is larger than 5 centimeters but not larger than 7 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the main bronchus and is at least 2 centimeters below where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the innermost layer of the membrane that covers the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Part of the lung has collapsed or developed pneumonitis (inflammation of the lung) in the area where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIB: (1) Cancer has spread to nearby lymph nodes on the same side of the chest as the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The lymph nodes with cancer are within the lung or near the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Also, one or more of the following is true: The tumor is larger than 5 centimeters but not larger than 7 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the main bronchus and is at least 2 centimeters below where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the innermost layer of the membrane that covers the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Part of the lung has collapsed or developed pneumonitis (inflammation of the lung) in the area where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>or (2) Cancer has not spread to lymph nodes and one or more of the following is true: The tumor is larger than 7 centimeters.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the main bronchus (and is less than 2 centimeters below where the trachea joins the bronchus), the chest wall, the diaphragm, or the nerve that controls the diaphragm.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to the membrane around the heart or lining the chest wall.<EOS>
<BOS>The whole lung has collapsed or developed pneumonitis (inflammation of the lung).<EOS>
<BOS>There are one or more separate tumors in the same lobe of the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIA Stage IIIA is divided into three sections depending on the size of the tumor, where the tumor is found, and which lymph nodes have cancer (if any).<EOS>
<BOS>(1) Cancer has spread to lymph nodes on the same side of the chest as the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The lymph nodes with cancer are near the sternum (chest bone) or where the bronchus enters the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Also: The tumor may be any size.<EOS>
<BOS>Part of the lung (where the trachea joins the bronchus) or the whole lung may have collapsed or developed pneumonitis (inflammation of the lung).<EOS>
<BOS>There may be one or more separate tumors in the same lobe of the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may have spread to any of the following: Main bronchus, but not the area where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Chest wall.<EOS>
<BOS>Diaphragm and the nerve that controls it.<EOS>
<BOS>Membrane around the lung or lining the chest wall.<EOS>
<BOS>Membrane around the heart.<EOS>
<BOS>or (2) Cancer has spread to lymph nodes on the same side of the chest as the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The lymph nodes with cancer are within the lung or near the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Also: The tumor may be any size.<EOS>
<BOS>The whole lung may have collapsed or developed pneumonitis (inflammation of the lung).<EOS>
<BOS>There may be one or more separate tumors in any of the lobes of the lung with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may have spread to any of the following: Main bronchus, but not the area where the trachea joins the bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Chest wall.<EOS>
<BOS>Diaphragm and the nerve that controls it.<EOS>
<BOS>Membrane around the lung or lining the chest wall.<EOS>
<BOS>Heart or the membrane around it.<EOS>
<BOS>Major blood vessels that lead to or from the heart.<EOS>
<BOS>Trachea.<EOS>
<BOS>Esophagus.<EOS>
<BOS>Nerve that controls the larynx (voice box).<EOS>
<BOS>Sternum (chest bone) or backbone.<EOS>
<BOS>Carina (where the trachea joins the bronchi).<EOS>
<BOS>or (3) Cancer has not spread to the lymph nodes and the tumor may be any size.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to any of the following: Heart.<EOS>
<BOS>Major blood vessels that lead to or from the heart.<EOS>
<BOS>Trachea.<EOS>
<BOS>Esophagus.<EOS>
<BOS>Nerve that controls the larynx (voice box).<EOS>
<BOS>Sternum (chest bone) or backbone.<EOS>
<BOS>Carina (where the trachea joins the bronchi).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IIIB Stage IIIB is divided into two sections depending on the size of the tumor, where the tumor is found, and which lymph nodes have cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>(1) Cancer has spread to lymph nodes above the collarbone or to lymph nodes on the opposite side of the chest as the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>Also: The tumor may be any size.<EOS>
<BOS>Part of the lung (where the trachea joins the bronchus) or the whole lung may have collapsed or developed pneumonitis (inflammation of the lung).<EOS>
<BOS>There may be one or more separate tumors in any of the lobes of the lung with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may have spread to any of the following: Main bronchus.<EOS>
<BOS>Chest wall.<EOS>
<BOS>Diaphragm and the nerve that controls it.<EOS>
<BOS>Membrane around the lung or lining the chest wall.<EOS>
<BOS>Heart or the membrane around it.<EOS>
<BOS>Major blood vessels that lead to or from the heart.<EOS>
<BOS>Trachea.<EOS>
<BOS>Esophagus.<EOS>
<BOS>Nerve that controls the larynx (voice box).<EOS>
<BOS>Sternum (chest bone) or backbone.<EOS>
<BOS>Carina (where the trachea joins the bronchi).<EOS>
<BOS>or (2) Cancer has spread to lymph nodes on the same side of the chest as the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The lymph nodes with cancer are near the sternum (chest bone) or where the bronchus enters the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Also: The tumor may be any size.<EOS>
<BOS>There may be separate tumors in different lobes of the same lung.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to any of the following: Heart.<EOS>
<BOS>Major blood vessels that lead to or from the heart.<EOS>
<BOS>Trachea.<EOS>
<BOS>Esophagus.<EOS>
<BOS>Nerve that controls the larynx (voice box).<EOS>
<BOS>Sternum (chest bone) or backbone.<EOS>
<BOS>Carina (where the trachea joins the bronchi).<EOS>
<BOS>Stage IV In stage IV, the tumor may be any size and cancer may have spread to lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>One or more of the following is true: There are one or more tumors in both lungs.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer is found in fluid around the lungs or the heart.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer has spread to other parts of the body, such as the brain, liver, adrenal glands, kidneys, or bone.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests and procedures that examine the lungs are used to detect (find), diagnose, and stage small cell lung cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The following tests and procedures may be used: Physical exam and history : An exam of the body to check general signs of health, including checking for signs of disease, such as lumps or anything else that seems unusual.<EOS>
<BOS>A history of the patients health habits, including smoking, and past jobs, illnesses, and treatments will also be taken.<EOS>
<BOS>Laboratory tests : Medical procedures that test samples of tissue, blood, urine, or other substances in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>These tests help to diagnose disease, plan and check treatment, or monitor the disease over time.<EOS>
<BOS>Chest xray : An xray of the organs and bones inside the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>An xray is a type of energy beam that can go through the body and onto film, making a picture of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>CT scan (CAT scan) of the brain, chest, and abdomen : A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>Sputum cytology : A microscope is used to check for cancer cells in the sputum (mucus coughed up from the lungs).<EOS>
<BOS>Biopsy : The removal of cells or tissues so they can be viewed under a microscope by a pathologist to check for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>The different ways a biopsy can be done include the following: Fineneedle aspiration (FNA) biopsy of the lung: The removal of tissue or fluid from the lung, using a thin needle.<EOS>
<BOS>A CT scan, ultrasound, or other imaging procedure is used to find the abnormal tissue or fluid in the lung.<EOS>
<BOS>A small incision may be made in the skin where the biopsy needle is inserted into the abnormal tissue or fluid.<EOS>
<BOS>A sample is removed with the needle and sent to the laboratory.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist then views the sample under a microscope to look for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>A chest xray is done after the procedure to make sure no air is leaking from the lung into the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>Bronchoscopy : A procedure to look inside the trachea and large airways in the lung for abnormal areas.<EOS>
<BOS>A bronchoscope is inserted through the nose or mouth into the trachea and lungs.<EOS>
<BOS>A bronchoscope is a thin, tubelike instrument with a light and a lens for viewing.<EOS>
<BOS>It may also have a tool to remove tissue samples, which are checked under a microscope for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Thoracoscopy : A surgical procedure to look at the organs inside the chest to check for abnormal areas.<EOS>
<BOS>An incision (cut) is made between two ribs, and a thoracoscope is inserted into the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>A thoracoscope is a thin, tubelike instrument with a light and a lens for viewing.<EOS>
<BOS>It may also have a tool to remove tissue or lymph node samples, which are checked under a microscope for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>In some cases, this procedure is used to remove part of the esophagus or lung.<EOS>
<BOS>If certain tissues, organs, or lymph nodes cant be reached, a thoracotomy may be done.<EOS>
<BOS>In this procedure, a larger incision is made between the ribs and the chest is opened.<EOS>
<BOS>Thoracentesis : The removal of fluid from the space between the lining of the chest and the lung, using a needle.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the fluid under a microscope to look for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Mediastinoscopy : A surgical procedure to look at the organs, tissues, and lymph nodes between the lungs for abnormal areas.<EOS>
<BOS>An incision (cut) is made at the top of the breastbone and a mediastinoscope is inserted into the chest.<EOS>
<BOS>A mediastinoscope is a thin, tubelike instrument with a light and a lens for viewing.<EOS>
<BOS>It may also have a tool to remove tissue or lymph node samples, which are checked under a microscope for signs of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Light and electron microscopy : A laboratory test in which cells in a sample of tissue are viewed under regular and highpowered microscopes to look for certain changes in the cells.<EOS>
<BOS>Immunohistochemistry : A test that uses antibodies to check for certain antigens in a sample of tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The antibody is usually linked to a radioactive substance or a dye that causes the tissue to light up under a microscope.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of test may be used to tell the difference between different types of cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Having certain inherited disorders can increase the risk of developing parathyroid cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Anything that increases the chance of getting a disease is called a risk factor.<EOS>
<BOS>Risk factors for parathyroid cancer include the following rare disorders that are inherited (passed down from parent to child): Familial isolated hyperparathyroidism (FIHP).<EOS>
<BOS>Multiple endocrine neoplasia type 1 (MEN1) syndrome.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment with radiation therapy may increase the risk of developing a parathyroid adenoma.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points After parathyroid cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>There is no standard staging process for parathyroid cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>After parathyroid cancer has been diagnosed, tests are done to find out if cancer cells have spread to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The process used to find out if cancer has spread to other parts of the body is called staging.<EOS>
<BOS>The following imaging tests may be used to determine if cancer has spread to other parts of the body such as the lungs, liver, bone, heart, pancreas, or lymph nodes: CT scan (CAT scan): A procedure that makes a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body, taken from different angles.<EOS>
<BOS>The pictures are made by a computer linked to an xray machine.<EOS>
<BOS>A dye may be injected into a vein or swallowed to help the organs or tissues show up more clearly.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called computed tomography, computerized tomography, or computerized axial tomography.<EOS>
<BOS>MRI (magnetic resonance imaging): A procedure that uses a magnet, radio waves, and a computer to make a series of detailed pictures of areas inside the body.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is also called nuclear magnetic resonance imaging (NMRI).<EOS>
<BOS>There are three ways that cancer spreads in the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer can spread through tissue, the lymph system, and the blood: Tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by growing into nearby areas.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the lymph vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer spreads from where it began by getting into the blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer travels through the blood vessels to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer may spread from where it began to other parts of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>When cancer spreads to another part of the body, it is called metastasis.<EOS>
<BOS>Cancer cells break away from where they began (the primary tumor) and travel through the lymph system or blood.<EOS>
<BOS>Lymph system.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the lymph system, travels through the lymph vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>Blood.<EOS>
<BOS>The cancer gets into the blood, travels through the blood vessels, and forms a tumor (metastatic tumor) in another part of the body.<EOS>
<BOS>The metastatic tumor is the same type of cancer as the primary tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>For example, if parathyroid cancer spreads to the lung, the cancer cells in the lung are actually parathyroid cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The disease is metastatic parathyroid cancer, not lung cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>There is no standard staging process for parathyroid cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Parathyroid cancer is described as either localized or metastatic: Localized parathyroid cancer is found in a parathyroid gland and may have spread to nearby tissues.<EOS>
<BOS>Metastatic parathyroid cancer has spread to other parts of the body, such as the lungs, liver, bone, sac around the heart, pancreas, or lymph nodes.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points Prostate cancer is a disease in which malignant (cancer) cells form in the tissues of the prostate.<EOS>
<BOS>Signs of prostate cancer include a weak flow of urine or frequent urination.<EOS>
<BOS>Tests that examine the prostate and blood are used to detect (find) and diagnose prostate cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Certain factors affect prognosis (chance of recovery) and treatment options.<EOS>
<BOS>Prostate cancer is a disease in which malignant (cancer) cells form in the tissues of the prostate.<EOS>
<BOS>The prostate is a gland in the male reproductive system.<EOS>
<BOS>It lies just below the bladder (the organ that collects and empties urine) and in front of the rectum (the lower part of the intestine).<EOS>
<BOS>It is about the size of a walnut and surrounds part of the urethra (the tube that empties urine from the bladder).<EOS>
<BOS>The prostate gland makes fluid that is part of the semen.<EOS>
<BOS>Prostate cancer is most common in older men.<EOS>
<BOS>In the U.<EOS>
<BOS>S.<EOS>
<BOS>, about 1 out of 5 men will be diagnosed with prostate cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Key Points There are different types of treatment for patients with prostate cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Seven types of standard treatment are used: Watchful waiting or active surveillance Surgery Radiation therapy and radiopharmaceutical therapy Hormone therapy Chemotherapy Biologic therapy Bisphosphonate therapy There are treatments for bone pain caused by bone metastases or hormone therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>New types of treatment are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>Cryosurgery Highintensityfocused ultrasound therapy Proton beam radiation therapy Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients can enter clinical trials before, during, or after starting their cancer treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Followup tests may be needed.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of treatment for patients with prostate cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Different types of treatment are available for patients with prostate cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Some treatments are standard (the currently used treatment), and some are being tested in clinical trials.<EOS>
<BOS>A treatment clinical trial is a research study meant to help improve current treatments or obtain information on new treatments for patients with cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>When clinical trials show that a new treatment is better than the standard treatment, the new treatment may become the standard treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Patients may want to think about taking part in a clinical trial.<EOS>
<BOS>Some clinical trials are open only to patients who have not started treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Seven types of standard treatment are used: Watchful waiting or active surveillance Watchful waiting and active surveillance are treatments used for older men who do not have signs or symptoms or have other medical conditions and for men whose prostate cancer is found during a screening test.<EOS>
<BOS>Watchful waiting is closely monitoring a patients condition without giving any treatment until signs or symptoms appear or change.<EOS>
<BOS>Treatment is given to relieve symptoms and improve quality of life.<EOS>
<BOS>Active surveillance is closely following a patient's condition without giving any treatment unless there are changes in test results.<EOS>
<BOS>It is used to find early signs that the condition is getting worse.<EOS>
<BOS>In active surveillance, patients are given certain exams and tests, including digital rectal exam, PSA test, transrectal ultrasound, and transrectal needle biopsy, to check if the cancer is growing.<EOS>
<BOS>When the cancer begins to grow, treatment is given to cure the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Other terms that are used to describe not giving treatment to cure prostate cancer right after diagnosis are observation, watch and wait, and expectant management.<EOS>
<BOS>Surgery Patients in good health whose tumor is in the prostate gland only may be treated with surgery to remove the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>The following types of surgery are used: Radical prostatectomy: A surgical procedure to remove the prostate, surrounding tissue, and seminal vesicles.<EOS>
<BOS>There are two types of radical prostatectomy: Retropubic prostatectomy: A surgical procedure to remove the prostate through an incision (cut) in the abdominal wall.<EOS>
<BOS>Removal of nearby lymph nodes may be done at the same time.<EOS>
<BOS>Perineal prostatectomy: A surgical procedure to remove the prostate through an incision (cut) made in the perineum (area between the scrotum and anus).<EOS>
<BOS>Nearby lymph nodes may also be removed through a separate incision in the abdomen.<EOS>
<BOS>Pelvic lymphadenectomy: A surgical procedure to remove the lymph nodes in the pelvis.<EOS>
<BOS>A pathologist views the tissue under a microscope to look for cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>If the lymph nodes contain cancer, the doctor will not remove the prostate and may recommend other treatment.<EOS>
<BOS>Transurethral resection of the prostate (TURP): A surgical procedure to remove tissue from the prostate using a resectoscope (a thin, lighted tube with a cutting tool) inserted through the urethra.<EOS>
<BOS>This procedure is done to treat benign prostatic hypertrophy and it is sometimes done to relieve symptoms caused by a tumor before other cancer treatment is given.<EOS>
<BOS>TURP may also be done in men whose tumor is in the prostate only and who cannot have a radical prostatectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>In some cases, nervesparing surgery can be done.<EOS>
<BOS>This type of surgery may save the nerves that control erection.<EOS>
<BOS>However, men with large tumors or tumors that are very close to the nerves may not be able to have this surgery.<EOS>
<BOS>Possible problems after prostate cancer surgery include the following: Impotence.<EOS>
<BOS>Leakage of urine from the bladder or stool from the rectum.<EOS>
<BOS>Shortening of the penis (1 to 2 centimeters).<EOS>
<BOS>The exact reason for this is not known.<EOS>
<BOS>Inguinal hernia (bulging of fat or part of the small intestine through weak muscles into the groin).<EOS>
<BOS>Inguinal hernia may occur more often in men treated with radical prostatectomy than in men who have some other types of prostate surgery, radiation therapy, or prostate biopsy alone.<EOS>
<BOS>It is most likely to occur within the first 2 years after radical prostatectomy.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy and radiopharmaceutical therapy Radiation therapy is a cancer treatment that uses highenergy xrays or other types of radiation to kill cancer cells or keep them from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>There are different types of radiation therapy: External radiation therapy uses a machine outside the body to send radiation toward the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Conformal radiation is a type of external radiation therapy that uses a computer to make a 3dimensional (3D) picture of the tumor and shapes the radiation beams to fit the tumor.<EOS>
<BOS>This allows a high dose of radiation to reach the tumor and causes less damage to nearby healthy tissue.<EOS>
<BOS>Hypofractionated radiation therapy may be given because it has a more convenient treatment schedule.<EOS>
<BOS>Hypofractionated radiation therapy is radiation treatment in which a larger than usual total dose of radiation is given once a day over a shorter period of time (fewer days) compared to standard radiation therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Hypofractionated radiation therapy may have worse side effects than standard radiation therapy, depending on the schedules used.<EOS>
<BOS>Internal radiation therapy uses a radioactive substance sealed in needles, seeds, wires, or catheters that are placed directly into or near the cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>In earlystage prostate cancer, the radioactive seeds are placed in the prostate using needles that are inserted through the skin between the scrotum and rectum.<EOS>
<BOS>The placement of the radioactive seeds in the prostate is guided by images from transrectal ultrasound or computed tomography (CT).<EOS>
<BOS>The needles are removed after the radioactive seeds are placed in the prostate.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiopharmaceutical therapy uses a radioactive substance to treat cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiopharmaceutical therapy includes the following: Alpha emitter radiation therapy uses a radioactive substance to treat prostate cancer that has spread to the bone.<EOS>
<BOS>A radioactive substance called radium223 is injected into a vein and travels through the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>The radium223 collects in areas of bone with cancer and kills the cancer cells.<EOS>
<BOS>The way the radiation therapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>External radiation therapy, internal radiation therapy, and radiopharmaceutical therapy are used to treat prostate cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Men treated with radiation therapy for prostate cancer have an increased risk of having bladder and/or gastrointestinal cancer.<EOS>
<BOS>Radiation therapy can cause impotence and urinary problems.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy Hormone therapy is a cancer treatment that removes hormones or blocks their action and stops cancer cells from growing.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormones are substances made by glands in the body and circulated in the bloodstream.<EOS>
<BOS>In prostate cancer, male sex hormones can cause prostate cancer to grow.<EOS>
<BOS>Drugs, surgery, or other hormones are used to reduce the amount of male hormones or block them from working.<EOS>
<BOS>Hormone therapy for prostate cancer may include the following: Luteinizing hormonereleasing hormone agonists can stop the testicles from making testosterone.<EOS>
<BOS>Examples are leuprolide, goserelin, and buserelin.<EOS>
<BOS>Antiandrogens can block the action of androgens (hormones that promote male sex characteristics), such as testosterone.<EOS>
<BOS>Examples are flutamide, bicalutamide, enzalutamide, and nilutamide.<EOS>
<BOS>Drugs that can prevent the adrenal glands from making androgens include ketoconazole and aminoglutethimide.<EOS>
<BOS>Orchiectomy is a surgical procedure to remove one or both testicles, the main source of male hormones, such as testosterone, to decrease the amount of hormone being made.<EOS>
<BOS>Estrogens (hormones that promote female sex characteristics) can prevent the testicles from making testosterone.<EOS>
<BOS>However, estrogens are seldom used today in the treatment of prostate cancer because of the risk of serious side effects.<EOS>
<BOS>Hot flashes, impaired sexual function, loss of desire for sex, and weakened bones may occur in men treated with hormone therapy.<EOS>
<BOS>Other side effects include diarrhea, nausea, and itching.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Prostate Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Chemotherapy Chemotherapy is a cancer treatment that uses drugs to stop the growth of cancer cells, either by killing the cells or by stopping them from dividing.<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is taken by mouth or injected into a vein or muscle, the drugs enter the bloodstream and can reach cancer cells throughout the body (systemic chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>When chemotherapy is placed directly into the cerebrospinal fluid, an organ, or a body cavity such as the abdomen, the drugs mainly affect cancer cells in those areas (regional chemotherapy).<EOS>
<BOS>The way the chemotherapy is given depends on the type and stage of the cancer being treated.<EOS>
<BOS>See Drugs Approved for Prostate Cancer for more information.<EOS>
<BOS>Biologic therapy Biologic therapy is a treatment that uses the patients immune system to fight cancer.<EOS>
